وخالف في هذا الأخير المعتزلة، فزعموا أن إرادة الله لا تتعلق بالشرور ولا القبائح ، ويُروى أنَّ القاضي عبد الجبار الهمداني دخل على الصاحب بن عباد ، فوجد عنده الأستاذ أبا إسحاق الإسفراييني ، والقاضي عبد الجبار من رؤوس المعتزلة ، والأستاذ أبو إسحاق من أكابر أهل السُّنة ، فقال القاضي عبد الجبار: سبحان من تنزه عن الفحشاء، يريد التنديد بأهل السنة الذين قالوا: إن الشَّرَ واقع من الناس بإرادة الله تعالى، فقال الأستاذ أبو إسحاق: سبحان من لا يجري في ملكه إلا ما يشاء، فقال القاضي عبد الجبار : أَفَيُريدُ ربُّنا أن يُعْصَى ؟! فقال الأستاذ أبو إسحاق أَفَيُعْصَى رَبُّنَا كُرها ؟! فقال القاضي عبد الجبار : أرأيتَ إن منعني الهُدى وقضى علي بالرِّدَى ، أَحسن إلي أم أساءَ ؟ فقال الأستاذ أبو إسحاق: إن منعكَ ما هو لك فقد أساء، وإن منعك ما هو له فهو يخص برحمته من يشاء، وانقطع الحديث بعد ذلك. والذي ذهب إليه المعتزلة من تخصيص الممكن الذي تتعلق الإرادة به بالخير ؛ مبني على ما ذهبوا إليه من أنَّ الإرادة توافقُ الرِّضَا في المعنى، والحقُّ ما ذهب إليه أهل السنة الرضا غير الإرادة وسيأتي إيضاح ذلك في الجهة الرابعة من الكلام على هذه الصفة أنَّ أهل السنة يرون أنَّ إرادة الله تتعلق بكل ممكن خيرًا كان ذلك الممكن أو شرا؛ فإنهم يختلفون في جواز نسبة الشرور والقبائح إليه تعالى ، والذي رجّحه المحققون: جواز ذلك في مقام التّعليم دون غيره، وهذا الخلاف جارٍ أيضًا في نسبة الأمور الخسيسة إليه تعالى والأصح أيضًا جواز ذلك في مقام التعليم دون غيره، فلا يجوز أن تقول: الله خالق القردة والخنازير ، إلا أن يكون ذلك في مقام التعليم، وهذا ضرب من الأدب ليس غير محي. قسم الإلهيات وقال المعتزلة : إن إرادة الله تعالى الفعل من المكلف عين أمره النفسي بهذا الفعل فعندهم أنَّ الله تعالى لا يريد إلا ما أمر به من الإيمان والطَّاعة سواء أوقع ذلك أم لا ، فإيمانُ أبي جهل مثلا - عند أهل السنة - مأمور به غير مراد له ، لأنه علم عدم وقوعه، وكفر أبي جهل منهي عنه وهو واقع بإرادة الله وقدرته ، وعند المعتزلة: إيمان أبي جهل هو المراد الله تعالى لا كفره ، لأن إيمانه هو المأمور به ، وقد تمسَّكَ أهل هذا الرَّأي بشُبَه هي أَوْهَى مِن بيت العنكبوت ، وحاصلها أنَّ إرادة القبيح ـ وهو المنهي عنه ككفر أبي جهل ـ قبيح، والعقاب على ما أُريدَ ظلم، والنهي عمَّا يراد والأمر بما لا يُراد سفة، والله تعالى منزه عن القبائح، وعن الظلم، وعن السفه. ورد قولهم بأن إرادة القبيح قبيح »: بأنَّا لا نُسلّم قُبْحَ ذلك ، بل هو حَسنُ ، غاية الأمر أنه قد يخفى علينا وجه حُسْنِه . ورُدَّ قولهم بأن العقاب على ما أريد ظلم»: بأنا لا نُسلّم كونه ظلما ؛ لأنَّ الظلم هو التصرف في ملك الغير بغير إذنه، والله تعالى إنما يتصرف في ملكه ورد قولهم بأن النهي عما يراد والأمر بما لا يرادُ سَفَهُ : بأنا لا نُسلّم ذلك ؛ لأن محل كون ذلك سفها أن لو انحصر المقصود في رغبة الامتثال ، ولكن قد يكون الغرض من الأمر والنهي الابتلاء أو إظهار فساد استعداد المأمور أو المنهي ، ثم إنه يلزم على مقالة المعتزلة هذه: أنّ الله تعالى قد يريد الشيء ولا يقع ، وأن الشيء قد يقع وهو سبحانه لا يريده. وقال ابن قاسم: «وصدور هذه المقالة من عاقل مستبعد ؛ إذ كيف يظنُّ إنسان تخلَّفَ مُرادِ الله تعالى ووقوع مُراد الشيطان ؟ حتى قال بعضهم : لا شك في كفر من يعتقد ذلك». وحكي عن عمرو بن عبيد أنه قال: «ما ألزمني أحد مثل ما ألزمني مجوسي كان معي في سفينة، قلتُ له: لم لا تسلم ؟ فقال : إن الله تعالى لم يرد إسلامي، فإذا أراد إسلامي أسلمتُ ، فقلتُ=
And the Mu'tazila disagreed in this last point, claiming that the will of Allah does not pertain to evils and ugly things. It is narrated that al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar al-Hamadhani visited al-Sahib ibn 'Abbad and found with him al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini. Al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar was a chief of the Mu'tazila, and al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq was a great figure of Ahl al-Sunna. Al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar said: 'Glory be to Him who is transcendent above foul deeds,' intending to censure Ahl al-Sunna who said that evil occurs from people by the will of Allah. So al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq said: 'Glory be to Him in whose dominion nothing occurs except what He wills.' Al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar said: 'Then does our Lord will to be disobeyed?' Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq said: 'Then is our Lord disobeyed against His will?' Al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar said: 'Do you think, if He prevents me from guidance and decrees upon me perdition, has He done good to me or evil?' Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq said: 'If He prevented you from what is yours, He has done evil; but if He prevented you from what is His, then He singles out for His mercy whom He wills.' And the discussion ended after that. And that to which the Mu'tazila went, of restricting the possible to which will pertains to good, is based on their view that will agrees with good pleasure (al-rida) in meaning. The truth is what Ahl al-Sunna went to: that good pleasure is other than will, and clarification of that will come in the fourth aspect of discourse on this attribute: that Ahl al-Sunna hold that the will of Allah pertains to every possible, whether that possible is good or evil. Yet they differ on the permissibility of attributing evils and ugly things to Him. What the investigators favored is the permissibility of that in the context of teaching (al-ta'lim) but not otherwise. This disagreement also applies to attributing base things to Him; the correct view again is permissibility in the context of teaching only. So you should not say: 'Allah is the creator of monkeys and pigs' except when teaching. This is a kind of reverence (adab) and nothing more. Section on Ilahiyyat. And the Mu'tazila said: 'The will of Allah Most High for the action of the responsible person is precisely His inner command (amruhu al-nafsi) for that action.' So according to them, Allah wills only what He commands of faith and obedience, whether that occurs or not. Thus, the faith of Abu Jahl, for example, according to Ahl al-Sunna, is commanded but not willed by Him, because He knew it would not occur; and the disbelief of Abu Jahl is forbidden and occurs by the will and power of Allah. According to the Mu'tazila, the faith of Abu Jahl is what Allah wills, not his disbelief, because his faith is what is commanded. The proponents of this view have clung to arguments flimsier than a spider's web. Their summary is: willing the ugly—which is the forbidden, like the disbelief of Abu Jahl—is ugly; punishment for what is willed is injustice; forbidding what is willed and commanding what is not willed is foolishness; and Allah is transcendent above ugly things, injustice, and foolishness. Their statement that 'willing the ugly is ugly' was rebutted: by saying we do not concede the ugliness of that; rather, it is good, the only issue being that its aspect of goodness may be hidden from us. Their statement that 'punishment for what is willed is injustice' was rebutted: by saying we do not concede it as injustice, because injustice is disposing of another's property without permission, and Allah only disposes of His own property. Their statement that 'forbidding what is willed and commanding what is not willed is foolishness' was rebutted: by saying we do not concede that; because the place for that being foolishness would be if the purpose were limited to desire for compliance, but the purpose of command and prohibition may be testing (al-ibtila') or showing the corruption of the preparedness of the commanded or forbidden one. Then it follows from this doctrine of the Mu'tazila that Allah may will a thing and it does not occur, and that a thing may occur while He does not will it. Ibn Qasim said: 'The emergence of this statement from an intelligent person is unlikely; how can a person imagine the failure of Allah's will and the occurrence of the devil's will?' Until some said: 'There is no doubt in the disbelief of one who believes that.' And it is narrated from 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd that he said: 'No one has ever compelled me like a Magian who was with me on a ship. I said to him: Why do you not embrace Islam? He said: Allah has not willed my Islam; if He wills my Islam, I will become Muslim. I said to him: ...'
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite position that God's will does not extend to evil or ugly actions. The author begins by recounting a famous debate between the Mu'tazilite leader al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar and the Sunni scholar Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini. The Mu'tazilite opens with a declaration of God's transcendence from foul acts, intending to criticize the Sunni view that evil occurs by God's will. Abu Ishaq responds by affirming God's absolute sovereignty: nothing occurs in His dominion except what He wills. The cross-examination continues, exploring whether God wills to be disobeyed, and God's justice in granting or withholding guidance. Abu Ishaq's final answer distinguishes between what is 'yours' (i.e., what God owes you) and what is 'His' (i.e., His gifts). The debate ends. The author then explains the basis of the Mu'tazilite error: they equate will (irada) with good pleasure (rida), whereas the Sunni position distinguishes them. The author notes that while Sunnis agree God's will encompasses all possibilities (good and evil), they differ on whether it is permissible to attribute evil or base things to God directly. The preferred view is that such attributions are only allowed in a teaching context, as a matter of reverent adab. The heading 'Section on Ilahiyyat' appears. The author then presents the Mu'tazilite argument in detail: they define God's will for an action as identical to His inner command, so He only wills what He commands (faith and obedience). Thus, they claim that God wills the faith of Abu Jahl (since it is commanded) not his disbelief (which is forbidden). They base this on three arguments: (1) willing the ugly is itself ugly, (2) punishing for what is willed is injustice, and (3) forbidding what is willed and commanding what is not willed is foolishness. Each of these is rebutted. The first rebuttal: we do not concede that willing the ugly is ugly; it can be good, even if the aspect of goodness is hidden. The second rebuttal: punishing for what is willed is not injustice because injustice is only in disposing of another's property; God acts within His own dominion. The third rebuttal: forbidding what is willed and commanding what is not willed is not foolishness, because the purpose of command and prohibition may be testing (ibtila') or revealing the corruption of the person's readiness, not merely to obtain compliance. The author then points out a devastating consequence of the Mu'tazilite position: it implies that God may will something that does not occur, and that something may occur that He does not will—both of which are impossible. Ibn Qasim is quoted to emphasize the absurdity: it would mean the devil's will prevails over God's. The passage ends with a story about 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd and a Magian, cutting off mid-sentence.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
أهل السنة
الإرادة
الفحشاء
الملك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God wills evil, then He is unjust or the author of sin.
Correction: The debate shows that God's will over all events does not make Him unjust, as He acts within His own dominion and may have hidden purposes.

Checkpoint
What was the central point of Abu Ishaq's final argument?
A) God does not will evil at all
B) God only wills good for believers
C) God's actions in His own dominion are not subject to human standards of justice
D) Evil is not real

Key Takeaway
God's will is absolute and encompasses all occurrences, and this does not imply injustice because God acts within His own sovereignty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you ever blame God for willing it? How does Abu Ishaq's response challenge that excuse? Will you repent genuinely, recognizing God's will and your responsibility?
?
Do you believe that God's withholding of guidance from some is unjust? Reflect on Abu Ishaq's distinction about what belongs to God. Do you accept that God owes you nothing? Are you living with that humility?
?
How does this debate affect your view of tragedy and suffering? Can you truly say 'nothing occurs except by God's will' without resentment? What needs to change in your heart?

Divine Attributes

The Mu'tazilite Error: Will and Command
Explanation of the Mu'tazilite equation of will and command, the Sunni response, and the example of Abu Jahl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite view that God's will is identical to His command, and the Sunni response that distinguishes will from good pleasure and command.

Core Idea
Will is not the same as command; God may command something He does not will.

The Mu'tazilites built their view on the equation of will and good pleasure. This lesson unpacks that error and shows how Sunnis affirm that God's will encompasses all possibilities while reserving the language of attribution to contexts of teaching.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite basis
Read the passage explaining that the Mu'tazilites equate will (irada) with good pleasure (rida). Note the implication: God wills only what He commands.
2
Learn the Sunni distinction
The Sunni position separates will from good pleasure; will is broader and includes evil. Also note the adab rule: attributing evil to God is allowed only in teaching contexts.
3
Study the example of Abu Jahl
The example illustrates how Sunnis say Abu Jahl's faith is commanded but not willed, while his disbelief is willed but forbidden. Contrast with the Mu'tazilite view.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this distinction resolves apparent contradictions between God's commands and His will.

وخالف في هذا الأخير المعتزلة، فزعموا أن إرادة الله لا تتعلق بالشرور ولا القبائح ، ويُروى أنَّ القاضي عبد الجبار الهمداني دخل على الصاحب بن عباد ، فوجد عنده الأستاذ أبا إسحاق الإسفراييني ، والقاضي عبد الجبار من رؤوس المعتزلة ، والأستاذ أبو إسحاق من أكابر أهل السُّنة ، فقال القاضي عبد الجبار: سبحان من تنزه عن الفحشاء، يريد التنديد بأهل السنة الذين قالوا: إن الشَّرَ واقع من الناس بإرادة الله تعالى، فقال الأستاذ أبو إسحاق: سبحان من لا يجري في ملكه إلا ما يشاء، فقال القاضي عبد الجبار : أَفَيُريدُ ربُّنا أن يُعْصَى ؟! فقال الأستاذ أبو إسحاق أَفَيُعْصَى رَبُّنَا كُرها ؟! فقال القاضي عبد الجبار : أرأيتَ إن منعني الهُدى وقضى علي بالرِّدَى ، أَحسن إلي أم أساءَ ؟ فقال الأستاذ أبو إسحاق: إن منعكَ ما هو لك فقد أساء، وإن منعك ما هو له فهو يخص برحمته من يشاء، وانقطع الحديث بعد ذلك. والذي ذهب إليه المعتزلة من تخصيص الممكن الذي تتعلق الإرادة به بالخير ؛ مبني على ما ذهبوا إليه من أنَّ الإرادة توافقُ الرِّضَا في المعنى، والحقُّ ما ذهب إليه أهل السنة الرضا غير الإرادة وسيأتي إيضاح ذلك في الجهة الرابعة من الكلام على هذه الصفة أنَّ أهل السنة يرون أنَّ إرادة الله تتعلق بكل ممكن خيرًا كان ذلك الممكن أو شرا؛ فإنهم يختلفون في جواز نسبة الشرور والقبائح إليه تعالى ، والذي رجّحه المحققون: جواز ذلك في مقام التّعليم دون غيره، وهذا الخلاف جارٍ أيضًا في نسبة الأمور الخسيسة إليه تعالى والأصح أيضًا جواز ذلك في مقام التعليم دون غيره، فلا يجوز أن تقول: الله خالق القردة والخنازير ، إلا أن يكون ذلك في مقام التعليم، وهذا ضرب من الأدب ليس غير محي. قسم الإلهيات وقال المعتزلة : إن إرادة الله تعالى الفعل من المكلف عين أمره النفسي بهذا الفعل فعندهم أنَّ الله تعالى لا يريد إلا ما أمر به من الإيمان والطَّاعة سواء أوقع ذلك أم لا ، فإيمانُ أبي جهل مثلا - عند أهل السنة - مأمور به غير مراد له ، لأنه علم عدم وقوعه، وكفر أبي جهل منهي عنه وهو واقع بإرادة الله وقدرته ، وعند المعتزلة: إيمان أبي جهل هو المراد الله تعالى لا كفره ، لأن إيمانه هو المأمور به ، وقد تمسَّكَ أهل هذا الرَّأي بشُبَه هي أَوْهَى مِن بيت العنكبوت ، وحاصلها أنَّ إرادة القبيح ـ وهو المنهي عنه ككفر أبي جهل ـ قبيح، والعقاب على ما أُريدَ ظلم، والنهي عمَّا يراد والأمر بما لا يُراد سفة، والله تعالى منزه عن القبائح، وعن الظلم، وعن السفه. ورد قولهم بأن إرادة القبيح قبيح »: بأنَّا لا نُسلّم قُبْحَ ذلك ، بل هو حَسنُ ، غاية الأمر أنه قد يخفى علينا وجه حُسْنِه . ورُدَّ قولهم بأن العقاب على ما أريد ظلم»: بأنا لا نُسلّم كونه ظلما ؛ لأنَّ الظلم هو التصرف في ملك الغير بغير إذنه، والله تعالى إنما يتصرف في ملكه ورد قولهم بأن النهي عما يراد والأمر بما لا يرادُ سَفَهُ : بأنا لا نُسلّم ذلك ؛ لأن محل كون ذلك سفها أن لو انحصر المقصود في رغبة الامتثال ، ولكن قد يكون الغرض من الأمر والنهي الابتلاء أو إظهار فساد استعداد المأمور أو المنهي ، ثم إنه يلزم على مقالة المعتزلة هذه: أنّ الله تعالى قد يريد الشيء ولا يقع ، وأن الشيء قد يقع وهو سبحانه لا يريده. وقال ابن قاسم: «وصدور هذه المقالة من عاقل مستبعد ؛ إذ كيف يظنُّ إنسان تخلَّفَ مُرادِ الله تعالى ووقوع مُراد الشيطان ؟ حتى قال بعضهم : لا شك في كفر من يعتقد ذلك». وحكي عن عمرو بن عبيد أنه قال: «ما ألزمني أحد مثل ما ألزمني مجوسي كان معي في سفينة، قلتُ له: لم لا تسلم ؟ فقال : إن الله تعالى لم يرد إسلامي، فإذا أراد إسلامي أسلمتُ ، فقلتُ=
And the Mu'tazila disagreed in this last point, claiming that the will of Allah does not pertain to evils and ugly things. It is narrated that al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar al-Hamadhani visited al-Sahib ibn 'Abbad and found with him al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini. Al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar was a chief of the Mu'tazila, and al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq was a great figure of Ahl al-Sunna. Al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar said: 'Glory be to Him who is transcendent above foul deeds,' intending to censure Ahl al-Sunna who said that evil occurs from people by the will of Allah. So al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq said: 'Glory be to Him in whose dominion nothing occurs except what He wills.' Al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar said: 'Then does our Lord will to be disobeyed?' Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq said: 'Then is our Lord disobeyed against His will?' Al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar said: 'Do you think, if He prevents me from guidance and decrees upon me perdition, has He done good to me or evil?' Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq said: 'If He prevented you from what is yours, He has done evil; but if He prevented you from what is His, then He singles out for His mercy whom He wills.' And the discussion ended after that. And that to which the Mu'tazila went, of restricting the possible to which will pertains to good, is based on their view that will agrees with good pleasure (al-rida) in meaning. The truth is what Ahl al-Sunna went to: that good pleasure is other than will, and clarification of that will come in the fourth aspect of discourse on this attribute: that Ahl al-Sunna hold that the will of Allah pertains to every possible, whether that possible is good or evil. Yet they differ on the permissibility of attributing evils and ugly things to Him. What the investigators favored is the permissibility of that in the context of teaching (al-ta'lim) but not otherwise. This disagreement also applies to attributing base things to Him; the correct view again is permissibility in the context of teaching only. So you should not say: 'Allah is the creator of monkeys and pigs' except when teaching. This is a kind of reverence (adab) and nothing more. Section on Ilahiyyat. And the Mu'tazila said: 'The will of Allah Most High for the action of the responsible person is precisely His inner command (amruhu al-nafsi) for that action.' So according to them, Allah wills only what He commands of faith and obedience, whether that occurs or not. Thus, the faith of Abu Jahl, for example, according to Ahl al-Sunna, is commanded but not willed by Him, because He knew it would not occur; and the disbelief of Abu Jahl is forbidden and occurs by the will and power of Allah. According to the Mu'tazila, the faith of Abu Jahl is what Allah wills, not his disbelief, because his faith is what is commanded. The proponents of this view have clung to arguments flimsier than a spider's web. Their summary is: willing the ugly—which is the forbidden, like the disbelief of Abu Jahl—is ugly; punishment for what is willed is injustice; forbidding what is willed and commanding what is not willed is foolishness; and Allah is transcendent above ugly things, injustice, and foolishness. Their statement that 'willing the ugly is ugly' was rebutted: by saying we do not concede the ugliness of that; rather, it is good, the only issue being that its aspect of goodness may be hidden from us. Their statement that 'punishment for what is willed is injustice' was rebutted: by saying we do not concede it as injustice, because injustice is disposing of another's property without permission, and Allah only disposes of His own property. Their statement that 'forbidding what is willed and commanding what is not willed is foolishness' was rebutted: by saying we do not concede that; because the place for that being foolishness would be if the purpose were limited to desire for compliance, but the purpose of command and prohibition may be testing (al-ibtila') or showing the corruption of the preparedness of the commanded or forbidden one. Then it follows from this doctrine of the Mu'tazila that Allah may will a thing and it does not occur, and that a thing may occur while He does not will it. Ibn Qasim said: 'The emergence of this statement from an intelligent person is unlikely; how can a person imagine the failure of Allah's will and the occurrence of the devil's will?' Until some said: 'There is no doubt in the disbelief of one who believes that.' And it is narrated from 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd that he said: 'No one has ever compelled me like a Magian who was with me on a ship. I said to him: Why do you not embrace Islam? He said: Allah has not willed my Islam; if He wills my Islam, I will become Muslim. I said to him: ...'
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite position that God's will does not extend to evil or ugly actions. The author begins by recounting a famous debate between the Mu'tazilite leader al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar and the Sunni scholar Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini. The Mu'tazilite opens with a declaration of God's transcendence from foul acts, intending to criticize the Sunni view that evil occurs by God's will. Abu Ishaq responds by affirming God's absolute sovereignty: nothing occurs in His dominion except what He wills. The cross-examination continues, exploring whether God wills to be disobeyed, and God's justice in granting or withholding guidance. Abu Ishaq's final answer distinguishes between what is 'yours' (i.e., what God owes you) and what is 'His' (i.e., His gifts). The debate ends. The author then explains the basis of the Mu'tazilite error: they equate will (irada) with good pleasure (rida), whereas the Sunni position distinguishes them. The author notes that while Sunnis agree God's will encompasses all possibilities (good and evil), they differ on whether it is permissible to attribute evil or base things to God directly. The preferred view is that such attributions are only allowed in a teaching context, as a matter of reverent adab. The heading 'Section on Ilahiyyat' appears. The author then presents the Mu'tazilite argument in detail: they define God's will for an action as identical to His inner command, so He only wills what He commands (faith and obedience). Thus, they claim that God wills the faith of Abu Jahl (since it is commanded) not his disbelief (which is forbidden). They base this on three arguments: (1) willing the ugly is itself ugly, (2) punishing for what is willed is injustice, and (3) forbidding what is willed and commanding what is not willed is foolishness. Each of these is rebutted. The first rebuttal: we do not concede that willing the ugly is ugly; it can be good, even if the aspect of goodness is hidden. The second rebuttal: punishing for what is willed is not injustice because injustice is only in disposing of another's property; God acts within His own dominion. The third rebuttal: forbidding what is willed and commanding what is not willed is not foolishness, because the purpose of command and prohibition may be testing (ibtila') or revealing the corruption of the person's readiness, not merely to obtain compliance. The author then points out a devastating consequence of the Mu'tazilite position: it implies that God may will something that does not occur, and that something may occur that He does not will—both of which are impossible. Ibn Qasim is quoted to emphasize the absurdity: it would mean the devil's will prevails over God's. The passage ends with a story about 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd and a Magian, cutting off mid-sentence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilite view, why do they say God wills the faith of Abu Jahl?
A) Because Abu Jahl was a believer
B) Because faith is commanded, and will equals command
C) Because God loves faith
D) Because faith is possible

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite error lies in equating will with command; the Sunni view separates them and allows God to will both good and evil without approving evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage teaches that God wills things He does not command (like Abu Jahl's disbelief) and commands things He does not will (like his faith). When you reflect on your own life, are there sins you commit that you know God wills to exist (by His overarching decree) but prohibits? Does knowing that God's will encompasses your disobedience disturb your conscience, or move you to rely more on His mercy? What one concrete step will you take today to align your actions with His command, even when you feel your will is weak?
?
The Mu'tazilites equated will with approval, leading them to deny that God wills evil. The Sunnis affirm that God wills all that occurs, including evil, without approving it. How does this truth affect your perspective on the trials and evils you witness in the world? Are you tempted to question God's goodness when you see suffering? In light of this lesson, how can you strengthen your trust that God's will, even for difficult events, is not a sign of His displeasure?
?
The passage mentions 'adab' (reverence) in how we attribute things to Allah—only mentioning evil attributions in teaching contexts. In your everyday speech, do you casually say things like 'God destined this tragedy' without the proper reverence? Write down one specific phrase you will avoid and one you will use instead, to maintain adab in your speech about Allah's will.

Divine Attributes
intermediate
8 min
Refuting Mu'tazilite Arguments on Evil
Refutation of the three Mu'tazilite arguments and the logical consequences of their view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three Mu'tazilite arguments against God willing evil and their Sunni rebuttals, as well as the logical consequences of the Mu'tazilite position.

Core Idea
Three reasoned rebuttals defend the Sunni view that God's will includes evil without injustice or folly.

The Mu'tazilites presented three ethical arguments: that willing evil is ugly, punishing the willed is unjust, and commanding contrary to will is foolish. This lesson shows how each is refuted by Sunni scholars, and why the Mu'tazilite view leads to impossible conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three arguments
Read the passage and find the three Mu'tazilite arguments: 1) willing evil is ugly, 2) punishment for willed evil is injustice, 3) forbidding what is willed is foolish.
2
Study rebuttal to the first argument
The Sunni response: we do not concede that willing evil is ugly; it may be good, even if the aspect of goodness is hidden.
3
Study rebuttal to the second argument
The Sunni response: punishment for what is willed is not injustice because God is acting within His own dominion, and injustice only applies to others' property.
4
Study rebuttal to the third argument
The Sunni response: forbidding what is willed is not foolish if the purpose is testing (al-ibtila') or revealing corruption, not merely compliance.
5
Consider the consequence
If God only wills what He commands, then He may will something that does not occur, or something may occur that He does not will. This absurdity refutes the Mu'tazilite view.

And the Mu'tazila disagreed in this last point, claiming that the will of Allah does not pertain to evils and ugly things. It is narrated that al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar al-Hamadhani visited al-Sahib ibn 'Abbad and found with him al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini. Al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar was a chief of the Mu'tazila, and al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq was a great figure of Ahl al-Sunna. Al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar said: 'Glory be to Him who is transcendent above foul deeds,' intending to censure Ahl al-Sunna who said that evil occurs from people by the will of Allah. So al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq said: 'Glory be to Him in whose dominion nothing occurs except what He wills.' Al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar said: 'Then does our Lord will to be disobeyed?' Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq said: 'Then is our Lord disobeyed against His will?' Al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar said: 'Do you think, if He prevents me from guidance and decrees upon me perdition, has He done good to me or evil?' Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq said: 'If He prevented you from what is yours, He has done evil; but if He prevented you from what is His, then He singles out for His mercy whom He wills.' And the discussion ended after that. And that to which the Mu'tazila went, of restricting the possible to which will pertains to good, is based on their view that will agrees with good pleasure (al-rida) in meaning. The truth is what Ahl al-Sunna went to: that good pleasure is other than will, and clarification of that will come in the fourth aspect of discourse on this attribute: that Ahl al-Sunna hold that the will of Allah pertains to every possible, whether that possible is good or evil. Yet they differ on the permissibility of attributing evils and ugly things to Him. What the investigators favored is the permissibility of that in the context of teaching (al-ta'lim) but not otherwise. This disagreement also applies to attributing base things to Him; the correct view again is permissibility in the context of teaching only. So you should not say: 'Allah is the creator of monkeys and pigs' except when teaching. This is a kind of reverence (adab) and nothing more. Section on Ilahiyyat. And the Mu'tazila said: 'The will of Allah Most High for the action of the responsible person is precisely His inner command (amruhu al-nafsi) for that action.' So according to them, Allah wills only what He commands of faith and obedience, whether that occurs or not. Thus, the faith of Abu Jahl, for example, according to Ahl al-Sunna, is commanded but not willed by Him, because He knew it would not occur; and the disbelief of Abu Jahl is forbidden and occurs by the will and power of Allah. According to the Mu'tazila, the faith of Abu Jahl is what Allah wills, not his disbelief, because his faith is what is commanded. The proponents of this view have clung to arguments flimsier than a spider's web. Their summary is: willing the ugly—which is the forbidden, like the disbelief of Abu Jahl—is ugly; punishment for what is willed is injustice; forbidding what is willed and commanding what is not willed is foolishness; and Allah is transcendent above ugly things, injustice, and foolishness. Their statement that 'willing the ugly is ugly' was rebutted: by saying we do not concede the ugliness of that; rather, it is good, the only issue being that its aspect of goodness may be hidden from us. Their statement that 'punishment for what is willed is injustice' was rebutted: by saying we do not concede it as injustice, because injustice is disposing of another's property without permission, and Allah only disposes of His own property. Their statement that 'forbidding what is willed and commanding what is not willed is foolishness' was rebutted: by saying we do not concede that; because the place for that being foolishness would be if the purpose were limited to desire for compliance, but the purpose of command and prohibition may be testing (al-ibtila') or showing the corruption of the preparedness of the commanded or forbidden one. Then it follows from this doctrine of the Mu'tazila that Allah may will a thing and it does not occur, and that a thing may occur while He does not will it. Ibn Qasim said: 'The emergence of this statement from an intelligent person is unlikely; how can a person imagine the failure of Allah's will and the occurrence of the devil's will?' Until some said: 'There is no doubt in the disbelief of one who believes that.' And it is narrated from 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd that he said: 'No one has ever compelled me like a Magian who was with me on a ship. I said to him: Why do you not embrace Islam? He said: Allah has not willed my Islam; if He wills my Islam, I will become Muslim. I said to him: ...'
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite position that God's will does not extend to evil or ugly actions. The author begins by recounting a famous debate between the Mu'tazilite leader al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar and the Sunni scholar Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini. The Mu'tazilite opens with a declaration of God's transcendence from foul acts, intending to criticize the Sunni view that evil occurs by God's will. Abu Ishaq responds by affirming God's absolute sovereignty: nothing occurs in His dominion except what He wills. The cross-examination continues, exploring whether God wills to be disobeyed, and God's justice in granting or withholding guidance. Abu Ishaq's final answer distinguishes between what is 'yours' (i.e., what God owes you) and what is 'His' (i.e., His gifts). The debate ends. The author then explains the basis of the Mu'tazilite error: they equate will (irada) with good pleasure (rida), whereas the Sunni position distinguishes them. The author notes that while Sunnis agree God's will encompasses all possibilities (good and evil), they differ on whether it is permissible to attribute evil or base things to God directly. The preferred view is that such attributions are only allowed in a teaching context, as a matter of reverent adab. The heading 'Section on Ilahiyyat' appears. The author then presents the Mu'tazilite argument in detail: they define God's will for an action as identical to His inner command, so He only wills what He commands (faith and obedience). Thus, they claim that God wills the faith of Abu Jahl (since it is commanded) not his disbelief (which is forbidden). They base this on three arguments: (1) willing the ugly is itself ugly, (2) punishing for what is willed is injustice, and (3) forbidding what is willed and commanding what is not willed is foolishness. Each of these is rebutted. The first rebuttal: we do not concede that willing the ugly is ugly; it can be good, even if the aspect of goodness is hidden. The second rebuttal: punishing for what is willed is not injustice because injustice is only in disposing of another's property; God acts within His own dominion. The third rebuttal: forbidding what is willed and commanding what is not willed is not foolishness, because the purpose of command and prohibition may be testing (ibtila') or revealing the corruption of the person's readiness, not merely to obtain compliance. The author then points out a devastating consequence of the Mu'tazilite position: it implies that God may will something that does not occur, and that something may occur that He does not will—both of which are impossible. Ibn Qasim is quoted to emphasize the absurdity: it would mean the devil's will prevails over God's. The passage ends with a story about 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd and a Magian, cutting off mid-sentence.

Key Terms
إرادة القبيح
الظلم
السفه
الابتلاء
المعتزلة
أهل السنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God wills evil, then He must be unjust or foolish.
Correction: The rebuttals show that willing evil is not necessarily ugly, punishment in God's dominion is not injustice, and forbidding what is willed may have a wise purpose like testing.

Checkpoint
How does the author rebut the Mu'tazilite claim that 'forbidding what is willed and commanding what is not willed is foolishness'?
A) By saying it is not foolish because God is not subject to human logic
B) By arguing that the purpose may be testing or revealing corruption, not just compliance
C) By stating that God never forbids what He wills
D) By citing the debate with al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar

Key Takeaway
The three Mu'tazilite arguments against God willing evil are each refuted, and their position leads to the impossible consequence that God's will could fail.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever questioned God's justice when something evil happened to you? How does the rebuttal that God acts only in His own dominion—and that injustice is defined by ownership—change your perspective? Will you now trust Him even when you don't understand?
?
When you fall into sin, do you sometimes excuse yourself by thinking 'Allah didn't will for me to obey'? The Magian used that exact logic. How does this lesson confront that excuse, and what will you say to yourself the next time it arises?
?
Think of a command of Allah that is difficult for you to follow. Can you see it as a test (al-ibtila') of your preparedness, not as a contradiction of His will? Will you commit to obeying that command today, knowing that your effort itself is part of the test?

The Purpose of Divine Commands
The author explains the first answer to the objection about divine command and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's commands may not correspond to His will and how this serves to cut off excuses.

Core Idea
Commands have multiple wisdoms beyond mere compliance.

This lesson addresses a key objection to the Ash'ari view about divine command and will. The author presents the problem: How can God command something He does not will? The answer reveals that divine commands serve multiple wisdoms, including cutting off arguments from those who disobey.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the author sets up the problem and gives a concise answer.
2
Identify the objection and answer
The passage begins with the fact that God commands what He does not will. The objection asks how this is possible. Find the answer and note its two parts: the benefit of command is not limited to compliance, and there may be hidden wisdoms.
3
Reflect on the wisdom of cutting off excuses
The author highlights one specific wisdom: cutting off excuses from disbelievers and sinners. Consider why this is important for divine justice.
This connects to the broader Ash'ari emphasis on establishing accountability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم الإلهيات بالإيمان ولم يرده منه ، وأمر عامة المذنبين بالطاعة ولم يردها منهم . فإن قلت فكيف يأمر الله تعالى بشيء وهو مريد لعدم حصوله ؟ عنا هذا القبيل. فالجواب: أن فائدة الأمر لم تنحصر في رغبة الامتثال ، بل يجوز أن تكون ثَمَّة حكمةٌ غابت وهي معلومة له سبحانه ، على أنا ندرك بعض حكم الله في أوامره التي من ومنها قطع الحجة على الكفَّار والعصاة حتّى لا يقول أحدهم : لو كلفتني لَقُمْتُ بما كلفتنيه. وقال المعتزلة : إن إرادة الله تعالى الفعل من المكلف عين أمره النفسي بهذا الفعل فعندهم أنَّ الله تعالى لا يريد إلا ما أمر به من الإيمان والطَّاعة سواء أوقع ذلك أم لا ، فإيمانُ أبي جهل مثلا - عند أهل السنة - مأمور به غير مراد له ، لأنه علم عدم وقوعه، وكفر أبي جهل منهي عنه وهو واقع بإرادة الله وقدرته ، وعند المعتزلة: إيمان أبي جهل هو المراد الله تعالى لا كفره ، لأن إيمانه هو المأمور به ، وقد تمسَّكَ أهل هذا الرَّأي بشُبَه هي أَوْهَى مِن بيت العنكبوت ، وحاصلها أنَّ إرادة القبيح ـ وهو المنهي عنه ككفر أبي جهل ـ قبيح، والعقاب على ما أُريدَ ظلم، والنهي عمَّا يراد والأمر بما لا يُراد سفة، والله تعالى منزه عن السفه. القبائح، وعن الظلم، وعن ورد قولهم بأن إرادة القبيح قبيح »: بأنَّا لا نُسلّم قُبْحَ ذلك ، بل هو حَسنُ ، غاية الأمر أنه قد يخفى علينا وجه حُسْنِه . ورُدَّ قولهم بأن العقاب على ما أريد ظلم»: بأنا لا نُسلّم كونه ظلما ؛ لأنَّ الظلم هو التصرف في ملك الغير بغير إذنه، والله تعالى إنما يتصرف في ملكه ورد قولهم بأن النهي عما يراد والأمر بما لا يرادُ سَفَهُ : بأنا لا نُسلّم ذلك ؛ لأن محل كون ذلك سفها أن لو انحصر المقصود في رغبة الامتثال ، ولكن قد يكون الغرض من الأمر والنهي الابتلاء أو إظهار فساد استعداد المأمور أو المنهي ، ثم إنه يلزم على مقالة المعتزلة هذه: أنّ الله تعالى قد يريد الشيء ولا يقع ، وأن الشيء قد يقع وهو سبحانه لا يريده. وقال ابن قاسم: «وصدور هذه المقالة من عاقل مستبعد ؛ إذ كيف يظنُّ إنسان تخلَّفَ مُرادِ الله تعالى ووقوع مُراد الشيطان ؟ حتى قال بعضهم : لا شك في كفر من يعتقد ذلك». وحكي عن عمرو بن عبيد أنه قال: «ما ألزمني أحد مثل ما ألزمني مجوسي كان معي في سفينة، قلتُ له: لم لا تسلم ؟ فقال : إن الله تعالى لم يرد إسلامي، فإذا أراد إسلامي أسلمتُ ، فقلت= للمجوسي: إن الله يريد إسلامك ولكنَّ الشياطين لا يتركونك ، فقال المجوسي: فأنا إذا أكونُ مع الشريك الأغلب». محي.
Section on Divine Matters: He believes but He did not will it from him, and He commanded the generality of sinners to obey but He did not will it from them. If you say: How does God Almighty command something while He wills its non-occurrence? [We say] this is of that type. The answer: The benefit of command is not limited to desire for compliance, rather it is possible that there is a wisdom that is hidden and known to Him, especially since we perceive some of God's wisdom in His commands, among which is cutting off the argument against the disbelievers and sinners so that none of them can say: If You had charged me, I would have done what You charged me. The Mu'tazilites said: Indeed, God Almighty's willing of an action from the morally responsible person is identical to His internal command of that action, so they hold that God Almighty only wills what He commands of faith and obedience, whether that occurs or not. For example, the faith of Abu Jahl – according to the Ahl al-Sunnah – is commanded but not willed by Him, because He knew it would not occur, and the disbelief of Abu Jahl is forbidden and occurs by God's will and power. According to the Mu'tazilites, the faith of Abu Jahl is what God Almighty wills, not his disbelief, because his faith is what is commanded. The adherents of this view have clung to arguments weaker than a spider's web. Their gist is that willing evil – which is what is forbidden, like the disbelief of Abu Jahl – is evil, punishment for what is willed is injustice, forbidding what is willed and commanding what is not willed is foolishness, and God Almighty is transcendent above foolishness, abominations, and injustice. Refutation of their statement that willing evil is evil: We do not concede the evilness of that; rather it is good, the only issue being that the aspect of its goodness may be hidden from us. Their statement that punishment for what is willed is injustice is refuted: We do not concede it is injustice, because injustice is disposing of another's property without permission, and God Almighty only disposes of His own property. Their statement that forbidding what is willed and commanding what is not willed is foolishness is refuted: We do not concede that, because the locus of such foolishness would be if the purpose were limited to desire for compliance, but the purpose of command and prohibition may be testing or revealing the corruption of the preparedness of the commanded or forbidden person. Furthermore, this doctrine of the Mu'tazilites entails that God Almighty may will something and it does not occur, and that something may occur while He does not will it. Ibn Qasim said: 'The issuance of this statement from an intelligent person is improbable; for how can a person imagine the will of God Almighty being thwarted and the will of Satan occurring?' Until some said: 'There is no doubt about the disbelief of one who believes that.' It is narrated from 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd that he said: 'No one has compelled me with a consequence as much as a Magian who was with me on a ship compelled me. I said to him: Why do you not accept Islam? He said: God Almighty did not will my Islam; if He wills my Islam, I will become Muslim. I said to the Magian: God wills your Islam, but the devils do not leave you. The Magian said: Then I am with the stronger partner.' End.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the Ash'ari position on divine will and command, refuting the Mu'tazilite view. It begins by presenting an objection: how can God command something He does not will? The answer is that commands have multiple purposes, not just desire for compliance—they may serve to cut off excuses or to test. The author then explains the Mu'tazilite claim that God's will is identical to His internal command, so He only wills what He commands. The Mu'tazilites argue that willing evil is evil, punishing what is willed is unjust, and commanding what is not willed is foolish. The refutation proceeds: (1) willing evil is not necessarily evil—it may be good in a way hidden from us; (2) punishment on what God wills is not injustice because God acts in His own dominion; (3) commanding what is not willed is not foolish because the purpose may be testing or revealing the person's preparedness. The passage then notes the logical entailments of the Mu'tazilite view: that God could will something that does not occur, and that something could occur without His will. This is impossible for the Ash'aris. Finally, a story illustrates the absurdity of the Mu'tazilite position through a dialogue between 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd and a Magian.

The Mu'tazilite Position on Will and Command
The author presents the Mu'tazilite position and their three arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite claim that God's will is identical to His command and their three rational arguments.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilites equate God's will with His internal command.

This lesson presents the Mu'tazilite understanding of divine will and command, which directly contradicts the Ash'ari view. The author outlines their position and the three arguments they use to support it, setting the stage for refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the Mu'tazilite definition of will and the example of Abu Jahl to clarify their view.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite view
They believe that God's will for an action is exactly the same as His internal command. Therefore, God only wills what He commands. This leads to the conclusion that the faith of Abu Jahl must be willed by God, even if it never occurs.
3
Recognize their three arguments
Identify the three claims: (1) willing evil is evil, (2) punishing what is willed is injustice, (3) commanding what is not willed is foolish. These will be refuted in the next lesson.

Source Text

Section on Divine Matters: He believes but He did not will it from him, and He commanded the generality of sinners to obey but He did not will it from them. If you say: How does God Almighty command something while He wills its non-occurrence? [We say] this is of that type. The answer: The benefit of command is not limited to desire for compliance, rather it is possible that there is a wisdom that is hidden and known to Him, especially since we perceive some of God's wisdom in His commands, among which is cutting off the argument against the disbelievers and sinners so that none of them can say: If You had charged me, I would have done what You charged me. The Mu'tazilites said: Indeed, God Almighty's willing of an action from the morally responsible person is identical to His internal command of that action, so they hold that God Almighty only wills what He commands of faith and obedience, whether that occurs or not. For example, the faith of Abu Jahl – according to the Ahl al-Sunnah – is commanded but not willed by Him, because He knew it would not occur, and the disbelief of Abu Jahl is forbidden and occurs by God's will and power. According to the Mu'tazilites, the faith of Abu Jahl is what God Almighty wills, not his disbelief, because his faith is what is commanded. The adherents of this view have clung to arguments weaker than a spider's web. Their gist is that willing evil – which is what is forbidden, like the disbelief of Abu Jahl – is evil, punishment for what is willed is injustice, forbidding what is willed and commanding what is not willed is foolishness, and God Almighty is transcendent above foolishness, abominations, and injustice. Refutation of their statement that willing evil is evil: We do not concede the evilness of that; rather it is good, the only issue being that the aspect of its goodness may be hidden from us. Their statement that punishment for what is willed is injustice is refuted: We do not concede it is injustice, because injustice is disposing of another's property without permission, and God Almighty only disposes of His own property. Their statement that forbidding what is willed and commanding what is not willed is foolishness is refuted: We do not concede that, because the locus of such foolishness would be if the purpose were limited to desire for compliance, but the purpose of command and prohibition may be testing or revealing the corruption of the preparedness of the commanded or forbidden person. Furthermore, this doctrine of the Mu'tazilites entails that God Almighty may will something and it does not occur, and that something may occur while He does not will it. Ibn Qasim said: 'The issuance of this statement from an intelligent person is improbable; for how can a person imagine the will of God Almighty being thwarted and the will of Satan occurring?' Until some said: 'There is no doubt about the disbelief of one who believes that.' It is narrated from 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd that he said: 'No one has compelled me with a consequence as much as a Magian who was with me on a ship compelled me. I said to him: Why do you not accept Islam? He said: God Almighty did not will my Islam; if He wills my Islam, I will become Muslim. I said to the Magian: God wills your Islam, but the devils do not leave you. The Magian said: Then I am with the stronger partner.' End.

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the Ash'ari position on divine will and command, refuting the Mu'tazilite view. It begins by presenting an objection: how can God command something He does not will? The answer is that commands have multiple purposes, not just desire for compliance—they may serve to cut off excuses or to test. The author then explains the Mu'tazilite claim that God's will is identical to His internal command, so He only wills what He commands. The Mu'tazilites argue that willing evil is evil, punishing what is willed is unjust, and commanding what is not willed is foolish. The refutation proceeds: (1) willing evil is not necessarily evil—it may be good in a way hidden from us; (2) punishment on what God wills is not injustice because God acts in His own dominion; (3) commanding what is not willed is not foolish because the purpose may be testing or revealing the person's preparedness. The passage then notes the logical entailments of the Mu'tazilite view: that God could will something that does not occur, and that something could occur without His will. This is impossible for the Ash'aris. Finally, a story illustrates the absurdity of the Mu'tazilite position through a dialogue between 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd and a Magian.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilites, what is the relationship between God's will and His command?
A) They are unrelated
B) God's will is identical to His internal command
C) God's will is limited to actions that occur
D) God commands what He does not will

Key Takeaway
Mu'tazilites believe God's will equals His command, leading to three specific contentions about evil, injustice, and foolishness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin despite knowing Allah's command, do you assume that Allah must have willed that sin because He commanded otherwise? How does this lesson challenge that assumption and shape your reliance on His mercy?
?
Think of a situation where you saw a believer struggling with a trial that seemed contrary to Allah's command of ease. Did you question Allah's wisdom or justice? How does the Ahl al-Sunnah distinction between will and command help you trust His plan while affirming His goodness?
?
If you truly believe that Allah's command and will are distinct, how should that change the way you respond to His commands in your life? Make a specific commitment today to obey a command of Allah even if you do not see its immediate outcome or benefit.

Refuting the Mu'tazilite Arguments
The author refutes the three Mu'tazilite arguments one by one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari refutations of the three Mu'tazilite claims about divine will and command.

Core Idea
Each Mu'tazilite argument fails due to God's sovereignty and hidden wisdoms.

This lesson presents the Ash'ari responses to each of the three Mu'tazilite contentions. The refutations center on the hidden goodness of God's actions, His absolute dominion, and the multiple purposes of commands.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutations
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that each refutation begins with 'We do not concede' and then provides a reason.
2
Analyze each refutation
For the claim about evil: the author says willing evil may be good in a hidden way. For injustice: defines injustice as acting in another's dominion; God owns everything. For foolishness: says commands have purposes beyond compliance.
3
Connect to the previous lesson
These refutations directly counter the Mu'tazilite arguments from the previous lesson. Note how each refutation preserves God's perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم الإلهيات بالإيمان ولم يرده منه ، وأمر عامة المذنبين بالطاعة ولم يردها منهم . فإن قلت فكيف يأمر الله تعالى بشيء وهو مريد لعدم حصوله ؟ عنا هذا القبيل. فالجواب: أن فائدة الأمر لم تنحصر في رغبة الامتثال ، بل يجوز أن تكون ثَمَّة حكمةٌ غابت وهي معلومة له سبحانه ، على أنا ندرك بعض حكم الله في أوامره التي من ومنها قطع الحجة على الكفَّار والعصاة حتّى لا يقول أحدهم : لو كلفتني لَقُمْتُ بما كلفتنيه. وقال المعتزلة : إن إرادة الله تعالى الفعل من المكلف عين أمره النفسي بهذا الفعل فعندهم أنَّ الله تعالى لا يريد إلا ما أمر به من الإيمان والطَّاعة سواء أوقع ذلك أم لا ، فإيمانُ أبي جهل مثلا - عند أهل السنة - مأمور به غير مراد له ، لأنه علم عدم وقوعه، وكفر أبي جهل منهي عنه وهو واقع بإرادة الله وقدرته ، وعند المعتزلة: إيمان أبي جهل هو المراد الله تعالى لا كفره ، لأن إيمانه هو المأمور به ، وقد تمسَّكَ أهل هذا الرَّأي بشُبَه هي أَوْهَى مِن بيت العنكبوت ، وحاصلها أنَّ إرادة القبيح ـ وهو المنهي عنه ككفر أبي جهل ـ قبيح، والعقاب على ما أُريدَ ظلم، والنهي عمَّا يراد والأمر بما لا يُراد سفة، والله تعالى منزه عن السفه. القبائح، وعن الظلم، وعن ورد قولهم بأن إرادة القبيح قبيح »: بأنَّا لا نُسلّم قُبْحَ ذلك ، بل هو حَسنُ ، غاية الأمر أنه قد يخفى علينا وجه حُسْنِه . ورُدَّ قولهم بأن العقاب على ما أريد ظلم»: بأنا لا نُسلّم كونه ظلما ؛ لأنَّ الظلم هو التصرف في ملك الغير بغير إذنه، والله تعالى إنما يتصرف في ملكه ورد قولهم بأن النهي عما يراد والأمر بما لا يرادُ سَفَهُ : بأنا لا نُسلّم ذلك ؛ لأن محل كون ذلك سفها أن لو انحصر المقصود في رغبة الامتثال ، ولكن قد يكون الغرض من الأمر والنهي الابتلاء أو إظهار فساد استعداد المأمور أو المنهي ، ثم إنه يلزم على مقالة المعتزلة هذه: أنّ الله تعالى قد يريد الشيء ولا يقع ، وأن الشيء قد يقع وهو سبحانه لا يريده. وقال ابن قاسم: «وصدور هذه المقالة من عاقل مستبعد ؛ إذ كيف يظنُّ إنسان تخلَّفَ مُرادِ الله تعالى ووقوع مُراد الشيطان ؟ حتى قال بعضهم : لا شك في كفر من يعتقد ذلك». وحكي عن عمرو بن عبيد أنه قال: «ما ألزمني أحد مثل ما ألزمني مجوسي كان معي في سفينة، قلتُ له: لم لا تسلم ؟ فقال : إن الله تعالى لم يرد إسلامي، فإذا أراد إسلامي أسلمتُ ، فقلت= للمجوسي: إن الله يريد إسلامك ولكنَّ الشياطين لا يتركونك ، فقال المجوسي: فأنا إذا أكونُ مع الشريك الأغلب». محي.
Section on Divine Matters: He believes but He did not will it from him, and He commanded the generality of sinners to obey but He did not will it from them. If you say: How does God Almighty command something while He wills its non-occurrence? [We say] this is of that type. The answer: The benefit of command is not limited to desire for compliance, rather it is possible that there is a wisdom that is hidden and known to Him, especially since we perceive some of God's wisdom in His commands, among which is cutting off the argument against the disbelievers and sinners so that none of them can say: If You had charged me, I would have done what You charged me. The Mu'tazilites said: Indeed, God Almighty's willing of an action from the morally responsible person is identical to His internal command of that action, so they hold that God Almighty only wills what He commands of faith and obedience, whether that occurs or not. For example, the faith of Abu Jahl – according to the Ahl al-Sunnah – is commanded but not willed by Him, because He knew it would not occur, and the disbelief of Abu Jahl is forbidden and occurs by God's will and power. According to the Mu'tazilites, the faith of Abu Jahl is what God Almighty wills, not his disbelief, because his faith is what is commanded. The adherents of this view have clung to arguments weaker than a spider's web. Their gist is that willing evil – which is what is forbidden, like the disbelief of Abu Jahl – is evil, punishment for what is willed is injustice, forbidding what is willed and commanding what is not willed is foolishness, and God Almighty is transcendent above foolishness, abominations, and injustice. Refutation of their statement that willing evil is evil: We do not concede the evilness of that; rather it is good, the only issue being that the aspect of its goodness may be hidden from us. Their statement that punishment for what is willed is injustice is refuted: We do not concede it is injustice, because injustice is disposing of another's property without permission, and God Almighty only disposes of His own property. Their statement that forbidding what is willed and commanding what is not willed is foolishness is refuted: We do not concede that, because the locus of such foolishness would be if the purpose were limited to desire for compliance, but the purpose of command and prohibition may be testing or revealing the corruption of the preparedness of the commanded or forbidden person. Furthermore, this doctrine of the Mu'tazilites entails that God Almighty may will something and it does not occur, and that something may occur while He does not will it. Ibn Qasim said: 'The issuance of this statement from an intelligent person is improbable; for how can a person imagine the will of God Almighty being thwarted and the will of Satan occurring?' Until some said: 'There is no doubt about the disbelief of one who believes that.' It is narrated from 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd that he said: 'No one has compelled me with a consequence as much as a Magian who was with me on a ship compelled me. I said to him: Why do you not accept Islam? He said: God Almighty did not will my Islam; if He wills my Islam, I will become Muslim. I said to the Magian: God wills your Islam, but the devils do not leave you. The Magian said: Then I am with the stronger partner.' End.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the Ash'ari position on divine will and command, refuting the Mu'tazilite view. It begins by presenting an objection: how can God command something He does not will? The answer is that commands have multiple purposes, not just desire for compliance—they may serve to cut off excuses or to test. The author then explains the Mu'tazilite claim that God's will is identical to His internal command, so He only wills what He commands. The Mu'tazilites argue that willing evil is evil, punishing what is willed is unjust, and commanding what is not willed is foolish. The refutation proceeds: (1) willing evil is not necessarily evil—it may be good in a way hidden from us; (2) punishment on what God wills is not injustice because God acts in His own dominion; (3) commanding what is not willed is not foolish because the purpose may be testing or revealing the person's preparedness. The passage then notes the logical entailments of the Mu'tazilite view: that God could will something that does not occur, and that something could occur without His will. This is impossible for the Ash'aris. Finally, a story illustrates the absurdity of the Mu'tazilite position through a dialogue between 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd and a Magian.

أمر
نهي
معتزلة

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the Mu'tazilite argument that punishing what is willed is injustice?
A) He says it is justice because the person deserves it
B) He says injustice is only in disposing of another's property without permission, and God owns everything
C) He says punishment is not actually willed by God
D) He says the punishment is a test

Key Takeaway
God's absolute dominion and the multiple purposes of commands refute the claims that willing evil is evil, punishment is unjust, or commanding what is not willed is foolish.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems evil or unjust, do you instinctively assume you know its full story? How does the hidden wisdom in God's will challenge the way you react?
?
If you truly believe that God owns everything and that His punishment is never injustice, how would that change the way you respond to people who are suffering—especially those you think 'deserve' it?
?
God commands things He knows you will disobey and forbids things you will do. How does that shift your understanding of why you exist? Is obedience merely for God's benefit, or for yours?

Consequences and Anecdote
The author presents the logical consequences of the Mu'tazilite view and an anecdote illustrating its absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical consequences of the Mu'tazilite view and see its absurdity through the anecdote of 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilite view implies God's will can be thwarted, which is absurd.

This lesson concludes the argument by showing the unacceptable implications of the Mu'tazilite position. The author then reinforces with an anecdote that illustrates the logical end of their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the consequences
Read the Arabic and translation. The author points out that if God only wills what He commands, then His will could fail to occur, or things could occur without His will.
2
Understand the logical entailments
These consequences are impossible for the Ash'aris because they believe God's will is always fulfilled. The Mu'tazilite view leads to a contradiction.
3
Analyze the anecdote
The story of 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd and the Magian shows that if God does not will someone's faith, then the devils become partners with God in causing disbelief. This reduces to absurdity.

Source Text

?

?

Divine Attributes
beginner

The Divine Attribute of Will and Its Distinctions
Sanusi defines divine will and distinguishes it from command, knowledge, and pleasure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the divine attribute of will (al-Irada) is defined and how it is distinguished from divine command, knowledge, and pleasure.

Core Idea
God's will is not the same as His command, knowledge, or pleasure.

This passage from al-Sanusi's Jawhara defines the attribute of will and clarifies common misunderstandings by distinguishing it from three related concepts. Understanding these distinctions is essential for precise theological discourse about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the English translation. Pay attention to how the author presents the definition and then the distinctions.
The Arabic uses footnote markers; focus on the main text.
2
Identify the definition of will
Notice that will is defined in relation to 'kaf' (refraining), which is an act of the soul. The definition uses a word other than the direct command 'kuffa'.
This shows will is not the same as verbal command.
3
See the distinction from command
The passage says the difference between will and verbal command is 'most apparent' (ghayat al-zuhur). Consider why this is obvious.
4
Distinguish will from knowledge
Will differs from knowledge, whether eternal or originated. Reflect on how knowledge covers all things but will only pertains to possible things.
5
Distinguish will from pleasure
Will differs from pleasure (rida), which is defined as 'non-objection'. Think of examples where God wills something He does not please (e.g., disbelief).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢ - صفة الإرادة في كفِّ (۱) ، مدلول عليه بلفظ غير نحو «كُفَّ»(۲)، ومغايرتها للأمر اللفظي غاية الظهور (۳) (وَ) غايرت الإرادة أيضا ( عِلْمًا ) (٤) أزليَّا كان أو حادثا ، (وَ) غايرت أيضًا (الرّضَا ) (٥) ، أي : رضاه تعالى، وهو ترك الاعتراض (كَمَا) أي كالتغاير الذي (تَبَتْ) (٦)
2 – The Attribute of Will: [It is] in refraining (kaf) (1), indicated by a word other than like 'kuffa' (2), and its difference from verbal command is most apparent (3). And will also differs from knowledge (4), whether eternal or originated, and it also differs from pleasure (rida) (5), i.e., His pleasure, which is non-objection. As a difference that is established (6).
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the divine attribute of will (al-Irada) and a demonstration of its distinctness from other related concepts. The author states that the attribute of will resides in 'refraining' (kaf), which is a volitional act of the soul, and it is indicated by a word other than 'kuffa' (a command word meaning 'refrain!') – this distinguishes the attribute from the verbal command. The difference between will and verbal command is very clear. Then the author asserts that will is also distinct from knowledge (ilm), whether that knowledge is eternal or originated, because knowledge pertains to all things (possible, impossible, necessary) while will pertains only to possible things. Finally, will is distinct from pleasure (rida), which is defined as non-objection; God may will something He does not please, such as the disbelief of Abu Jahl. The passage concludes by stating that this differentiation is firmly established.

Key Terms
إرادة
أمر
علم
رضا
كف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some mistakenly equate God's will with His command, knowledge, or pleasure.
Correction: This passage shows that will is distinct from verbal command, from knowledge (even eternal), and from pleasure (non-objection). They are separate attributes.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, from what is the attribute of will (al-Irada) distinguished?
A) Only from verbal command
B) From verbal command, knowledge, and pleasure
C) Only from knowledge
D) Only from pleasure

Key Takeaway
God's will is distinct from His command, knowledge, and pleasure; conflating them leads to theological errors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial, do you assume that because God willed it, He must be pleased with your suffering? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what will you now reflect on when hardship comes?
?
You know that God's will differs from His command: He commanded obedience but wills to test you through trials. In what area of your life have you been conflating God's will with what you think He commands, leading to confusion? How will you correct this?
?
If God's will is not His pleasure, then consider your own attitudes: Do you ever feel that because you desire something, it must be good and pleasing to God? How does this lesson call you to surrender your desires to His knowledge and command?

The Attribute of Will: Distinct from Command, Knowledge, and Pleasure
Sanusi defines the attribute of will and distinguishes it from command, knowledge, and pleasure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the divine attribute of will (al-irāda) is defined and how it differs from command, knowledge, and pleasure.

Core Idea
God's will is not the same as His command, knowledge, or pleasure.

Sanusi now turns to a critical distinction in divine attributes: the attribute of will. Many may confuse God's will with His command, knowledge, or pleasure, but each is a distinct concept. This passage clearly sets them apart.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage uses a compact style with key terms.
2
Identify the three distinctions
Find the three things from which the will is distinguished: command (al-amr), knowledge (al-ʿilm), and pleasure (al-riḍā). Note the specific phrasing for each.
For command, Sanusi says 'غاية الظهور' (extremely apparent).
3
Understand the reason for each distinction
For knowledge, the will only pertains to possible things, while knowledge includes necessary, impossible, and possible. For pleasure, the will may seek something God does not approve of, like disbelief.
This reasoning is hinted at in the passage and expanded in the commentary.
4
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to ensure you grasp the key difference between will and knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢ - صفة الإرادة في كفِّ، مدلول عليه بلفظ غير نحو «كُفَّ»، ومغايرتها للأمر اللفظي غاية الظهور (و) غايرت الإرادة أيضا عِلْمًا أزليَّا كان أو حادثا، (و) غايرت أيضًا الرّضَا، أي: رضاه تعالى، وهو ترك الاعتراض (كما) أي كالتغاير الذي ثبت.
2 – The attribute of will: in terms of [command] to refrain, indicated by a word other than like 'Refrain!', and its distinction from the verbal command is extremely apparent. And the will also differs from knowledge – eternal or temporal – and also differs from being pleased, i.e., God’s being pleased, which is refraining from objection, as a distinction like that which is established.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute of will (al-irāda) and clarifies its distinctness from three other concepts: command (al-amr), knowledge (al-ʿilm), and being pleased (al-riḍā). The author begins by addressing the will's relation to command: the will can be indicated by a word like 'kuff' (refrain), which is a request for refraining, but differs from the verbal command itself. The distinction is obvious. Next, the will is distinguished from knowledge, whether eternal or temporal, because knowledge encompasses both possible and impossible things, while will only pertains to possible things. Finally, the will is distinguished from God's pleasure (riḍā), which is understood as His refraining from objecting to something; the will can be directed to something God does not approve of, like the disbelief of Abū Jahl. The passage ends with the phrase 'as a distinction like that which is established', which serves as a transition to the rational proof of the distinctness.

الأمر
العلم
الرضا

Misconception: God's will is identical to His knowledge or His pleasure.
Correction: Will is distinct: it only applies to possible things, while knowledge also includes the impossible; will may seek what God does not approve of, while pleasure is refraining from objection.

Key Takeaway
The will of Allah is distinct from His command, knowledge, and pleasure; each attribute has a unique scope and function.

Rational Proof for the Attribute of Will
Sanusi gives a rational proof for God's will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for God's will based on His being a free agent, as presented by Sanusi.

Core Idea
God's will is proven because He acts by choice.

Sanusi opens the section on divine matters by affirming the necessity of God's will for Ahl al-Sunna. He grounds this in three sources: consensus, scripture, and rational proof. The rational argument centers on the concept of God as a free agent, which necessarily implies will. This lesson unpacks that proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three bases
Read the passage and note that Sanusi gives three bases for affirming God's will: consensus, widespread scriptural mention, and rational proof from free agency.
Focus on the rational proof as the main point.
2
Understand free agency
Free agency (fāʿil bi-l-ikhtiyār) means the agent intends and wills with consideration of alternatives. A chooser looks at both sides and inclines to one.
This definition is key to the syllogism.
3
Reflect on the syllogism
The rational proof is: God is a free agent; every free agent has will; therefore God has will. See how the passage implies this logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم الإلهيات في كونه بالضرورة عند أهل السُّنَّة؛ لأنَّه اتفق على إطلاق القول بأنَّه تعالى مُريد، وشاع ذلك في كلامه تعالى، وكلام أنبيائه عليهم الصَّلاة والسَّلام، ودلّ عليه ما ثبتَ مِن كونه تعالى فاعلا بالاختيار؛ لأنَّ معناه: القصد والإرادة مُلاحظة ما للطَّرَف الآخر، فكأنَّ المُختار ينظر إلى مع الطَّرفين ويميل إلى أحدهما، والمريدُ ينظر إلى الطرف الذي يريده، فإن قلت: فعلى هذا التقدير يتحد المشبَّه والمشبه به، لأن المشبَّه هو التَّغاير وهو أيضا المشبه به، وهذا لا معنى له. فالجواب عن هذا أن للمشبّه متعلقا محذوفا، وللمشبه به متعلقا محذوفا أيضا، والمتعلقان مختلفان، وبهما يختلف المشبه والمشبه به، وتقدير الكلام وغايرت الإرادة الأمر والعلم والرضًا شرعاً تغايرا كائناً كالتغير الذي ثبت عقلا، فالمشبه به هو: التغاير العقلي، والمشبَّه: التَّغاير الشَّرعي، ويجوز في الكاف وجه آخر، وهو أن يكون حرفا دالا على التعليل، ويكون (ما) اسما موصولاً مرادا به الدليل، وكأنه قال: وغايرت الإرادة الأمر والعلم والرضا للدليل الذي ثبت.
Section of Divine Matters: On its being necessary [to affirm the will] according to the Ahl al-Sunna; because there was consensus on stating that God Most High is willing, and that was widespread in His speech and the speech of His prophets, peace and blessings upon them, and it is indicated by what has been established of His being a free agent (fāʿil bi-l-ikhtiyār); because its meaning is: intention and will in consideration of what is for the other side. So the chooser looks at both sides and inclines to one of them, and the willer looks to the side that He wills. If you say: on this estimation the thing compared and the thing compared to become the same, because the thing compared is the distinction and it is also the thing compared to, and this has no meaning. The answer to this is that the thing compared has an omitted relation, and the thing compared to also has an omitted relation, and the two relations are different, and by them the thing compared and the thing compared to differ. The estimation of the speech: 'And the will differs from command, knowledge, and pleasure in the Sharʿ a difference that is like the difference that is established rationally.' So the thing compared to is the rational distinction, and the thing compared is the Sharʿī distinction. And another interpretation of the letter kāf is possible: that it is a letter indicating causation, and the word 'mā' is a relative pronoun meaning 'proof', as if he said: 'And the will differs from command, knowledge, and pleasure for the proof that is established.'
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof for the attribute of will (al-irāda) in God, followed by an objection and its answer concerning a problematic comparison (tashbīh). The author begins by stating that affirming God's will is necessary for Ahl al-Sunna, based on consensus (ijmāʿ), the widespread mention in scripture, and rational proof. The proof is a syllogism: God acts by choice (fāʿil bi-l-ikhtiyār), and every agent by choice has will, therefore God has will. 'Acting by choice' means the agent intends and wills with consideration of alternatives; a chooser looks at both sides and inclines to one, and a willer looks to the side He wills. An objector then raises a problem: if we say the distinction (taghāyur) between will and other attributes is what is compared, and we compare it to a distinction established earlier, then the thing compared (mushabbah) and the thing compared to (mushabbah bihi) become the same, which is meaningless. The answer explains that each has a different omitted relation (mutaʿallaq). The intended meaning is that the will differs in the Sharʿ (scriptural context) just as it differs rationally (ʿaqlan). So the thing compared to is the rational distinction, and the thing compared is the Sharʿī distinction. Alternatively, the kāf of comparison can be interpreted as a kāf of causation, meaning the distinctness of the will is proven by rational proof. This resolves the objection by specifying different aspects for the two sides of the comparison.

Key Terms
الفاعل بالاختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the main rational proof for God's will?
A) Consensus of scholars.
B) Widespread mention in scripture.
C) God being a free agent.
D) Analogical reasoning.

Key Takeaway
God's will is rationally proven because He acts by choice, and choice implies will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will is deliberate and intentional, what does that mean for your daily choices? Do you live as if every moment is under God's will, or do you act as if things happen randomly? How will you change your mindset today to rely on God's will in your decisions?
?
The proof shows God is a free agent, not compelled. Reflect on your own actions: are you acting out of free choice or habit? Are you choosing to submit to God, or are you just going through the motions? Make a specific commitment to make one intentional act of worship today, consciously acknowledging God's will.
?
If God's will is the ultimate cause of all things, how does that challenge your sense of control? Are you holding onto something as if you control it? Identify one area where you need to surrender to God's will and actively do so this week.

Resolving the Tashbih Objection Regarding God's Will
Sanusi resolves a tashbih objection concerning the comparison of God's will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that comparing God's will to other attributes leads to tashbih, and Sanusi's resolution via omitted relations.

Core Idea
A proper comparison distinguishes between rational and Sharʿī distinctions.

In the previous lesson, Sanusi established God's will through rational proof. However, a careful reader might object that comparing the will to other attributes (like command, knowledge, and pleasure) leads to a problematic similarity (tashbih). In this passage, Sanusi anticipates this objection and provides a sophisticated answer that preserves God's transcendence while maintaining the distinction of attributes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector argues that if the distinction of will is compared to a distinction, the thing compared and the thing compared to become identical, which is meaningless.
This would imply tashbih (anthropomorphism).
2
Analyze Sanusi's answer
Sanusi explains that each side has a different omitted relation (mutaʿallaq). The thing compared is the Sharʿī distinction, and the thing compared to is the rational distinction.
Thus they are not identical.
3
Consider the alternative interpretation
The kāf of comparison can also be interpreted as a causative letter, meaning the distinctness of will is proven by rational proof.
This avoids any comparison altogether.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
