The Relation of Divine Speech to Commands and Prohibitions
Sanusi presents the debate on the relation of divine speech to commands and prohibitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly debate on how the attribute of divine speech relates to commands and prohibitions, and how it differs from knowledge.

Core Idea
The relation of God's speech to commands depends on whether they require existence.

This passage explores a nuanced debate in kalam about the relation (ta'luq) of God's attribute of speech to commands and prohibitions. While scholars agree on the general scope of speech, they differ on whether commands and prohibitions require existing objects, leading to different views on the kind of relation speech has to them. This lesson unpacks that disagreement and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the agreed scope of speech's relation
Notice that scholars agree speech relates to everything knowledge relates to (necessary, possible, impossible) with a pre-eternal actual relation, except for command and prohibition.
This sets the baseline.
2
Understand the root disagreement
The disagreement hinges on whether commands and prohibitions necessitate the existence of a commanded or prohibited entity (mamur/manhi).
Two groups emerge from this root.
3
Learn the two positions and their resulting relations
One group (doesn't require existence) maintains a single actual relation. The other group (requires existence) posits two relations: potential before existence, actual after.
Thus, some scholars say one relation exists; others say three.
4
Compare speech with knowledge
Both share the same scope (everything), but they differ in mode: knowledge discloses, speech indicates.
This clarifies why the relation is not identical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكلام على تعلق الكلام فنقول: اتفق العلماء على أنَّ صفة الكلام تتعلق بكل ما يتعلق به العلم من الواجب والجائز والمستحيل، تعلقا تنجيزيا قديما بالنسبة لغير الأمر والنهي، واختلفوا في تعلّق هذه الصفة بالأمر والنهي، والخلاف في هذا مبني على خلاف آخر، حاصله: هل الأمر والنهي يقتضيان مأمورًا ومنهيَّا يتوجهان إليه أو لا يقتضيان ذلك؟ فذهب قوم إلى أنهما لا يقتضيان مأمورًا ومنهيَّا، وهذا الفريق يرى تعلقا تنجيزيا قديما كتعلقه بغيرهما، وذهب فريق آخر: إلى أنَّ الأمر والنهي يقتضيان وجود مأمورٍ ومنهي يتوجهان إليه، وهذا الفريق ذهب إلى أنَّ للكلام بالنظر إلى الأمر والنهي: تعلقا صلوحيًّا قديماً قبل وجود المأمور والمنهي، وتعلقا تنجيزيا حادثا بعد وجود المأمور والمنهي؛ ومِن هنا تعلم أن من العلماء من ذهب إلى أن للكلام تعلقاً واحداً هو: التنجيزي القديم، ومنهم من ذهب إلى أن للكلام ثلاث تعلقات: أحدها تنجيزي قديم، وثانيها صلوحي قديم، وثالثها تنجيزي حادث. وتعلم أنَّ هذا الخلاف مترتب على خلاف آخر في اشتراط وجود المأمور والمنهي وعدم وجودهما. وقد علمت أيضا أنَّ الكلام مساو للعلم في المتعلَّق؛ لأن من علم أمرًا صح أن يتكلم به. والمولى سبحانه عالمٌ في الأزل بما كان، وما يكون، وما لا يكون؛ فصح أن يكلم بهما، ومع تساوي العلم والكلام فيما يتعلقان به فهما مختلفان في التعلق؛ لأن العلم يتعلق بما يتعلق به تعلق انكشاف والكلام يتعلق به تعلق دلالةٍ، فكلامه تعالى يدل على الواجب، وعلى المستحيل، وعلى الجائز.
And as for the discussion on the relation (taluq) of speech, we say: The scholars agreed that the attribute of speech relates to everything that knowledge relates to, of the necessary, the possible, and the impossible, with a pre-eternal actual relation (taluq tanjizi qadim) with respect to other than command and prohibition. And they differed concerning the relation of this attribute to command and prohibition. The disagreement in this is based on another disagreement, the summary of which: Do command and prohibition necessitate a commanded and a prohibited [entity] to which they are directed, or do they not necessitate that? One group held that they do not necessitate a commanded and a prohibited [entity], and this group sees a pre-eternal actual relation like its relation to other than them. Another group held that command and prohibition necessitate the existence of a commanded and a prohibited [entity] to which they are directed. This group held that speech, with respect to command and prohibition, has a pre-eternal potential relation (taluq suluhi qadim) before the existence of the commanded and the prohibited, and a temporal actual relation (taluq tanjizi hadith) after the existence of the commanded and the prohibited. From this you know that among the scholars are those who held that speech has one relation: the pre-eternal actual [relation]; and among them are those who held that speech has three relations: one pre-eternal actual, a second pre-eternal potential, and a third temporal actual. And you know that this disagreement is dependent on another disagreement concerning the condition of the existence of the commanded and the prohibited and their non-existence. And you also know that speech is equal to knowledge in what it relates to, because whoever knows a thing, it is correct for him to speak about it. And the Master—glorified is He—is eternally knower of what was, what will be, and what will not be; so it is correct for Him to speak about them. And with the equality of knowledge and speech in what they relate to, they differ in the [mode of] relation; because knowledge relates to what it relates to as a relation of disclosure (inkishaf), and speech relates to it as a relation of indication (dalalah). So His speech—exalted is He—indicates the necessary, the impossible, and the possible.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage deals with the relation (taluq) of the attribute of speech to its objects. It begins by noting scholarly agreement: the attribute of speech relates to everything that knowledge relates to—the necessary (wajib), the possible (ja'iz), and the impossible (mustahil)—with a pre-eternal actual relation (tanjizi qadim), except in the case of command and prohibition (amr and nahy). Regarding command and prohibition, there is a disagreement that stems from a prior dispute: Do commands and prohibitions require the existence of a commanded entity (ma'mur) and a prohibited entity (manhi) to which they are directed? One group says no, so the relation of speech to them is the same pre-eternal actual relation as to other objects. Another group says yes, they require existence; therefore, before the commanded/prohibited exist, the relation is potential (suluhi qadim), and after they exist, it becomes actual but temporal (tanjizi hadith). Thus, some scholars hold that speech has only one relation (the pre-eternal actual for all), while others hold that it has three relations: pre-eternal actual (for non-command/prohibition), pre-eternal potential (for command/prohibition before existence), and temporal actual (for command/prohibition after existence). The passage then notes that this difference depends on whether existence of the commanded/prohibited is a condition. Finally, it compares speech and knowledge: they share the same scope (everything), but differ in mode—knowledge is a relation of disclosure (inkishaf), while speech is a relation of indication (dalalah). Allah's speech indicates the necessary, the impossible, and the possible.

تعلق
تنجيزي
صلوحي
الأمر والنهي
المأمور
المنهي
انكشاف
دلالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the relation of divine speech to all objects is uniform and straightforward.
Correction: In fact, there is a significant debate about commands and prohibitions, leading to multiple types of relation (actual, potential, temporal) depending on whether the commanded/prohibited object must exist.

Checkpoint
According to those who hold that commands and prohibitions require existence, what is the relation of speech to command before the commanded exists?
A) Pre-eternal actual (tanjizi qadim).
B) Pre-eternal potential (suluhi qadim).
C) Temporal actual (tanjizi hadith).
D) There is no relation.

Key Takeaway
The relation of divine speech is not uniform; it has distinct modes (actual, potential) depending on the object and whether existence is required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a divine command in the Quran, do you feel it is binding on you only when you are about to act (temporal actual), or do you accept that it has been directed at you pre-eternally (pre-eternal potential)? How would your obedience change if you truly believed the command was speaking to you before you existed?
?
The passage teaches that God's speech indicates (dalalah) what is necessary, impossible, and possible. Which of these categories do you struggle to accept most in your life? Are there prohibitions (impossible for you to do?) that you still try to justify? How can you rely more on the indication of His speech rather than your own reasoning?
?
Compare your reliance on God's knowledge (that He knows everything about you) with your reliance on His speech (what He commands and forbids). Which one shapes your daily choices more? If you found that you trust His knowledge but not His speech, what would that reveal about your heart? What step can you take today to align your actions with His speech?

The Primary Proofs for God's Attribute of Speech
Sanusi lists the three evidences for the attribute of Speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural evidence, consensus, and recurrent transmission that establish God's attribute of Speech.

Core Idea
The primary proof for divine speech is revelation and consensus, not reason alone.

Sanusi begins by clarifying which evidence is reliable when proving that God has the attribute of Speech. While rational arguments exist, he emphasizes that the foundational proofs come from scripture, the unanimous agreement of scholars, and the consistent message of the prophets. This lesson examines these three pillars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compact style here.
2
Identify the three proofs
The passage lists three types of evidence. Find each one and notice how they are presented as the relied-upon method.
The rational proof is mentioned elsewhere as weak; focus on scriptural, consensus, and tawatur.
3
Understand why rational proof is weak
The commentary explains that rational proof relies on analogy from the witnessed world, which does not always apply to the unseen. Ask yourself why this is a limitation.
4
Reflect on the importance of consensus
Consensus (ijma') is given special mention even though it falls under scriptural evidence. Consider why the author highlights it separately.
This shows the weight of unanimous agreement in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتمد في الاستدلال على ثبوتِ صفة الكلام: الدليل السمعي (١) ، وإجماعُ الأمَّة (۲) ، وتواتر النقل عن الأنبياء عليهم الصَّلاة والسلام أنه تعالى متكلم.
وشاع (۳) فيما بين أهل اللسان إطلاق اسم الكلام والقول على المعنى القائم بالنَّفس (٤) ، والأصل في الإطلاق الحقيقةُ .
وإذ ثبتَ أنَّ الباري تعالى متكلّم ، وأنَّه لا معنى للمتكلم إلَّا مَن قامتْ به صفة الكلام، وأنَّ الكلام نفسي وحِسِّيٌّ ، وأنَّه يمتنع قيام الكلام الحِسي بذاته سبحانه .. تعيَّنَ النَّفسي (٥) ، ولا يكونُ إلَّا قديما.
(۱) قوله: (والمعتمد في الاستدلال ... الخ) أشار بذلك إلى أن الدليل العقلي وهو: أنه لو لم يتصف به لاتصف بضده، وهو نقص يستحيل عليه؛ ضعيف، لأنه لم يثبت كونه كمالا إلا الشاهد، وليس كل ما كان كمالا في الشاهد يكون كمالا في الغائب كالزوجة والولد. الشنواني .
(۲) قوله : (وإجماع الأمة) هو داخل في الدليل السمعي، لأن الإجماع لا بد له من الكتاب أو الحديث، وإنما صرّح به من باب ذكر الخاص بعد العام اهتماما بشأنه. الشنواني .
(۳) قوله : ( وشاع ... الخ) جواب عما يقال : مُسلّم ما قلته أنه يدل على أن له تعالى كلاما، من أين يُطلق على المعنى القائم بالذات حتى يكون دالا على المُدَّعى؟ الشنواني .
(٤) قوله: قامت به خلافا للمعتزلة الذين قالوا خلق الكلام، ويلزمهم صحة «أسود» بمعنى خلق السواد، وهي سَفاهة سمجة. الأمير .
(٥) قوله: (وأنه يمتنع قيام الحسي بذاته أي الحروف والأصوات؛ لأنها لا تكون إلا حادثة. وقوله: (تعيّن النفسي :أي: ولا يكون إلا ،قديما :أي: لامتناع قيام الحوادث بذاته سبحانه . الشنواني .
The relied-upon [method] in inference for the establishment of the attribute of Speech is the scriptural evidence (1), and the consensus of the community (2), and the recurrent transmission from the prophets, peace be upon them, that He, exalted, is speaking. And it became widespread (3) among the people of language to apply the terms 'speech' and 'saying' to the meaning subsisting in the soul (4), and the default in application is literality. And when it is established that the Creator, exalted, is speaking, and that there is no meaning of 'speaker' except one in whom the attribute of speech subsists, and that speech is psychological and sensory, and that it is impossible for sensory speech to subsist in His essence, glorified be He, then the psychological [speech] is determined (5), and it cannot but be eternal.
(1) His statement: 'The relied-upon [method] in inference ... etc.' He indicated thereby that the rational proof — which is: that if He were not described with it, He would be described with its opposite, which is a deficiency impossible for Him — is weak, because its being a perfection has only been established in the witnessed [realm], and not everything that is a perfection in the witnessed is a perfection in the unseen, like wife and child. — Al-Shinwani.
(2) His statement: 'And the consensus of the community' — it is included in the scriptural evidence, because consensus must have a basis in the Book or the Sunnah; he only mentioned it explicitly as a mention of the specific after the general due to concern for its importance. — Al-Shinwani.
(3) His statement: 'And it became widespread ... etc.' — This is a response to what might be said: Granted what you have said, that it indicates that He, exalted, has speech, but from where do you apply [the term] to the meaning subsisting in the essence so that it indicates the claim? — Al-Shinwani.
(4) His statement: 'subsisting in Him' — contrary to the Mu'tazila who said that He created speech; and it follows from them the validity of 'aswad' (black) in the meaning of 'he created blackness', and that is a crude foolishness. — Al-Amir.
(5) His statement: 'And that it is impossible for sensory [speech] to subsist in His essence' — i.e., letters and sounds; because they cannot but be originated. And his statement: 'then the psychological [speech] is determined' — i.e., and it cannot but be eternal — i.e., due to the impossibility of originated things subsisting in His essence, glorified be He. — Al-Shinwani.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is a combined proof and definition for the attribute of Speech (kalam). It begins by stating the primary evidence for establishing that attribute: scriptural evidence (the Qur'an and hadith), the consensus of the Muslim community (ijma'), and the recurrent transmission (tawatur) from the prophets that God is a speaker. The author then addresses a potential objection: while the rational proof (that the opposite of speech is deficiency, impossible for God) exists, it is weak because it relies on analogy from the witnessed to the unseen, which does not always hold. The passage then defines the nature of divine speech. It notes that the linguistic custom is to apply the word 'speech' to the inner meaning (al-ma'na al-qa'im bi al-nafs), and the default is literal usage. From the established fact that God is a speaker, and that a speaker is only one in whom the attribute of speech subsists, and that speech is of two types — psychological (nafsi) and sensory (hissi) — it is impossible for sensory speech (letters and sounds) to subsist in God because they are originated. Therefore, the speech attributed to God must be the psychological speech, which is eternal. The footnotes expand on each point: the weakness of the rational proof (not all perfections in the witnessed are perfections in the unseen), the inclusion of consensus under scriptural evidence, the response to the objection of applying the term to an inner meaning, the refutation of the Mu'tazila who say speech is created, and the explanation that sensory speech (letters and sounds) cannot be eternal.

Key Terms
دليل سمعي
إجماع
تواتر
كلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that rational proof alone is sufficient to establish divine speech.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that the relied-upon proof is scriptural, and rational proof is weak.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the relied-upon evidence for establishing the attribute of Speech?
A) Scriptural evidence, consensus, and recurrent transmission
B) Rational proof alone
C) Only consensus
D) Sensory experience

Key Takeaway
The foundation for proving God's attribute of Speech is scriptural evidence, consensus, and prophetic transmission.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you rely more on logical arguments or on the revealed texts when affirming God's attribute of Speech? How does Sanusi's prioritization challenge your own approach?
?
If the primary proof for God speaking is the Qur'an itself, how does that change the way you recite and reflect on the Qur'an? Do you truly hear it as His speech to you right now?
?
The consensus of the early Muslims is a key proof. Are you willing to submit your personal opinions about God to the collective understanding of the righteous predecessors? What is holding you back?

The Nature of Divine Speech: Psychological and Eternal
Sanusi explains that divine speech is psychological and eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's speech must be a psychological attribute, not sensory, and thus eternal.

Core Idea
God's speech is a timeless inner meaning, not sounds and letters.

Having established that God is a speaker, Sanusi now defines what that means. He draws on linguistic usage to show that 'speech' fundamentally refers to a meaning in the soul. Then, through a logical argument, he demonstrates that God's speech cannot consist of sounds and letters (which are created) but must be a psychological attribute that is eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical structure: premise, premise, conclusion.
2
Understand the linguistic argument
Sanusi points out that common usage applies the word 'speech' to the meaning in the soul. This is important because the default is literal, so 'speech' primarily refers to inner meaning.
This counters those who think speech is only sounds.
3
Follow the logical deduction
From the fact that God is a speaker, and that a speaker has the attribute of speech in him, Sanusi deduces that speech must be either psychological or sensory. Since sensory speech (sounds) cannot subsist in God (they are created), it must be psychological.
The conclusion: God's speech is eternal.
4
Note the refutation of the Mu'tazila
The commentary explains that the Mu'tazila claimed God creates speech but does not have speech as an attribute. This argument refutes them by showing that speech must be an attribute subsisting in the essence.
This is a key point of contention with the Mu'tazila school.

The Divine Attribute of Hearing: Definition and First Aspect
Sanusi defines Hearing and begins its first aspect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the divine attribute of Hearing as an eternal attribute, the three aspects of its discussion, and how it differs from human hearing.

Core Idea
God's hearing is an eternal attribute, not a sensory process.

Sanusi now turns to the sixth attribute, Hearing. He defines it concisely and then outlines three angles for discussion: meaning, proof, and object. The passage begins the first angle by contrasting divine hearing with human hearing, emphasizing that God's hearing is not through a sense organ or air conduction. This lesson unpacks the definition and the key clarification regarding the nature of divine hearing.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and three aspects
Read the Arabic passage from 'وسادستها' to 'تتعلق به' to grasp the definition of Hearing and the three aspects Sanusi will discuss.
Notice the concise style: an eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence, perceiving completely.
2
Compare divine and human hearing
Read the first aspect explanation. Contrast human originated hearing (a power in the ear nerve) with divine hearing (eternal attribute). Note that human hearing involves sense organs and air, while divine hearing does not.
The example of Moses hearing God's speech without sound is crucial.
3
Note the dispute with al-Ka'bi
The passage ends by stating that the verifying scholars hold Hearing to be additional to knowledge, contrary to al-Ka'bi and some Mu'tazila. Identify this as a key theological position.

And its sixth [attribute] is Hearing (al-sam‘). So it is similar to what was mentioned regarding the necessity of His being described with it, exalted. And it is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, exalted, pertaining to heard things or to existent things. So it perceives (1) a complete perception, not by way of imagination and mere supposition (2), nor by way of the impression of a sense and the arrival of air (3).
·
(1) His statement: 'so it perceives' — with fat-ha on the ra’: the heard things and the existent things, or with kasra: the attribute. — Al-Shinwani.
(2) His statement: 'a complete perception' — i.e., real. What indicates it is his statement: 'not by way of imagination and mere supposition'. The conjunction of 'mere supposition' to what precedes it is either explicative, as the author mentioned in his small commentary, or according to what al-Kastali mentioned in his commentary on al-Nasafiyya, it is a conjunction of difference. For it is said: imagination (takhayyul) is the observation of perceptibles after their absence from sense, and mere supposition (tawahhum) is the perception of particular meanings related to perceptibles, like the friendship of Zayd and the enmity of ‘Amr. And his statement: 'after the impression of a sense' — i.e., the passion and imprint. And his statement: 'the arrival of air' — i.e., to the auditory meatus. The meaning: the arrival of air qualified with the quality of sound to the meatus, along with its striking the nerve spread in its cavity like a drum skin, as in our hearing. — Al-Shinwani.
(3) The discussion on the attribute of Hearing pertains to it from three aspects: the first aspect: the explanation of the meaning of hearing; the second aspect: the explanation of the proof for the necessity of God, exalted, being described with hearing; and the third aspect: the explanation of what the attribute of hearing pertains to. As for the discussion on the first aspect, we say: Know first that originated hearing, with which man and others are described, is a power deposited in the nerve spread in the cavity of the ear meatus; by it the animal perceives sounds in the usual manner. And there is no impediment to perceive other than sounds with it if God grants that, just as Moses heard the speech of God — which, as we know, is not possessed of sound or letter. Then know that this meaning is impossible for God, exalted. And the verifying scholars have interpreted God’s hearing as: an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, exalted. And they mentioned that this attribute is additional to the attribute of knowledge, contrary to al-Ka‘bi and some of the Mu‘tazila who said that hearing and sight reduce to knowledge of audible and visible things. And the explanation of that will come at the statement of the author: 'and other than knowledge, these as established.' And its meaning is that the attribute of speech, the attribute of hearing, and the attribute of sight are other than the attribute of knowledge. And the evidence that these attributes are other than knowledge is that they are additional [to it] = 263
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of Hearing (al-sam‘) and outlines the three aspects of its discussion. The main text states that Hearing is the sixth attribute, similar to others in necessity, and defines it as an eternal attribute subsisting in God’s essence, pertaining to heard things or existent things. It perceives them completely, not through imagination or mere supposition, nor through sensory impression or air conduction. The first footnote explains the grammatical reading of 'tudraku' and the distinction between imagination and supposition. The second footnote clarifies the nature of the perception as real, not based on sensory processes like human hearing involving air striking the ear drum. The third footnote introduces the three aspects of the discussion: (1) the meaning of hearing, (2) the proof for its necessity, (3) what it pertains to. It then begins the first aspect, contrasting human originated hearing (a power in the ear nerve for perceiving sounds) with divine hearing. It notes that originated hearing can, by God's power, perceive other things (e.g., Moses hearing God's speech without sound). Divine hearing, however, is an eternal attribute, and the verifying scholars hold it to be additional to the attribute of knowledge, contrary to al-Ka‘bi and some Mu‘tazila who reduce hearing and sight to knowledge. The passage ends mid-sentence, noting that the evidence will come later.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's hearing is like human hearing, involving sense organs and air conduction.
Correction: The passage clearly states divine hearing is not via impression of a sense or arrival of air; it is an eternal attribute that perceives directly.

Checkpoint
According to the verifying scholars (al-muhaqqiqun) in the passage, how is God's attribute of Hearing defined?
A) It is identical to the attribute of knowledge.
B) It is an eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence, additional to knowledge.
C) It is a power deposited in the ear nerve.
D) It is the perception of sounds through air.

Key Takeaway
God's hearing is an eternal, non-sensory attribute that is distinct from and additional to His knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you cry out to Allah in du'a, do you truly believe that He hears you in a manner beyond any ear or sound wave? Or do you secretly imagine His hearing as amplified human hearing? How can you actively remind yourself before each prayer that His hearing is eternal and perfect, not dependent on creation?
?
The Mu'tazila reduced God's hearing to His knowledge, perhaps because they found it hard to affirm multiple distinct attributes. Is there any attribute of Allah that you tend to neglect or merge with another in your heart? What step will you take today to affirm each of His attributes as real and distinct?
?
If Allah hears all things completely—even your silent thoughts and unspoken fears—how does that change the way you live when no one else is watching? Will you let this lesson reshape your consciousness of His presence today?

The Divine Attribute of Hearing: Definition and Distinction from Knowledge
Definition and refutation regarding the attribute of hearing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of Allah's attribute of hearing (as-sam') as an eternal, distinct attribute that is not reducible to knowledge or to sensory perception.

Core Idea
Allah's hearing is an eternal, perfect attribute distinct from His knowledge, not a sensory faculty.

This passage from Itḥāf al-Murīd presents the definition of the divine attribute of hearing, clarifying its eternal nature and its distinction from knowledge. The commentator refutes the view of al-Kaʿbī and some Muʿtazila who reduce hearing to knowledge, emphasizing that divine attributes are real and distinct. Understanding this distinction is crucial for proper tawḥīd.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of hearing
Read the opening statement: hearing is an eternal attribute subsisting in Allah's essence, relating to things heard or existents, perceiving completely without imagination, supposition, or sensory means.
The definition uses negative language to exclude human-like perception.
2
Contrast with human hearing
Notice that the commentator distinguishes Allah's hearing from created hearing, which is a power in the ear nerve perceiving sounds. This contrast shows that divine hearing is not like human hearing.
3
Identify the three aspects of discussion
The passage outlines three aspects: meaning, proof, and what it relates to. The commentator focuses on the first aspect (meaning) in this excerpt.
This structure helps organize the discussion.
4
Understand the refutation
Read the refutation of al-Kaʿbī and the Muʿtazila who claim hearing and sight are reducible to knowledge. The commentator argues that in the human case, these faculties are additional to knowledge, so the same must hold for Allah unless evidence forces otherwise.
The principle is that terms retain their distinct meanings.
5
Reflect on divine perfection
Note the emphasis that Allah's attributes are perfect and free from created defects like hiddenness, increase, or decrease. Even if the object and type of disclosure are similar, there is necessarily a difference due to absolute perfection.
The true reality of these attributes is entrusted to Allah.

٦ - صفة السمع، تعريفها، وبيان تعلقها

و سادستُها : (السَّمْعُ) فهو مِثلُ ما ذُكر في وجوب اتصافه تعالى به. وهو: صفةٌ أزليَّةٌ قائمة بذاتِه تعالى، تتعلق بالمسموعات، أو بالموجودات ، فتُدرَكُ (۱) إدراكًا تاما (۲)، لا على طريق التخيل والتّوهم(٢) ، ولا على طريق تأثر حاسة ووصول هواء (۳) .

(۱) قوله: (فتدرك) بفتح الراء ؛ المسموعات والموجودات ، أو بكسرها ؛ الصفة الشنواني (۲) قوله: (إدراكا (تاما أي حقيقيا، يدل عليه قوله : (لا) على طريق التخيل والتوهم) وعطف التوهم على ما قبله تفسير ، ذكره المنصف في شرحه الصغير، وعلى ما ذكره الكستلي على شرح النسفية) عطف مغاير ؛ حيث يقال: التخيل أي ملاحظة المحسوسات بعد غيبتها عن الحس والتوهم إدراك المعاني الجزئية المتعلقة بالمحسوسات ؛ كصداقة زيد، وعداوة عمرو. وقوله : بعد تأثر حاسة) أي: الانفعال والانطباع وقوله: (ووصول هواء) أي: إلى الصماخ والمعنى: وصول هواء مُتكيف بكيفية الصوت إلى الصماخ ، مع قرعه للعصبة المفروشة في مقعره كجلدة الطبل كما في سمعنا الشنواني .

(۳) الكلام في صفة السمع يتعلق بها من ثلاث جهات الجهة الأولى: بيان معنى السمع، والجهة الثانية: بيان الدليل على وجوب اتصاف الله تعالى بالسمع ، والجهة الثالثة: بيان ما تتعلق به

سمع
صفة السمع. أما الكلام على الجهة الأولى فنقول : اعلم أولا أنَّ السَّمع الحادث الذي يوصف به الإنسان وغيره قوَّة مودعة في العصب المفروش في مُقَرَّر صِماخ الأُذُنِ ، يدرك بها الحيوانُ الأصوات على وجه العادة، ولا مانع من أنْ يُدرك بها غير الأصواتِ إذا وهبه الله ذلك، كما موسى كلام الله وهو ـ كما علمنا - ليس بذي صوت ولا حرف. ثمَّ اعلم أنَّ هذا المعنى مستحيل على الله تعالى، وقد فسر المحققونَ سمع الله تعالى بأنه: صفة أزلية قائمة بذاته تعالى وذكروا أنَّ هذه الصفةَ زائدة على صفة العلم، خلافًا للكعبي وبعض المعتزلة الذين قالوا: إنّ السَّمع والبصر يرجعان إلى العلم بالمسموعات والمبصرات ، وسيأتي بيان ذلك عند قول المصنف: وغير علم هذه كما ثبت» ومعناه: أنَّ صفة الكلام وصفة السمع وصفة البصر غير صفة العلم، وآيةُ أنَّ هذه الصفات غير صفة العلم أنها زائدة على العلم في الشاهد وهو الإنسان، والأصل المغايرة فيما ورد في حق الله تعالى ليكونَ لكل لفظ معناه، والتأويل من غير دليل ومن غير حاجة إلى التأويل تلاعب يجب ألَّا يُصار إليه . نعم ، الواجب أن تعتقد أنَّ عِلم الله تعالى يستحيل عليه الخفاء بوجه من الوجوه، وأنَّ الأمر ليس على ما يعهد لنا مِن أنَّ البصر يفيد بالمشاهدة وضوحاً فوق العلم، بل جميع صفاته تامة كاملة يستحيل عليها ما كان مِن سِماتِ الحوادثِ من الخفاء، والزيادة، والنقص.. إلى غير ذلك ، وإنْ اتَّحد المُتعلّق وكانت الجهة متحدة بالنّوع كالانكشاف في السمع والبصر والعلم، لكن لا بدَّ مِن تغاير ، خصوصاً مع الكمال المطلق ، وكنه ذلك وحقيقته يُفَوَّض عِلْمُه إلى الله تعالى . ومن العلماء من عرَّف صفة السَّمع بقوله: صفة ينكشف بها الشيء ويتضح كالعلم وسنشرح هذا التعريف في الكلام على صفة البصر.
6 - The attribute of hearing, its definition, and explanation of its relation. And its sixth is: (Hearing) so it is like what was mentioned regarding the obligation of His, exalted is He, being described with it. And it is: an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, exalted is He, relating to things heard, or to existents, so it perceives a complete perception, not by way of imagination and supposition, nor by way of the impression of a sense and the arrival of air. (1) His statement: (so it perceives) with fat-ha on the ra'; the things heard and existents, or with kasra; the attribute... al-Shinwani (2) His statement: (a complete perception) i.e., real, as indicated by his statement: (not) by way of imagination and supposition... al-Kastali... (3) The discussion on the attribute of hearing relates to it from three aspects: the first aspect: explanation of the meaning of hearing, the second aspect: explanation of the proof of the obligation of Allah, exalted is He, being described with hearing, and the third aspect: explanation of what it relates to. Hearing. The attribute of hearing. As for the discussion on the first aspect, we say: Know first that created hearing, with which man and others are described, is a power deposited in the nerve spread in the cavity of the ear canal, by which the animal perceives sounds in the usual way, and there is no prevention that it perceives other than sounds if Allah grants him that, as Moses perceived the speech of Allah – and it, as we know, is not with sound or letter. Then know that this meaning is impossible for Allah, exalted is He, and the investigators have interpreted Allah's hearing as: an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, exalted is He, and they mentioned that this attribute is additional to the attribute of knowledge, contrary to al-Ka'bi and some Mu'tazila who said that hearing and sight reduce to knowledge of things heard and seen, and the explanation of that will come at the statement of the author: 'And other than knowledge these are as established.' And its meaning is that the attribute of speech, the attribute of hearing, and the attribute of sight are other than the attribute of knowledge, and the sign that these attributes are other than knowledge is that they are additional to knowledge in the witnessed (world), which is man, and the principle is divergence in what is reported regarding Allah, exalted is He, so that every term has its meaning, and interpretation without proof and without need for interpretation is frivolity which must not be resorted to. Yes, it is obligatory to believe that Allah's knowledge is impossible for it to be hidden in any way, and that the matter is not as we are accustomed to that sight provides through observation clarity above knowledge; rather, all His attributes are perfect and complete, impossible for them what is of the marks of accidents of hiddenness, increase, decrease, etc., and even if the object is unified and the aspect is unified in type like disclosure in hearing, sight, and knowledge, there must be divergence, especially with absolute perfection, and the core of that and its reality is entrusted to Allah, exalted is He. And among the scholars is one who defined the attribute of hearing by saying: an attribute by which the thing is disclosed and becomes clear like knowledge, and we will explain this definition in the discussion on the attribute of sight.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of hearing (as-sam') of Allah and explains its nature, distinguishing it from human hearing and from the attribute of knowledge. It begins with the matn statement that hearing is an eternal attribute subsisting in Allah's essence, relating to things heard or to all existents, and that it perceives completely without imagination, supposition, sensory impression, or air conduction. The commentator then provides marginal notes clarifying points: 'perceives' can be read with fat-ha (meaning the attribute perceives) or kasra (the attribute is perceived? – unclear); 'complete perception' means real, as opposed to imaginative or suppositional. The commentator then divides the discussion into three aspects: meaning, proof, and what it relates to. For the first aspect (meaning), he explains created hearing as a power in the ear nerve, but notes that Allah's hearing is impossible to be like that. The investigators define Allah's hearing as an eternal attribute additional to knowledge, refuting al-Ka'bi and some Mu'tazila who reduce hearing and sight to knowledge. The sign that these attributes are distinct from knowledge is that in the human (witnessed) case, hearing and sight are additional to knowledge. The principle is that terms used for Allah retain their distinct meanings unless evidence forces interpretation. Interpretation without need is frivolity. The commentator then emphasizes that while Allah's knowledge is perfect and never hidden, His attributes are all perfect and free from the defects of created things. Even if the object and aspect of perception are unified in type, there is necessarily divergence due to absolute perfection. The true reality of these attributes is entrusted to Allah. The passage ends with an alternate definition: hearing is an attribute by which things are disclosed and become clear like knowledge, to be explained later.

Correction: The passage clarifies that hearing is an eternal attribute distinct from knowledge and not a sensory faculty; it perceives existents in a way befitting Allah's perfection.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the correct understanding of Allah's attribute of hearing (as-sam')?
A) It is a created power in the ear that perceives sounds.
B) It is an eternal attribute subsisting in Allah's essence, distinct from knowledge, perceiving existents without sensory means.
C) It is the same as Allah's knowledge of sounds.
D) It is a temporary attribute that Allah acquires when He hears.

Key Takeaway
Allah's hearing is an eternal, perfect attribute distinct from knowledge, and its true reality is known only to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your own hearing is a fragile, created faculty—it can be fooled, fail, or be blocked. When you truly internalise that Allah's hearing is eternal, perfect, and not limited by any sense, does it change how you think about the words you speak when you think no one is listening? Will you let that truth shape your speech today, starting now?
?
The passage calls interpreting Allah's attributes without proof 'frivolity'—a game. Have you ever tried to rationalise Allah's attributes to fit your own understanding, reducing them to what feels logical to you? What does that subtle arrogance reveal about your trust in the revelation and the scholars who preserved it?
?
If Allah's hearing is distinct from His knowledge, yet both are perfect and complete, then He hears you in a way that is beyond your comprehension—yet He hears you fully. When you feel unheard by people, do you truly turn to Allah with the confidence that His hearing is not like theirs? What would it take for you to live with that awareness every moment?

The Principle of Interpreting Divine Attributes
Explains the rule of understanding scriptural words about God without anthropomorphism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how to interpret scriptural descriptions of God without falling into anthropomorphism, based on the principle of al-mughayara and the prohibition of unwarranted ta'wil.

Core Idea
Scriptural words about God are not identical to human meanings but share a conceptual basis with necessary difference.

This lesson introduces a foundational rule for understanding divine attributes in Islamic theology. We start with the recognition that words like 'knowledge' and 'sight' are known from human experience, but when applied to God, they require the principle of difference (al-mughayara). The passage warns against interpretive tampering (ta'wil) without evidence and clarifies that God's attributes are perfect, free from the imperfections of created things.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once through, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: a principle, a prohibition, and an affirmation of divine perfection.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two key principles
The passage gives two main rules: (1) al-mughayara – words about God differ from their human analogues; (2) ta'wil without evidence is forbidden. Jot down how each is articulated.
3
Understand the perfection of divine attributes
Focus on the sentences describing God's attributes as perfect, lacking concealment, increase, or decrease. Reflect on how they differ from human attributes like sight, which can be partial.
This guards against thinking God's attributes are like ours.
4
Answer the checkpoint question
Use the quiz to test your grasp of the prohibition of ta'wil. The answer is directly from the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم الإلهيات على العلم في الشاهد وهو الإنسان، والأصل المغايرة فيما ورد في حق الله تعالى ليكونَ لكل لفظ معناه، والتأويل من غير دليل ومن غير حاجة إلى التأويل تلاعب يجب ألَّا يُصار إليه . نعم ، الواجب أن تعتقد أنَّ عِلم الله تعالى يستحيل عليه الخفاء بوجه من الوجوه، وأنَّ الأمر صفاته تامة ليس على ما يعهد لنا مِن أنَّ البصر يفيد بالمشاهدة وضوحاً فوق العلم، بل جميع كاملة يستحيل عليها ما كان مِن سِماتِ الحوادثِ من الخفاء، والزيادة، والنقص.. إلى غير ذلك ، وإنْ اتَّحد المُتعلّق وكانت الجهة متحدة بالنّوع كالانكشاف في السمع والبصر والعلم، لكن لا بدَّ مِن تغاير ، خصوصاً مع الكمال المطلق ، وكنه ذلك وحقيقته يُفَوَّض عِلْمُه إلى الله تعالى .
The section on divine matters: (knowledge) is based on knowledge in the witnessed (world), which is man, and the principle is that there is a difference in what is reported concerning God Almighty, so that every word has its meaning. Interpretive shift (ta'wil) without evidence and without need for interpretive shift is tampering which must not be resorted to. Yes, it is obligatory to believe that knowledge of God Almighty makes concealment impossible in any way, and that the matter—His attributes are perfect—is not as we are accustomed to: that sight provides by observation clarity beyond knowledge; rather, all (attributes) are perfect, impossible for them to be what are marks of contingent things: of concealment, increase, decrease, etc. And if the object is one and the aspect is uniform in type—like disclosure in hearing, sight, and knowledge—still there must be distinction, especially with absolute perfection; and the essence of that and its reality, knowledge of it is delegated to God Almighty.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage defines the proper method for interpreting scriptural descriptions of God. It begins by stating that we take words in their ordinary sense (as understood from human experience) but with the principle that there is a fundamental difference (al-mughayara) between God and His creation. Interpretive shift (ta'wil) without proof is prohibited as tampering. Then it explains that we must believe God's attributes—like hearing and sight—are perfect and free from any imperfection found in creatures (e.g., concealment, increase, decrease). Even if the object of knowledge is the same, the mode of disclosure differs for God. The ultimate reality of how He hears and sees is delegated to God.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because God's attributes are described with human words, they are exactly like human attributes.
Correction: The passage establishes al-mughayara: a fundamental difference. Human attributes are imperfect; divine attributes are perfect and free from limitations.

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on interpretive shift (ta'wil) of divine attributes without evidence or need?
A) Permissible as a way to protect God's transcendence
B) Obligatory to avoid anthropomorphism
C) It is tampering and must not be resorted to
D) Recommended but not required

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes are known through scriptural words, but they are not identical to human attributes; we affirm their perfection and delegate the true reality to Allah.

Transmitted Proofs for God's Hearing
Cites transmitted proofs for God's hearing from Quran, Sunnah, and consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the primary evidence for God's attribute of hearing comes from transmitted sources (Quran, Sunnah, consensus), while rational arguments serve as support.

Core Idea
God's hearing is proven by revelation, not reason alone.

In this passage, the author builds on a previous discussion about God's speech to establish a key principle: the divine attributes of hearing, sight, and speech are ultimately proven by transmitted evidence (naql). While reason can confirm these attributes, the foundational proof lies in the Book, the Sunnah, and scholarly consensus. The passage then provides a wealth of Quranic verses and a hadith to demonstrate that God is described as 'Hearing' in revelation itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: the author first states the principle, then gives the three transmitted sources, and finally provides examples from the Quran and Sunnah.
2
Identify the three transmitted sources
The passage explicitly mentions three sources for transmitted proof: the Quran (al-Kitab), the Sunnah, and consensus (ijma'). Under each, examples are given. Note that the Quran examples are numerous and the hadith is partially quoted.
The passage ends abruptly; the hadith and conclusion are incomplete.
3
Understand the role of rational proof
The author states that rational proof 'strengthens and confirms' the transmitted proof. Reflect on why transmitted proof is considered primary—because it directly conveys God's revelation about Himself.
4
Reflect on the Quranic verses
Read each Quranic verse cited. Notice that they all explicitly attribute hearing to God, using the word 'sameea' (hearing) or related verbs. This establishes hearing as a divine attribute beyond doubt.
5
Consider the misconception
The source mentions a common misunderstanding: thinking reason alone suffices. The passage clarifies that transmitted evidence is the primary and authoritative source. This helps avoid overreliance on rational speculation.

As for the discussion on the proof of the obligation of describing God Almighty with the attribute of hearing: we have already alerted you in the proof of the attribute of speech for Him, may He be exalted, that what is considered by the verifying theologians in proving these attributes—hearing, sight, and speech—is the transmitted proof (al-dalil al-naqli), and that the rational proof strengthens and confirms the transmitted proof. The proof of establishing the attribute of hearing from transmission: the Book, the Sunnah, and the consensus of those whose consensus is counted among the scholars of this community. As for the Book: there are many verses, among them His saying, 'Indeed, Allah has heard the statement of her who argues with you concerning her husband and complains to Allah. And Allah hears your dialogue; indeed, Allah is Hearing and Seeing' (58:1), and among them His saying, 'There is nothing like unto Him, and He is the Hearing, the Seeing' (42:11), and His saying, 'Go with Our signs; indeed, We are with you, listening' (26:15), and His saying, 'Or do they think that We hear not their secrets and their private conversations?' (43:80), and His saying, 'Indeed, I am with you, hearing and seeing' (20:46), and His saying, 'Allah has certainly heard the speech of those who said, "Indeed, Allah is poor while we are rich." We will record what they said and their killing of the prophets without right. And We will say, "Taste the punishment of the Burning Fire."' (3:181). As for the Sunnah: there are hadiths, among them his saying—when his Companions raised their voices in supplication—'O people, be gentle with yourselves, for you are not calling upon a deaf or an absent one; He is with you, Hearing and Near.' And we have known that=

Commentary
This passage argues that proof from revelation (naql) is the primary source for affirming the divine attributes of hearing, sight, and speech. The author references a previous discussion about speech, stating that verified theologians (muhaqqiqun) hold that these attributes are proven by transmitted evidence (kitab, sunna, ijma'), while rational proof serves as support and confirmation. The passage then lists Quranic verses that explicitly attribute hearing to God—each verse using the name 'al-Sami' (the All-Hearing) or a verb form. It also provides a hadith where the Prophet teaches that God hears prayers, unlike a deaf or absent being. The section is cut off, but the intent is clear: the transmitted sources establish that hearing is an attribute of God.

Allah hears even the private conversations of those who speak against Him (43:80). If you knew that Allah hears every word you say, including your complaints and gossip, what specific change will you make in your speech starting tomorrow?

Rational Proof for God's Hearing
Presents the rational argument for God's hearing with Abrahamic support.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that God must possess hearing because its absence would imply imperfection, and how this proof is supported by the example of Prophet Abraham.

Core Idea
God's hearing is rationally necessary; lacking it would be a deficiency, impossible for God.

This lesson presents the rational proof for God's attribute of hearing. Before diving into the proof, the author reaffirms the principle that scriptural words about God's attributes are understood in their primary meanings unless evidence requires otherwise. Then he lays out the logical argument: hearing is a perfection, so denying it would imply a deficiency in God, which is impossible. The lesson also shows how Prophet Abraham used this same reasoning against his father's idol worship.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the three parts: hermeneutical principle, rational proof, and Abrahamic example.
The proof is not just a statement; it's a logical conditional.
2
Analyze the logical structure
Identify the premises: (1) hearing is a perfection, (2) its absence would be a deficiency, (3) deficiency is impossible for God. Conclusion: God must have hearing.
This is a demonstration (istidlal) not a direct proof.
3
Examine the Abrahamic example
See how Abraham uses the same reasoning: the idol lacks hearing, so it is deficient and unworthy of worship. This shows the rational proof is rooted in scripture.
The example also shows that the proof is not solely rational but reinforced by revelation.
4
Reflect on the connection between reason and revelation
Note that the author does not pit reason against scripture. Rather, reason confirms what scripture teaches about God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٦ - صفة السمع، تعريفها، وبيان تعلقها ألفاظ الشرع يجب كمال، أن تُصرَف إلى معانيها التي تَسبِقُ منها إلى الأفهام، ما لم يجب صرفها عنها لدليل، كما علمنا أنه لا استحالة في كونه سبحانه سميعاً بصيرا على المعنى الذي يليق بذاته العليَّة ، لا على المعنى الذي نجده في أنفسنا ، فلا معنی للتَّحَكُّم بإنكارِ ما فهمه مِن القرآن أهل الإجماع من صحابة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ومن تبعهم بإحسان. وأما الدليل العقلي على أنَّ الله تعالى متصف بالسمع؛ فتقريره أن نقول: إِنَّ السمع والسميع أكمل ممن لا يسمع ، فلو لم يتصف اللهُ بالسَّمع لزم النَّقْصُ في بالسمع لزم النَّقْصُ في حقه ، والنقص عليه تعالى محال ، فاستحال ما أدى إليه وهو عدم اتصافه بالسمع ، فثبت نقيضه وهو متصف بالسَّمع ، وقد أراد إبراهيم الخليل صلوات الله وسلامه عليه تقرير هذا الدليل لأبيه فيما حكاه الله تعالى عنه بقوله: (يَتأَبَتِ لِمَ تَعْبُدُ مَا لَا يَسْمَعُ وَلَا يُبْصِرُ وَلَا يُغْنِي عَنكَ شَيْئًا ﴾ [مريم : ٤٢] ، وبرهانُ أنه أرادَ ذلك ؛ أَنَّا نعلم أنه لو كان لا يعتقد أنَّ عدم السمع نقص لخافَ أنْ يقلبوا عليه الدليل في معبوده فيقولوا له: وأنت أيضا تعبد ما لا يسمع ولا يبصر ، تعالى الله عما يقول المبطلون علوا كبيرًا !
6 - The attribute of hearing: its definition and explanation of its relation. The words of the Law must be taken, with perfection, to their meanings that come first to minds, unless it becomes necessary to turn them away due to evidence, as we have known that there is no impossibility in His, exalted, being Hearing and Seeing in the sense befitting His supreme essence, not in the sense we find in ourselves. So there is no basis for arbitrary rejection of what the people of consensus—the Companions of the Messenger of God, peace and blessings be upon him, and those who followed them in excellence—understood from the Qur'an. As for the rational proof that God Almighty is described with hearing: its formulation is that we say: 'Hearing and the one who hears are more perfect than one who does not hear. So if God were not described with hearing, deficiency would follow—deficiency would follow regarding Him—and deficiency is impossible for Him. Thus, what leads to it, which is His not being described with hearing, is impossible; so its opposite is established, which is that He is described with hearing.' And Abraham, the Friend, peace be upon him, intended to establish this proof to his father, as God Almighty related of him saying: 'O my father, why do you worship that which does not hear and does not see and cannot avail you in anything?' (19:42). And the proof that he intended that is that we know that if he did not believe that lack of hearing is a deficiency, he would have feared that they might turn the argument against him regarding his worshipped object and say to him, 'And you also worship that which does not hear and does not see.' Exalted is God above what the falsifiers say, with great exaltation!
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage begins with a heading indicating the section on the attribute of hearing, its definition, and the explanation of its relation. It first restates the hermeneutical principle: Scriptural words are taken in their primary meanings unless evidence requires departure; there is no impossibility in God being Hearing and Seeing in a way befitting His essence, not ours. Arbitrary denial is rejected. Then it presents the rational proof: Hearing and the hearer are more perfect (akmal) than the one who does not hear. If God were not hearing, deficiency would follow—but deficiency is impossible for God. Therefore, the absence of hearing is impossible, so its opposite—that He has hearing—is established. The passage reinforces this with the example of Abraham (Ibrahim) arguing against his father's idol worship: Abraham argued that the idol cannot hear, implying that lack of hearing is a deficiency; if he did not believe that, he would have feared the argument turned against him. Thus, the rational proof is sound.

أكمل

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the rational proof is independent of revelation and could be used without scripture.
Correction: The passage shows the proof is intertwined with scripture: it cites Abraham's argument and upholds the principle that scriptural meanings are primary. Reason and revelation are complementary.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the rational proof for God's hearing?
A) God must be all-hearing because of His power
B) God must have hearing because its absence would imply deficiency, which is impossible for Him
C) God's hearing is proven only by revelation, not reason
D) God's hearing is metaphorical and not real

Key Takeaway
God's hearing is rationally necessary because its absence would imply a deficiency, and deficiency is impossible for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Two Scholarly Views on the Object of God's Hearing
Presents two scholarly opinions on the object of God's hearing and their reconciliation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main scholarly opinions on what the divine attribute of hearing relates to, and how they can be reconciled to affirm that God hears all existents.

Core Idea
God's hearing encompasses all existents, not just sounds.

Scholars have differed on the precise scope of God's attribute of hearing. Some hold that it relates to all existent things, while others restrict it to audible things. However, a careful reading shows that these views can be reconciled, affirming that God hears everything in a manner befitting His majesty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the two opinions and the author's commentary.
The passage ends abruptly; focus on the clear part.
2
Identify the two opinions
Notice that al-Sanusi says hearing relates to all existents, while al-Sa'd says it relates to 'audible things'.
3
Explore the two interpretations of al-Sa'd's view
Understand that 'audible things' could mean only sounds (narrower) or all existents (broader, aligning with al-Sanusi).
The author suggests the broader interpretation reconciles the views.
4
Reflect on the reconciliation
Realize that both views can be harmonized if the object of hearing is considered one: God hears all existents.
5
Note the incomplete conclusion
The passage ends by distinguishing disclosure by hearing from disclosure by sight, but the full argument is missing.
This does not affect the main point about the scope of hearing.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكلام على تعلق صفة السَّمع فنقول : اعلم أن عبارة العلماء تختلف في بيان ما تتعلق به صفة السمع ؛ فمنهم من يقول : إنها تتعلق بجميع الموجودات، سواء في ذلك القديم منها: كذاته وصفاته، والحادث، وسواء في ذلك الأصوات وغيرها، وهذه عبارة السنوسي في عقيدته ، وهو الذي دَرَجَ عليه المصنف ، حيث يقول فيما يأتي: «وَكُلَّ مَوجودٍ أَنِطٌ للسَّمع حقنا، به)) ومنهم من يقول : إن صفة السمع تتعلق بالمسموعات ، وهذه عبارة السعد ، وهذه العبارة الأخيرة تحتمل وجهين أما أحدهما فأن يكون مراده بالمسموعات التي تتعلق بها صفة السمع المسموعات في وهي الأصواتُ، فيكون مخالفًا لأصحاب العبارة الأولى، ويكون مُتعلّق صفة السمع عنده أخصَّ مِن مُتعلّقها عندهم، وأما ثانيهما: فأن يكون مراده بالمسموعات التي تتعلق بها صفة السمع المسموعات في حقه تعالى، وهي الموجودات عامة ، أصواتا كانت أو غير أصوات، فيكون موافقا لأصحاب العبارة الأولى ، ويكون مُتعلّق كلا من الأصوات والذوات: بمعنى: أنَّ يسمع صفة السمع عند الفريقين واحدا ؛ فالله كلا منها منكشف له بسمعه . ويجب لگے اعتقاد أنَّ الانكشاف بالسمع غير الانكشاف بالبصر ، وأنَّ كلا منهما غير الانكشاف=
And as for the discussion on the relation (ta'alluq) of the attribute of hearing, we say: Know that the expressions of the scholars differ in explaining what the attribute of hearing relates to. Among them is one who says: It relates to all existents, equally including the eternal among them like His essence and attributes, and the contingent, and equally including sounds and others. This is the expression of al-Sanusi in his creed, and it is what the author followed, where he says in what is to come: 'And every existent is referred to hearing in reality, by it.' And among them is one who says: The attribute of hearing relates to audible things (al-masmu'at), and this is the expression of al-Sa'd. And this latter expression admits two possible interpretations: first, that by 'audible things' he means the audibles which are sounds, thus differing from the holders of the first expression, and the object of hearing according to him is more specific than their object; second, that by 'audible things' he means what is audible in relation to Him, which are existents generally, whether sounds or not, thus agreeing with the holders of the first expression, and the object of both sounds and essences is one: meaning that God hears each of them, the attribute of hearing is one for both parties, so God—each of them is disclosed to Him by His hearing. And it is obligatory for you to believe that disclosure by hearing is other than disclosure by sight, and that each of them is other than disclosure (the sentence ends here).
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage explains the different scholarly positions on the scope of God's hearing. Some scholars say hearing relates to all existent things—whether eternal (God's own essence and attributes) or contingent, and whether sounds or not. This is the view of Sanusi and the author of the poem. Others, like al-Sa'd, say hearing relates to 'audible things' (masmu'at). This latter view can be interpreted in two ways: either it means only sounds (making it narrower) or it means all existents (making it equivalent to the first view). The author notes that both views can be reconciled if the object of hearing is considered one: God hears everything. He then affirms that the mode of disclosure (inkishaf) through hearing differs from that through sight, and each is distinct—though the text is cut off before fully concluding.

مسموعات
السنوسي
السعد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God only hears sounds like humans do.
Correction: Most scholars affirm that God's hearing encompasses all existents, including non-sounds.

Checkpoint

D) Only contingent beings

Key Takeaway
The attribute of God's hearing relates to all existents, though scholars express this differently; the key is that God hears everything in a way distinct from sight.

The author insists that God's hearing and sight are distinct disclosures. How does knowing that God perceives your condition in multiple, unique ways affect your honesty with Him in your du‘a and repentance?

How We Know God Hears: Definition and Proof
Sanusi explains the hermeneutic principle and rational proof for God's hearing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the divine attribute of hearing is defined in a manner befitting God and the rational proof that establishes it.

Core Idea
God's hearing is perfect; perfection demands it.

Before we can affirm that God hears, we need a clear principle: words about God must be understood in their true meanings, but without anthropomorphism. This lesson shows how Sanusi grounds the attribute of hearing in both hermeneutics and rational necessity, using a proof from perfection and a Qur’anic example from Prophet Abraham.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Hermeneutic Principle
Learn that words of the Law are taken in their customary meanings unless evidence forces a different interpretation. Apply this to 'hearing' to avoid anthropomorphism.
This prevents both denial and literalist misunderstanding.
2
Learn the Rational Proof
Follow the argument: hearing and the one who hears are more perfect than not hearing; God must be perfect; therefore, God possesses hearing.
This is a standard kalam perfection argument.
3
See the Qur’anic Support
Examine Abraham's argument against his father, which assumes that the inability to hear is a deficiency, reinforcing the rational proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٦ - صفة السمع، تعريفها، وبيان تعلقها
ألفاظ الشرع يجب كمال، أن تُصرَف إلى معانيها التي تَسبِقُ منها إلى الأفهام، ما لم يجب صرفها عنها لدليل، كما علمنا أنه لا استحالة في كونه سبحانه سميعاً بصيرا على المعنى الذي يليق بذاته العليَّة ، لا على المعنى الذي نجده في أنفسنا ، فلا معنی للتَّحَكُّم بإنكارِ ما فهمه مِن القرآن أهل الإجماع من صحابة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ومن تبعهم بإحسان. وأما الدليل العقلي على أنَّ الله تعالى متصف بالسمع؛ فتقريره أن نقول: إِنَّ السمع والسميع أكمل ممن لا يسمع ، فلو لم يتصف اللهُ بالسَّمع لزم النَّقْصُ في بالسمع لزم النَّقْصُ في حقه ، والنقص عليه تعالى محال ، فاستحال ما أدى إليه وهو عدم اتصافه بالسمع ، فثبت نقيضه وهو متصف بالسَّمع ، وقد أراد إبراهيم الخليل صلوات الله وسلامه عليه تقرير هذا الدليل لأبيه فيما حكاه الله تعالى عنه بقوله: (يَتأَبَتِ لِمَ تَعْبُدُ مَا لَا يَسْمَعُ وَلَا يُبْصِرُ وَلَا يُغْنِي عَنكَ شَيْئًا ﴾ [مريم : ٤٢] ، وبرهانُ أنه أرادَ ذلك ؛ أَنَّا نعلم أنه لو كان لا يعتقد أنَّ عدم السمع نقص لخافَ أنْ [؟] أنه تعالى يسمع ولا يبصر ، تعالى الله [؟] يقلبوا عليه الدليل في معبوده فيقولوا له: وأنت أيضا تعبد ما لا [؟] عما يقول المبطلون علوا كبيرًا ! وأما الكلام على تعلق صفة السَّمع فنقول : اعلم أن عبارة العلماء تختلف في بيان ما تتعلق به صفة السمع ؛ فمنهم من يقول : إنها تتعلق بجميع الموجودات، سواء في ذلك القديم منها: كذاته وصفاته، والحادث، وسواء في ذلك الأصوات وغيرها، وهذه عبارة السنوسي في عقيدته ، وهو الذي دَرَجَ عليه المصنف ، حيث يقول فيما يأتي: «وَكُلَّ مَوجودٍ أَنِطٌ للسَّمع حقنا، به)) ومنهم من يقول : إن صفة السمع تتعلق بالمسموعات ، وهذه عبارة السعد ، وهذه العبارة الأخيرة تحتمل وجهين أما أحدهما فأن يكون مراده بالمسموعات التي تتعلق بها صفة السمع المسموعات في وهي الأصواتُ، فيكون مخالفًا لأصحاب العبارة الأولى، ويكون مُتعلّق صفة السمع عنده أخصَّ مِن مُتعلّقها عندهم، وأما ثانيهما: فأن يكون مراده بالمسموعات التي تتعلق بها صفة السمع المسموعات في حقه تعالى، وهي الموجودات عامة ، أصواتا كانت أو غير أصوات، فيكون موافقا لأصحاب العبارة الأولى ، ويكون مُتعلّق كلا من الأصوات والذوات: بمعنى: أنَّ يسمع صفة السمع عند الفريقين واحدا ؛ فالله كلا منها منكشف له بسمعه . ويجب لگے اعتقاد أنَّ الانكشاف بالسمع غير الانكشاف بالبصر ، وأنَّ كلا منهما غير الانكشاف=
6 – The attribute of hearing (al-sam‘), its definition, and an explanation of its relation (ta‘alluq). The words of the Law must be directed to their meanings that precede from them to the understandings, unless there is evidence requiring to turn them away from them, as we know that there is no impossibility in His being All-Hearing, All-Seeing according to the meaning that befits His exalted essence, not according to the meaning we find in ourselves. So there is no meaning for arbitrary denial of what the people of consensus from the Companions of the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) and those who followed them in goodness understood from the Qur’an. As for the rational proof (al-dalīl al-‘aqlī) that God is characterized by hearing, its formulation is that we say: hearing and the one who hears are more perfect than one who does not hear. So if God were not characterized by hearing, then deficiency would be necessary in His right, and deficiency upon Him is impossible. So what leads to it – which is His not being characterized by hearing – is impossible, and its opposite is established: that He is characterized by hearing. Abraham the friend (peace and blessings be upon him) intended to present this proof to his father, according to what God narrated about him in His saying: 'O my father, why do you worship that which does not hear and does not see and does not avail you anything?' [Maryam: 42]. The proof that he intended that is that we know that if he did not believe that absence of hearing is a deficiency, he would have feared that they would turn the proof against him concerning his object of worship, saying: 'You also worship that which does not hear or see, exalted is God above what the wrongdoers say, a great exaltation!' As for the discussion on the relation of the attribute of hearing, we say: know that the expressions of the scholars differ in explaining what the attribute of hearing attaches to. Some of them say that it attaches to all existents, equally the eternal among them – like His essence and attributes – and the temporal, and equally sounds and other things. This is the expression of al-Sanūsī in his creed, and it is what the author followed, as he says in what comes: 'And every existent is [؟] for hearing [؟].' And some of them say that the attribute of hearing attaches to audible things (al-masmū‘āt). This is the expression of al-Sa‘d. This latter expression has two aspects: first, that he intends by audible things that the attribute of hearing attaches to, sounds, and thus he opposes the first expression, and the object of the attribute of hearing is more specific according to him. Second, that he intends by audible things that are in His right, i.e., existents generally, sounds or non-sounds, and thus he agrees with the first expression. The object of each of sounds and essences is that the attribute of hearing is one for both groups: God [؟] each of them is disclosed to Him through His hearing. And it is necessary for you to believe that disclosure (inkishāf) by hearing is other than disclosure by sight, and that each of them is other than disclosure=
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is a theological discussion on the divine attribute of hearing (al-sam‘). It begins with a hermeneutic principle: words of the Law must be understood in their customary meanings unless there is evidence to the contrary. The attribute of hearing is affirmed in a manner befitting God, not analogous to human hearing. The rational proof (dalīl ‘aqlī) is then presented: hearing and the one who hears are more perfect than the opposite; since God is perfect, He must possess hearing; otherwise deficiency would follow, which is impossible. The proof is supported by citing Abraham’s argument against his father. The author then discusses the object (ta‘alluq) of hearing, noting two scholarly views: one (al-Sanūsī) that it attaches to all existents, another (al-Sa‘d) that it attaches to audible things specifically. The latter view is analyzed as possibly meaning sounds only (narrower) or all existents (broader), thus agreeing with the first. The passage concludes by emphasizing that the disclosure by hearing is distinct from disclosure by sight and from disclosure by knowledge.

Key Terms
السمع (al-sam‘)
الدليل العقلي (al-dalīl al-‘aqlī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's hearing is like human hearing with ears and sound waves.
Correction: The passage clarifies that God's hearing is in a manner befitting His essence, not like our hearing.

Checkpoint
According to the rational proof presented in this passage, why must God be characterized by hearing?
A) Because the Qur’an explicitly states it and we must follow scripture.
B) Because hearing is more perfect than not hearing, and God must be perfect.
C) Because Abraham used this proof, and his proof is infallible.
D) Because the scholars of consensus agreed on it.

Key Takeaway
God's hearing is a necessary perfection, affirmed by rational argument and supported by scripture.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon God in du'a, do you truly believe He hears you in a way befitting His majesty, not like a human ear? How would your du'a change if you internalized this?
?
Abraham argued that inability to hear is a deficiency. Do you live as though God hears every word and thought? What would you need to stop saying or thinking if you truly believed that?
?
If you ever fear that affirming God's attributes like hearing might make Him seem too 'human', how can the principle of 'in a way befitting His essence' free you from that fear and bring you closer to Him?

What Does God Hear? The Ta‘alluq of Hearing
Sanusi presents differing views on what the attribute of hearing attaches to.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the differing scholarly views on what the attribute of hearing attaches to and how they can be reconciled.

Core Idea
Scholars agree that God hears all existents, but differ in expression.

After establishing that God hears, the next question is: what does He hear? Does His hearing encompass all things, or only sounds? Sanusi presents two views from scholars, including his own, and shows how the differences may be more linguistic than substantive.

Guided Path
1
Learn the First View
Al-Sanūsī says hearing attaches to all existents, both eternal and temporal, sounds and non-sounds.
This is the view followed by the author.
2
Learn the Second View
Al-Sa‘d says hearing attaches to audible things, but this phrase has two possible interpretations.
3
Reconcile the Views
See how the two interpretations of 'audible things' either agree or disagree with the first view.
The broader interpretation matches the first view.
4
Understand Distinct Disclosure
Regardless of scope, the disclosure by hearing is different from that by sight or knowledge.
This is a necessary belief to avoid conflating attributes.
