٦ - صفة السمع، تعريفها، وبيان تعلقها
ألفاظ الشرع يجب كمال، أن تُصرَف إلى معانيها التي تَسبِقُ منها إلى الأفهام، ما لم يجب صرفها عنها لدليل، كما علمنا أنه لا استحالة في كونه سبحانه سميعاً بصيرا على المعنى الذي يليق بذاته العليَّة ، لا على المعنى الذي نجده في أنفسنا ، فلا معنی للتَّحَكُّم بإنكارِ ما فهمه مِن القرآن أهل الإجماع من صحابة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ومن تبعهم بإحسان. وأما الدليل العقلي على أنَّ الله تعالى متصف بالسمع؛ فتقريره أن نقول: إِنَّ السمع والسميع أكمل ممن لا يسمع ، فلو لم يتصف اللهُ بالسَّمع لزم النَّقْصُ في بالسمع لزم النَّقْصُ في حقه ، والنقص عليه تعالى محال ، فاستحال ما أدى إليه وهو عدم اتصافه بالسمع ، فثبت نقيضه وهو متصف بالسَّمع ، وقد أراد إبراهيم الخليل صلوات الله وسلامه عليه تقرير هذا الدليل لأبيه فيما حكاه الله تعالى عنه بقوله: (يَتأَبَتِ لِمَ تَعْبُدُ مَا لَا يَسْمَعُ وَلَا يُبْصِرُ وَلَا يُغْنِي عَنكَ شَيْئًا ﴾ [مريم : ٤٢] ، وبرهانُ أنه أرادَ ذلك ؛ أَنَّا نعلم أنه لو كان لا يعتقد أنَّ عدم السمع نقص لخافَ أنْ [؟] أنه تعالى يسمع ولا يبصر ، تعالى الله [؟] يقلبوا عليه الدليل في معبوده فيقولوا له: وأنت أيضا تعبد ما لا [؟] عما يقول المبطلون علوا كبيرًا ! وأما الكلام على تعلق صفة السَّمع فنقول : اعلم أن عبارة العلماء تختلف في بيان ما تتعلق به صفة السمع ؛ فمنهم من يقول : إنها تتعلق بجميع الموجودات، سواء في ذلك القديم منها: كذاته وصفاته، والحادث، وسواء في ذلك الأصوات وغيرها، وهذه عبارة السنوسي في عقيدته ، وهو الذي دَرَجَ عليه المصنف ، حيث يقول فيما يأتي: «وَكُلَّ مَوجودٍ أَنِطٌ للسَّمع حقنا، به)) ومنهم من يقول : إن صفة السمع تتعلق بالمسموعات ، وهذه عبارة السعد ، وهذه العبارة الأخيرة تحتمل وجهين أما أحدهما فأن يكون مراده بالمسموعات التي تتعلق بها صفة السمع المسموعات في وهي الأصواتُ، فيكون مخالفًا لأصحاب العبارة الأولى، ويكون مُتعلّق صفة السمع عنده أخصَّ مِن مُتعلّقها عندهم، وأما ثانيهما: فأن يكون مراده بالمسموعات التي تتعلق بها صفة السمع المسموعات في حقه تعالى، وهي الموجودات عامة ، أصواتا كانت أو غير أصوات، فيكون موافقا لأصحاب العبارة الأولى ، ويكون مُتعلّق كلا من الأصوات والذوات: بمعنى: أنَّ يسمع صفة السمع عند الفريقين واحدا ؛ فالله كلا منها منكشف له بسمعه . ويجب لگے اعتقاد أنَّ الانكشاف بالسمع غير الانكشاف بالبصر ، وأنَّ كلا منهما غير الانكشاف=
6 – The attribute of hearing (al-sam‘), its definition, and an explanation of its relation (ta‘alluq). The words of the Law must be directed to their meanings that precede from them to the understandings, unless there is evidence requiring to turn them away from them, as we know that there is no impossibility in His being All-Hearing, All-Seeing according to the meaning that befits His exalted essence, not according to the meaning we find in ourselves. So there is no meaning for arbitrary denial of what the people of consensus from the Companions of the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) and those who followed them in goodness understood from the Qur’an. As for the rational proof (al-dalīl al-‘aqlī) that God is characterized by hearing, its formulation is that we say: hearing and the one who hears are more perfect than one who does not hear. So if God were not characterized by hearing, then deficiency would be necessary in His right, and deficiency upon Him is impossible. So what leads to it – which is His not being characterized by hearing – is impossible, and its opposite is established: that He is characterized by hearing. Abraham the friend (peace and blessings be upon him) intended to present this proof to his father, according to what God narrated about him in His saying: 'O my father, why do you worship that which does not hear and does not see and does not avail you anything?' [Maryam: 42]. The proof that he intended that is that we know that if he did not believe that absence of hearing is a deficiency, he would have feared that they would turn the proof against him concerning his object of worship, saying: 'You also worship that which does not hear or see, exalted is God above what the wrongdoers say, a great exaltation!' As for the discussion on the relation of the attribute of hearing, we say: know that the expressions of the scholars differ in explaining what the attribute of hearing attaches to. Some of them say that it attaches to all existents, equally the eternal among them – like His essence and attributes – and the temporal, and equally sounds and other things. This is the expression of al-Sanūsī in his creed, and it is what the author followed, as he says in what comes: 'And every existent is [؟] for hearing [؟].' And some of them say that the attribute of hearing attaches to audible things (al-masmū‘āt). This is the expression of al-Sa‘d. This latter expression has two aspects: first, that he intends by audible things that the attribute of hearing attaches to, sounds, and thus he opposes the first expression, and the object of the attribute of hearing is more specific according to him. Second, that he intends by audible things that are in His right, i.e., existents generally, sounds or non-sounds, and thus he agrees with the first expression. The object of each of sounds and essences is that the attribute of hearing is one for both groups: God [؟] each of them is disclosed to Him through His hearing. And it is necessary for you to believe that disclosure (inkishāf) by hearing is other than disclosure by sight, and that each of them is other than disclosure=
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is a theological discussion on the divine attribute of hearing (al-sam‘). It begins with a hermeneutic principle: words of the Law must be understood in their customary meanings unless there is evidence to the contrary. The attribute of hearing is affirmed in a manner befitting God, not analogous to human hearing. The rational proof (dalīl ‘aqlī) is then presented: hearing and the one who hears are more perfect than the opposite; since God is perfect, He must possess hearing; otherwise deficiency would follow, which is impossible. The proof is supported by citing Abraham’s argument against his father. The author then discusses the object (ta‘alluq) of hearing, noting two scholarly views: one (al-Sanūsī) that it attaches to all existents, another (al-Sa‘d) that it attaches to audible things specifically. The latter view is analyzed as possibly meaning sounds only (narrower) or all existents (broader), thus agreeing with the first. The passage concludes by emphasizing that the disclosure by hearing is distinct from disclosure by sight and from disclosure by knowledge.

Key Terms
التعلق (al-ta‘alluq)
المسموعات (al-masmū‘āt)
الموجودات (al-mawjūdāt)
الانكشاف (al-inkishāf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God only hears sounds, like humans.
Correction: Most scholars hold that God hears all existents, not just sounds.

Checkpoint
According to al-Sanūsī’s view as presented in this passage, what does the attribute of hearing attach to?
A) Only sounds
B) Only living beings
C) All existents, eternal and temporal
D) Only human prayers

Key Takeaway
The divine attribute of hearing encompasses all existents, whether sounds or otherwise, as a unique form of disclosure distinct from sight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God hears every existent—your whispered doubts, your silent prayers, your unspoken grievances—how would your internal dialogue change today?
?
The scholars debated whether God hears sounds only or all existents. But the reality is that nothing escapes His hearing. What is one thing you have been hiding in your heart that you need to bring before His hearing now?
?
Disclosure by hearing is distinct from sight and knowledge. God hears you in a way unique to His attribute. How often do you live as if your private words are unheard? What will you do differently to live in that reality?

The Divine Attribute of Sight: Definition and Aspects
Sanusi defines the divine attribute of sight, outlines three aspects of discussion, and begins analyzing the first aspect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the divine attribute of sight as defined by Sunni theologians, its distinction from human sight, and the three aspects of discussion about it.

Core Idea
Divine sight is an eternal, perfect disclosure of existents, unlike human vision.

This passage introduces the divine attribute of sight (al-baṣar) as the seventh attribute. It explains that sight is an eternal attribute that perceives existents completely, not through physical means. The author outlines three aspects to be discussed: the meaning, the proof, and its relation. Here we focus on the first aspect: defining divine sight and distinguishing it from human sight.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Read the opening statement: sight is the seventh attribute, similar to hearing in necessity. Note the preliminary definition: an eternal attribute relating to visible things or existents, perceiving perfectly without imagination or sense.
Notice the phrase 'not by way of imagination or illusion' – this distinguishes divine sight from human visual imagination.
2
Identify the three aspects
The author announces three aspects of discussion: meaning of sight, proof of its existence, and what it relates to. This structure organizes the upcoming analysis.
We will only cover the first aspect in this passage.
3
Analyze the first definition
The verifying theologians define divine sight as 'an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence.' Consider why this definition is minimal and how it avoids anthropomorphism.
This definition emphasizes eternality and essentiality, not physicality.
4
Examine the second definition
Another definition: 'Sight is an attribute by which a thing is disclosed, like knowledge.' Follow the analysis of its components: genus (attribute), first differentia (by which it is disclosed), second differentia (the thing = existent), and simile (like knowledge).
Notice that this definition still includes hearing; the author discusses why this is acceptable given our limited knowledge.

[ ٧ - صفة البصر، تعريفها، وبيان تعلقها] (ثُمَّ البَصَر) سابعتُها ، فهو مِثلُ مَا ذُكِرَ في وجوب الانصاف به. وهو : صفةٌ أزليَّة تتعلّق بالمبصرات ، أو بالموجودات ، فتدرك إدراكًا تاما ، لا على طريق التخيل والتّوهُم ، ولا على طريق تأثر حاسة ووصولِ شُعاع (۱). = أَنَّ بالعلم ، ولكل واحد منها حقيقة نفوّض علمها الله تعالى، وليس الأمر على ما نعهده من البصر يفيد بالمشاهدة وضوحًا فوق العلم، بل جميع صفاته تامة كاملة يستحيل عليه الخفاء والزيادة والنقص إلى غير ذلك مما قدمنا الإشارة إليه، وسيأتي لنا ـ عند الكلام على تعلق صفة البصر - ذكرُ أنواع تعلق كلّ منهما بالموجودات . محي (۱) الكلام في صفة البصر به يتعلق بها من ثلاث جهات الجهة الأولى: في معنى البصر ، والجهة الثانية: في بيان الدليل على ثبوت صفة البصر الله تعالى، والجهة الثالثة : في بيان ما تتعلق به صفة البصر وأنواع تعلّق البصر والسمع. أمَّا الكلام على الجهة الأولى فنقول : اعلم أوّلاً أنَّ البصر الحادث الذي يوصف به الإنسانُ وغيره من أنواع الحيوان هو : قوَّة مخلوقة في العصبتين المجعولتين المتلاقيتين تلاقي دالين ظهر إحداهما في ظهر الأخرى ، تدرك الأضواء والألوان والأشكال وغير ذلك» وأنَّ هذا المعنى مُحالٌ على الله تعالى ؛ لأنه يستدعي التركيب أو قيام الحادث به أو نحو ذلك مما مستحيل في حقه جل شأنه. في (( هو وقد عرَّف محققو المتكلمين صفة البصر بالنّسبة الله تعالى بأنها: «صفة أزلية قائمة بذاته تعالى ومِن العلماء من قال : «البصر صفة ينكشف بها الشيء كالعلم فقوله: «صفة» كالجنس التّعريف شمل جميع الصفات، وقوله: ينكشف بها كالفصل الأوّل، أخرج جميع الصفات ما عدا صفتي السَّمع والعلم ؛ فإنّ كلّ واحدة منهما صفة ينكشف بها، وقوله: «الشيء ومعناه: الموجود ، كالفصل الثاني ، خرج به صفة العلم ؛ فإنه ينكشف به الموجود والمعدوم ، وقوله: «كالعلم» تشبيه أريد به أنَّ الانكشاف بالبصر اتضاح تام كاتضاح العلم، وأنت خبير أنه بعد هذا الشرح يبقى تعريف صفة البصرِ شاملا لتعريف صفة السمع، وعذرُ من ذكر هذا التعريف أنه يتعذر معرفة ما يخصُّ كل واحدة من صفتي السمع والبصر من الانكشافات ، ولما لم يجد العقلُ مَسَاءًا لإدراك ذلك لَجَأَ إلى السمع يبحث فيه ، فلم يجد=
[7 – The attribute of sight (al-baṣar), its definition, and an explanation of its relation.] Then sight (al-baṣar) is the seventh of them, and it is similar to what was mentioned regarding the necessity of being characterized by it. It is an eternal attribute, relating to visible things (al-mubṣarāt) or to existents (al-mawjūdāt), and it perceives with a complete perception, not by way of imagination (takhayyul) or illusion (tawahhum), nor by way of being affected by a sense or the arrival of a ray. = that with knowledge, and each of them has a reality whose knowledge we entrust to God Almighty. The matter is not as we are accustomed to: that sight gives by observation a clarity above knowledge. Rather, all His attributes are perfect and complete; it is impossible for Him to be hidden, or increase, or decrease, etc., as we have previously indicated. And there will come for us – when speaking about the relation of the attribute of sight – a mention of the types of relation of each of them to existents. (1) The discussion on the attribute of sight – it relates to it from three aspects: the first aspect: concerning the meaning of sight; the second aspect: concerning explaining the proof for the establishment of the attribute of sight for God Almighty; the third aspect: concerning explaining what the attribute of sight relates to and the types of relation of sight and hearing. As for the discussion on the first aspect, we say: know first that temporal sight (al-baṣar al-ḥādith), by which humans and other types of animals are described, is a power created in the two optic nerves that converge like two pairs crossing each other, perceiving lights, colors, shapes, and other things. And this meaning is impossible for God Almighty because it requires composition (tarkīb) or the occurrence of temporal events in Him, etc., which are impossible in His right, exalted is His station. And the verifying theologians (muḥaqqiqū al-mutakallimīn) have defined the attribute of sight with respect to God Almighty as: 'An eternal attribute subsisting in His essence.' And among the scholars is one who said: 'Sight is an attribute by which a thing is disclosed, like knowledge.' So his saying 'attribute' is like the genus in the definition, encompassing all attributes. His saying 'by which it is disclosed' is like the first differentia, excluding all attributes except the two attributes of hearing and knowledge, for each of them is an attribute by which something is disclosed. His saying 'the thing' – its meaning is 'the existent' – is like the second differentia, excluding the attribute of knowledge, for by knowledge the existent and the non-existent are disclosed. His saying 'like knowledge' is a simile intended to show that disclosure by sight is complete clarity like the clarity of knowledge. And you are aware that after this explanation, the definition of the attribute of sight remains inclusive of the definition of the attribute of hearing. The excuse of the one who mentioned this definition is that it is difficult to know what specifically distinguishes each of the two attributes of hearing and sight in terms of disclosures. Since the intellect found no way to perceive that, it resorted to hearing (sam‘) to investigate it, but it did not find=
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage discusses the divine attribute of sight (al-baṣar). It begins by stating that sight is the seventh attribute and that its necessity is similar to that of hearing. The definition is given: an eternal attribute that relates to visible things (mubṣarāt) or existents (mawjūdāt), perceiving them perfectly, not through imagination or sense perception. A note follows asserting that disclosure by sight is unlike human sight; all divine attributes are perfect, with no deficiency. The author then outlines three aspects of the discussion on sight: (1) the meaning of sight, (2) the proof for its establishment, and (3) what it relates to. The passage focuses on the first aspect: the meaning. Human sight is a physical power in the eyes, impossible for God due to composition and temporality. The verifying theologians define divine sight as an eternal attribute subsisting in God. Another definition is presented: 'Sight is an attribute by which a thing is disclosed, like knowledge.' This definition is analyzed: 'attribute' is the genus; 'by which it is disclosed' distinguishes it from non-disclosure attributes but includes hearing and knowledge; 'the thing' (i.e., existent) excludes knowledge (which also discloses non-existents); 'like knowledge' indicates complete clarity. However, this definition still overlaps with hearing. The author notes that the intellect cannot distinguish the specific disclosures of hearing and sight, so it relies on revelation, which does not provide a clear distinction either.

Key Terms
البصر (al-baṣar)
صفة أزلية (ṣifa azaliyya)
المبصرات (al-mubṣarāt)
الانكشاف (al-inkishāf)
محققو المتكلمين (muḥaqqiqū al-mutakallimīn)
الجنس (al-jins)
الفصل (al-faṣl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think divine sight is like human sight, involving physical eyes or rays.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that divine sight is an eternal, perfect attribute without any physical means, composition, or temporality.

A) It excludes the attribute of hearing.
B) It excludes the attribute of knowledge.
C) It excludes all attributes except sight.
D) It excludes the created world.

Key Takeaway
The divine attribute of sight is an eternal, perfect disclosure of existents, distinct from human sight and defined by theologians as an eternal attribute subsisting in God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God's sight sees every molecule of your being—including the thoughts you try to hide—would you continue to entertain that sin in your heart? What would it take for you to live as if you are always seen, not just by people, but by the One whose sight is perfect and without limit?
?
The scholars defined divine sight as an eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence, not dependent on any organ. Do you still think you can hide anything from Him? What area of your life do you treat as if He does not see, and what step can you take today to bring it into the light?
?
Human sight is limited, can be blocked, and fails. Divine sight is perfect and complete. When you consider that your Creator sees everything you do—not through a lens but through an eternal attribute—does that fill you with awe or fear? How should it change the way you speak, act, and think from now on?

Proof of Hearing, Sight, and Speech from Scripture and Their Three Attachments
Sanusi proves hearing, sight, and speech from Quran, gives an analogy, and enumerates three attachments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the attributes of hearing, sight, and speech are proven from scripture, and the three distinct types of attachment (talluq) for hearing and sight.

Core Idea
God's hearing and sight are distinct from His knowledge and have three modes of relation to existents.

Sanusi begins by citing the Quran to establish that God speaks, hears, and sees literally. He then draws an analogy from human experience to show that these attributes are not reducible to knowledge. Finally, he explains how hearing and sight relate to created beings in three ways: eternal actual, temporal actual, and eternal potential. This lesson unpacks that structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the scriptural proof
Read the opening where Sanusi cites Quran 4:164 and explains that the application of these attributes to God is literal, not metaphorical.
2
Understand the analogy to human experience
Note the distinction between knowing, hearing, and seeing something, using the example of a black ant crawling in the dark. This illustrates that disclosure through sight differs from knowledge and hearing.
3
Learn the three attachments
Identify the three kinds of relation: eternal actual disclosure of God's essence and attributes, temporal actual disclosure of beings when they exist, and eternal potential disclosure of beings before existence.
These are called 'talluq' in Arabic.
4
Clarify the implication
Realize that God's attributes are not negated before creation; they simply do not apply to non-existents. There is no deafness or blindness before creation.
This prevents attributing deficiency to God.

قسم الإلهيات صفة السمع والبصر والكلام مُثبتة بالنقل] (بذي ) أي : بصفة الكلام والسَّمع والبصر (أَتَانَا) أي: وَرَدَ (السَّمْعُ) أي: دليل هو المسموع ، ومراده : أَنَّهُ ورد بإطلاق مُشتقاتها عليه تعالى، والأصل في الإطلاق الحقيقة (۱) ، قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَكَلَّمَ اللَّهُ مُوسَى تَكْلِيمًا ﴾ [النساء: ١٦٤] له ببصره أن تكون قديمة كذات الله تعالى وصفاته، أو حادثةٌ ، وأعم من أن تكون الحادثة ذوات أو غيرها، وهذه عبارة السنوسي في عقيدته، ومنهم من قال تتعلق صفة البصر بالمبصرات، وهذه العبارة تحتمل معنيين ، إحداهما : أن يكون مراد صاحبها المبصرات في حقنا، وثانيهما: أن يكون مراده المبصرات في حقه سبحانه ، فإن حملناها على الثاني استوت العبارتان ، وعيله يكون الله تعالى مبصراً جميع الموجودات حتى ذاته وصفاته التي منها بصره وسمعه ، وحتى أن ذلك منكشف الأصوات ولو خفية جدًّا ، كدبيب النملة السوداء في الليل الحالك ، بمعنى انكشافاً زائداً على الانكشاف الحاصل بالعلم وبالسمع، نعني: أن هذا الانكشاف الحاصل بالبصر غير الانكشاف الحاصل بالعلم وبالسمع، فالانكشاف في الثلاثة: العلم، والسمع والبصر متغاير ، ولا يستغني سبحانه بكونه عليمًا عن كونه سميعاً، ولا بهما أو أحدهما عن كونه بصيرا ، كما تجده في نفسك من الفرق بين علمك وبصرك وسمعك ؛ فإن الفرق ضروري بين علمك بالشيء حال غيبته عنك وبين تعلّق بصرك أو سمعك به، ونحن نذكر هذا التمثيل للتقريب ، والله المثل الأعلى سبحانه ! ليس كمثله شيء وهو السميع البصير. ثم اعلم ان للسمع والبصر في تعلقهما بالموجودات ثلاث تعلقات : فانكشاف الذات العليَّة والصفات القائمة بهما تعلق تنجيزي قديم، وانكشاف ذوات الكائنات وصفاتها الوجودية بهما عند وجود هذه الذوات وهذه الصفات: هذه الذوات وهذه الصفات تنجيزي حادث ، وانكشاف ذوات الكائنات وصفاتها بهما قبل وجودها صلوحي قديم، ولا يلزم على تأخر التعلق التنجيزي الحادث وجود ضد هاتين الصفتين قبل وجود الحوادث؛ لأنهما ـ كما علمت ـ لا يتعلقان إلا بالموجودات، فقبل وجود الحوادث لا يتأتى سمعها ولا بصرها، فلا يثبت قبل وجودها عمى ولا صمم ، وإلا لزم عليه نسبة الجهل قبل وجود الحوادث فاعلم هذا ولا يلتبس عليك . محي.
Section on Divinity. The attribute of hearing, sight, and speech are established by transmission. By these (attributes) meaning the attribute of speech, hearing, and sight, came to us the transmitted proof, i.e., the proof that is heard, and he means that it came by applying their derivatives to Him, Exalted, and the principle in application is literal meaning. Allah Almighty said: 'And Allah spoke to Moses directly.' For His sight, (it is possible) that (objects) are eternal like the essence of Allah and His attributes, or temporal, and more general than that the temporal be essences or other. This is the expression of Sanusi in his creed. Some said the attribute of sight is related to visible things. This expression has two possible meanings: one, that its author means visible things in our regard; the second, that he means visible things in His, Exalted, regard. If we take it in the second sense, the two expressions are equal, and accordingly Allah is seeing all existing things, even His essence and attributes which include His sight and hearing, and even that (includes) the disclosure of sounds, however faint, like the crawling of a black ant in pitch darkness, meaning a disclosure additional to the disclosure obtained by knowledge and by hearing. We mean that this disclosure obtained by sight is not the disclosure obtained by knowledge and hearing. So disclosure in the three (modes) — knowledge, hearing, and sight — is distinct. He, Exalted, is not independent by being Knower from being Hearer, nor by them or one of them from being Seeing. As you find in yourself the difference between your knowledge, sight, and hearing; for the difference is necessary between your knowledge of something when it is absent from you and between your sight or hearing being attached to it. We mention this analogy for approximation, and Allah is the highest attribute! Nothing is like Him, and He is the Hearing, the Seeing. Then know that hearing and sight, in their relation to existents, have three relations: the disclosure of the supreme essence and the attributes subsisting in them is an eternal actual relation; the disclosure of the essences of beings and their existential attributes by them at the existence of these essences and attributes is a temporal actual relation; the disclosure of the essences of beings and their attributes by them before their existence is an eternal potential relation. It follows not from the delay of the temporal actual relation the existence of the opposite of these two attributes before the existence of temporal things; because they — as you know — are related only to existents. So before the existence of temporal things, their hearing and sight are not feasible. So before their existence, neither blindness nor deafness is established. Otherwise, ignorance would be attributed before the existence of temporal things. Know this, and do not be confused.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is both a proof from scripture for God's attributes of hearing, sight, and speech, and a detailed explanation of the three types of attachment (talluq) for hearing and sight. It begins by stating that these attributes are proven by transmitted evidence, citing the Quranic verse where Allah speaks to Moses directly. The principle is that when a derivative term (like 'Speaker') is applied to God, it indicates a literal meaning, not a metaphorical one. The author then discusses whether the objects of God's sight are eternal or temporal, explaining that the expression of Sanusi covers both. The main point is that God sees all things, even His own essence and attributes, and that the disclosure of things through sight differs from disclosure through knowledge or hearing. To illustrate, the author uses an analogy from human experience: there is a necessary difference between knowing something in absence and seeing or hearing it. The passage then enumerates the three attachments for hearing and sight: (1) eternal actual disclosure of God's essence and attributes, (2) temporal actual disclosure of contingent beings when they exist, and (3) eternal potential disclosure of beings before they exist. Importantly, the temporal actual attachment does not imply that God lacked hearing or sight before creation, because these attributes only relate to what exists; thus, before contingent things exist, there is no deafness or blindness. The author warns against confusion.

Key Terms
السمع
البصر
الكلام
التعلق التنجيزي
التعلق الصلوحي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's hearing and sight are the same as His knowledge.
Correction: The passage stresses that disclosure through hearing and sight is distinct from disclosure through knowledge; each attribute provides a different mode of awareness.

Checkpoint

A) Eternal actual, temporal actual, eternal potential
B) Eternal potential, temporal actual, temporal potential
C) Eternal actual, eternal potential, temporal potential

Key Takeaway
God's attributes of hearing, sight, and speech are confirmed by scripture, and they relate to creation in three distinct modes without implying any deficiency in God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin in secret, do you truly believe that God hears your whisper with an attribute distinct from His knowledge? How does that change your attitude toward repentance?
?
The passage says God's disclosure of His own essence and attributes is eternal and actual. If God is constantly aware of Himself in this way, are you content with how little you reflect on His majesty? What will you do to change that starting today?
?
Before you were born, God had an eternal potential relation to you. Does that truth make you feel insignificant or profoundly cared for? How should it shape your reliance on Him?

The Three Positions on the Attribute of Perception
States the three schools on whether perception is an additional attribute beyond the seven.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scholarly positions regarding the attribute of perception (idrak) and why the question of whether it is an additional attribute matters in Ash'ari theology.

Core Idea
Perception: is it a separate divine attribute or not? Three schools differ.

This lesson examines a specific dispute within Ash'ari theology about the divine attribute of perception. While the seven essential attributes are agreed upon, scholars differed on whether perception is an additional attribute or already included in hearing and sight. The passage from the Jawhara presents the three positions concisely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three schools
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. Try to spot the three positions mentioned: affirmation, denial, and abstention (waqf).
The phrase 'على ثلاثة مذاهب' directly tells you there are three schools.
2
Understand key terms
Focus on the terms 'الإدراك' (perception) and 'الوقف' (abstention). Note that 'السبع المعاني' refers to the seven essential attributes (life, knowledge, will, power, hearing, sight, speech).
Waqf is a valid methodological stance in theology.
3
Reflect on the question about speech
The passage ends with a question about an additional attribute over speech. Consider how this connects to the broader debate about whether perception is distinct from hearing and sight.
This clarifies that the core issue is whether perception is separate from other attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخلاف في زيادة صفة الإدراك على السبع المعاني على ثلاثة مذاهب] ٣٠ - فَهَلْ لَهُ إِدْرَاكٌ أَوْ لَا خُلْفُ وَعِنْدَ قَوْمٍ صَحَ فِيهِ الوَقْفُ ١ (ε) (فَهَلْ لَهُ تعالى صفةٌ زائدة على الكلام (٤)
Disagreement on the increase of the attribute of perception over the seven meanings: it is on three schools. So, does He have perception or not? Disagreement (exists). And according to some, abstention therein is sound. So, does He, Exalted, have an additional attribute over speech?
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage introduces the dispute on whether perception (idrak) is an additional attribute of God beyond the seven essential attributes (life, knowledge, will, power, hearing, sight, speech). It states that there are three scholarly positions: (1) affirmation that perception is an additional attribute, (2) denial that it is additional (i.e., it is not a separate attribute), and (3) abstention (waqf) from taking a definite position. The line quoted from the Jawhara poem asks two questions: 'Does He have perception or not? There is disagreement, and with some people abstention therein is valid.' The commentary then adds a clarification: 'Does He, Exalted, have an additional attribute over speech?' suggesting that the core question is whether perception is distinct from other attributes like hearing and sight.

Key Terms

السبع المعاني
الوقف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the dispute is about denying perception itself.
Correction: The debate is not about whether God perceives (that is agreed), but whether perception is a separate attribute or already included in hearing and sight.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The attribute of perception is a point of disagreement among Ash'aris, with three valid positions: affirmation, denial, and abstention.

Derivative Names Prove God's Real Attributes
Sanusi presents a rational proof for God's attributes using the linguistic principle of derivation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how applying derived names to God rationally proves His eternal attributes of speech, hearing, and sight.

Core Idea
Derived names demand their roots—God's attributes are real, not mere titles.

The Qur'an calls Allah 'the Hearing, the Seeing.' All rational people agree He is Speaker, Hearing, and Seeing. But how do we know these names correspond to real attributes? Sanusi unpacks a powerful linguistic argument: when you call someone 'speaker,' the name only makes sense if they actually possess speech. This lesson walks through that proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage and Translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the logical structure: a Quranic verse, a consensus claim, then the derivation principle, and finally three supporting premises.
2
Identify the Key Derivation Principle
Sanusi states: 'Applying the derived term as a description to something necessitates the existence of the source of derivation for it.' This is the core idea. Understand that 'derived term' (mushtaqq) means an active participle like 'Speaker,' and its source (ma'khadh al-ishtiqaq) is the attribute (speech).
This principle is logical: you cannot call someone a speaker if they have no speech.
3
Examine the Supporting Premises
Three premises reinforce the proof: (1) temporal accidents cannot subsist in God's essence, so the attribute cannot be temporary; (2) an attribute must subsist in the thing it qualifies, so God's attributes are real; (3) speech is distinct from knowledge and will, proving each attribute is separate.
These premises rule out alternative explanations.
4
Synthesize the Full Argument
Combine the derivation principle with the premises. Since God is called 'Speaker' and derived names require their source, speech must exist in God. It cannot be temporal, so it is eternal. It must subsist in Him, so it is a real attribute. And it is distinct from other attributes. Thus, God truly has speech, hearing, and sight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
﴿وَهُوَ السَّمِيعُ الْبَصِيرُ ﴾ [الشورى: ١١] معَ إجماع أهل الملل والأديان وجميعِ العقلاء على أنه تعالى متكلّم ، وسميع ، وبصير، وإطلاق المشتقّ وصفا لشيء يقتضي ثبوت مأخذ الاشتقاق له (۱) ، مع استحالة قيام الحوادث بذاته تعالى : و وجوبِ قيامِ صفة الشَّيء به(۲) وقيام الدليل على مغايرة الكلام للعلم والإرادة (۳).
And He is the Hearing, the Seeing (Quran 42:11) with the consensus of the people of religious communities and all rational people that He, Exalted, is Speaker, Hearing, and Seeing. And applying the derived term as a description to something necessitates the existence of the source of derivation for it (i.e., the root meaning), along with the impossibility of temporal accidents subsisting in His essence, Exalted, and the necessity of the attribute of a thing subsisting in it, and the establishment of the proof for the distinctness of speech from knowledge and will.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage provides a rational proof for God's attributes of speech, hearing, and sight, based on the linguistic principle of derivation. It begins with a Quranic verse and notes the consensus of all religious communities and rational people that God is described as Speaker, Hearing, and Seeing. The key argument is that when a derived term (mushtaqq) such as 'Speaker' is applied to God, it necessarily implies that the source of derivation (ma'khadh al-ishtiqaq) — i.e., the attribute itself — must exist in Him. This is combined with two other premises: first, temporal accidents cannot subsist in God's essence (so the attribute cannot be temporal); second, an attribute must subsist in the thing it qualifies (so God's speech, hearing, and sight are real attributes subsisting in His essence). Additionally, a separate proof establishes that speech is distinct from knowledge and will, countering claims that attributes are reducible to each other. Thus, the passage concludes that God truly has the attributes of speech, hearing, and sight as eternal, distinct attributes.

Key Terms
المشتق
مأخذ الاشتقاق
قيام الحوادث
مغايرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Derived names like 'Speaker' are merely metaphorical or honorific titles that do not imply a real attribute in God.
Correction: The derivation principle shows that a derived term logically requires its root attribute to exist. Combined with other premises, this proves God truly possesses speech, hearing, and sight as real, eternal attributes.

Checkpoint
What does the principle of derivative adjectives (al-mushtaqq) entail for God's attributes?
A) That the attribute must be temporal
B) That the root attribute must exist in God
C) That the adjective is metaphorical
D) That the attribute is identical to the essence

Key Takeaway
The derived names of God necessarily imply that He truly possesses the corresponding eternal attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God hears every word you speak, even the ones you whisper to yourself? How would your speech change if you lived constantly aware that His attribute of hearing is real and eternal?
?
When you sin in secret, do you act as if God's sight does not reach you? What would it take for you to internalize that His seeing is not a metaphor but a real, ever-present attribute?
?
If God's speech is not merely a created sound but an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, then His words in the Quran are forever true. Does this truth move you to approach the Quran with awe and obedience, or do you treat it like a book of suggestions?

The Three Positions on the Attribute of Perception
States the disagreement over whether perception is an additional attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three positions scholars have taken regarding whether perception (idrak) is an additional attribute of Allah, and what suspension (waqf) means in this context.

Core Idea
The attribute of perception has three positions: affirmation, denial, and suspension.

This lesson presents a foundational disagreement in Islamic theology about Allah's attributes. Some scholars argue that perception is a distinct attribute beyond hearing and sight, while others deny it, and a third group suspends judgment. The following passage from Sanusi's poem summarizes these three views.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic verse and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic line of poetry along with its literal translation. Notice the concise way Sanusi presents the disagreement.
The verse uses three key terms: إدراك (perception), خلف (disagreement), وقف (suspension).
2
Identify the three schools
The passage mentions three schools: those who affirm perception as an additional attribute, those who deny it, and those who suspend judgment. Understand what each position entails.
Suspension (waqf) means neither affirming nor denying due to lack of decisive evidence.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why such a distinction matters. Disagreements about attributes affect our understanding of Allah's nature. This lesson establishes the range of scholarly views on perception.
Later lessons will explore evidence for each position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَهَلْ لَهُ إِدْرَاكٌ أَوْ لَا خُلْفُ وَعِنْدَ قَوْمٍ صَحَ فِيهِ الوَقْفُ (ε) (فَهَلْ لَهُ تعالى صفةٌ زائدة على الكلام والسمع والبصر يُقال لها الإدراك على ثلاثة مذاهب: فمنهم من أثبتها، ومنهم من نفاها، ومنهم من وقف فيها).
So, does He have perception or not? A disagreement, and among some people suspension (waqf) is considered valid. (So, does He, exalted, have an attribute additional to speech, hearing, and sight called perception? [There are] three schools: some affirm it, some deny it, and some suspend judgment regarding it.)
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a statement of the theological disagreement concerning the attribute of perception (al-idrak) for Allah. The author, Sanusi, presents a line of poetry (verse 30) that encapsulates the three positions: affirmation, negation, and suspension (waqf). The commentary then clarifies that the dispute is about whether Allah has an additional attribute beyond speech, hearing, and sight—that is, a distinct attribute called perception—or not. Some scholars affirm it as a real attribute, some deny its existence entirely, and some withhold judgment, neither affirming nor denying it.

Key Terms
إدراك
خلف
وقف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Suspension (waqf) means denying the attribute or expressing uncertainty about Allah's perfection.
Correction: Suspension is a principled position of withholding judgment due to insufficient evidence, not denial. It is a valid scholarly stance, especially when scriptural texts are ambiguous.

Key Takeaway

The Human Concept of Perception Impossible for Allah
Defines human perception and states its impossibility for Allah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the human concept of perception (al-idrak) is impossible for Allah by consensus of scholars.

Core Idea
Human perception requires effort and acquisition – impossible for Allah.

Before engaging in the broader debate about proving divine attributes, we must clarify what perception means in human terms. This passage defines perception as a conceptualization involving effort and acquisition, then states the consensus that this meaning cannot apply to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of perception
Read the Arabic definition of al-idrak: 'the conceptualization of the reality of the thing with which perception is concerned for the one who perceives it.'
Note that this definition includes effort and acquisition.
2
Understand why it is impossible for Allah
The passage explains that perception in this sense requires effort, labor, and acquisition, which entail the precedence of their opposite – both impossible for Allah.
3
Recognize the consensus
Notice that the impossibility is said to be 'by consensus' (bil-ittifaq), meaning all scholars agree that this type of perception does not apply to Allah.
This sets the stage for the methodological dispute that follows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أوَّلاً: أن «الإدراك» في حقنا هو: تصور حقيقة الشيء الذي يتعلق به الإدراك عند من يدركه»، وهذا المعنى مستحيل على الله تعالى باتفاق؛ لأنه يقتضي التعمل والتكلف والكسب، وهي تقتضي سَبْقَ ضده، وذلك مستحيل بالنظر له تعالى. ثم اعلم أنه قد وقع خلاف بين العلماء حاصله: هل يجوز إثبات صفة الله تعالى لم يرد نص من الشرع على ثبوتها بمجرد دليل العقل؟ فذهب قوم منهم إمام الحرمين والقاضي الباقلاني: إلى جواز ذلك ما دامت الصفة التي يراد إثباتها له سبحانه تدل على الكمال المحض. وذهب قوم إلى أنه لا يجوز إثبات صفة لم يرد في لسان الشارع وصفه تعالى بها؛ فإن جاء في لسان الشرع وصف الله تعالى بصفة أثبتناها بالعقل تحقيقاً لما ورد الشرع به، وإن لم يرد أمسكنا. وقد انبنى على هذا الخلاف اختلافهم في إثبات صفة «الإدراك» الله تعالى؛ فمَن ذهب إلى أنه لا يجوز لنا أن نُثبت بمحض العقل صفة ولو كانت دالة على كمال محض قال: ليس الله تعالى صفة تسمى الإدراك، ومن ذهب إلى أنه يجوز لنا أن نُثبت له تعالى كل صفة تدل على الكمال المحض قال الله تعالى متصف بالإدراك كما أنه متصف بالعلم، والقدرة، والسمع، والبصر، والكلام.
Know first that 'perception' (al-idrak) in our context is: the conceptualization of the reality of the thing with which perception is concerned for the one who perceives it. This meaning is impossible for Allah, exalted, by consensus, because it requires effort, labor, and acquisition, which require the precedence of their opposite, and that is impossible with respect to Him, exalted. Then know that a disagreement has occurred among scholars, the gist of which is: Is it permissible to prove an attribute of Allah, exalted, for which no scriptural text has come confirming its existence, by mere rational proof? A group, including Imam al-Haramayn and al-Qadi al-Baqillani, held that this is permissible as long as the attribute that is intended to be proven for Him, glorified, indicates pure perfection. Another group held that it is not permissible to prove an attribute that has not come in the language of the Lawgiver describing Him, exalted; if the language of the Law comes describing Allah, exalted, with an attribute, we prove it by reason to confirm what the Law has brought, but if it does not come, we refrain. Based on this disagreement, their disagreement about proving the attribute of 'perception' for Allah, exalted, was built: whoever holds that we are not allowed to prove by mere reason an attribute even if it indicates pure perfection, says: Allah, exalted, does not have an attribute called perception; and whoever holds that it is permissible for us to prove for Him, exalted, every attribute that indicates pure perfection, says: Allah, exalted, is described with perception just as He is described with knowledge, power, hearing, sight, and speech.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a methodological analysis of how divine attributes may be proven. It first defines 'perception' (al-idrak) in human terms as a conceptualization that involves effort and acquisition, which is impossible for Allah by consensus. Then it presents the central methodological dispute: whether reason alone can establish an attribute not mentioned in scripture. Two schools are described: one (led by Imam al-Haramayn and al-Baqillani) permits rational proof for any attribute that signifies pure perfection; the other restricts proof to attributes that have scriptural warrant, using reason only to confirm what scripture states. This dispute directly determines the position on the attribute of perception: those who restrict to scripture deny it; those who allow rational proof affirm it as an attribute of perfection akin to knowledge, power, etc.

Key Terms
إدراك
تصور
التعمل
التكلف
الكسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that when scholars say Allah 'perceives,' it means He goes through a mental process like humans do.
Correction: This passage clarifies that the human meaning of perception involves effort and acquisition, which is impossible for Allah. Any attribution of perception to Allah must be understood differently, not as a process.

Checkpoint
Why is the human concept of perception impossible for Allah according to the passage?
A) Because Allah is too exalted to have any attributes.
B) Because it requires effort, labor, and acquisition, which imply temporal procession.
C) Because the Quran denies perception to Allah.
D) Because perception is a created thing.

Key Takeaway
Human perception is a process of effort and acquisition, which cannot apply to the eternal and perfect nature of Allah.

?
The passage says that human perception requires the precedence of its opposite (ignorance). When you feel proud of your knowledge, do you recognize that it came from a state of ignorance, and that Allah's knowledge has no such history? How will this realization cultivate humility in your heart and deepen your awe of Allah?

Two Schools on Proving Divine Attributes by Reason
Presents two schools on rational proof of attributes and applies to perception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two schools of thought on whether reason alone can prove a divine attribute not mentioned in scripture, and how this applies to the attribute of perception.

Core Idea
Some allow rational proof for attributes of pure perfection; others require scriptural warrant.

After establishing that human perception cannot be directly applied to Allah, the author introduces a deeper methodological question: can reason alone prove an attribute of Allah if that attribute is not explicitly mentioned in the Quran and Sunnah? This passage presents two scholarly positions and shows how they lead to different conclusions about the attribute of perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the dispute and its two positions
Read the passage describing the disagreement. Identify the two positions: one allowing rational proof for any attribute of pure perfection, the other restricting to scriptural attributes.
Note the names: Imam al-Haramayn and al-Qadi al-Baqillani for the first position.
2
Understand the rationale of each school
The first school believes that if an attribute indicates pure perfection (al-kamal al-mahd), reason can prove it. The second school fears exceeding the limits of revelation and only confirms what scripture teaches.
3
Apply to the attribute of perception
The passage applies the dispute to al-idrak: the first school affirms it as an attribute of perfection; the second denies it as a distinct attribute.
This shows the practical impact of the methodological disagreement.

Source Text

Based on this disagreement, their disagreement about proving the attribute of 'perception' for Allah, exalted, was built: whoever holds that we are not allowed to prove by mere reason an attribute even if it indicates pure perfection, says: Allah, exalted, does not have an attribute called perception; and whoever holds that it is permissible for us to prove for Him, exalted, every attribute that indicates pure perfection, says: Allah, exalted, is described with perception just as He is described with knowledge, power, hearing, sight, and speech.

The Divine Attribute of Perception: Unity or Multiplicity?
Defines perception as an eternal attribute and discusses its objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the divine attribute of perception and the scholarly disagreement regarding its unity or multiplicity.

Core Idea
Perception is one eternal attribute – or is it many? The scholars disagree.

In this lesson, we examine the attribute of perception (al-idrak) as defined by those who affirm it. Like hearing and sight, perception is an eternal attribute subsisting in Allah's essence. The main point of controversy is whether it is a single attribute encompassing all types of perceived objects or multiple attributes each corresponding to a different sense modality. The passage also notes a tension in the commentator’s own treatment.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of perception
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note that perception is defined as an eternal attribute subsisting in Allah's essence, similar to hearing and sight.
The proof for perception is analogous to the rational proof for hearing and sight.
2
Identify the two scholarly views
Find the two opinions on the objects of perception: some say it is one attribute for all perceived things; others say each type of perceived object requires a distinct perception.
The disagreement is not about the objects themselves but about the attribute's unity or multiplicity.
3
Analyze the commentator's tendency
Notice that the commentator initially leans toward unity but when explaining the proof leans toward multiplicity. Consider why there might be this tension.
4
Reflect on the implications
Think about how this dispute affects our understanding of Allah's attributes. Does it make a difference whether perception is one or many?
This connects to the broader principle of divine simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أنَّ الذين ذهبوا إلى أنَّ الله تعالى صفة تسمى الإدراك جعلوها صفةً من المعاني، وعرفوها بأنها: صفة أزلية قائمة بذاته تعالى واستدلوا عليها بمثل الدليل العقلي الذي ذكرناه في الاستدلال على صفتي: السمع والبصر. ثم اختلفوا في متعلقها؛ فمنهم من قال: تتعلق هذه الصفة بالمسموعات والمشمومات والمذوقات، من غير اتصال بمَحَالٍّ ولا مُمَاسَّة ولا تكيف بكيفياتها، وهؤلاء ذهبوا إلى أنَّ الإدراك صفةٌ واحدةٌ ومنهم من قال: للمَلْموسات إدراك، وللمشمومات إدراك آخر وللمَذُوقات إدراك ثالث .. وهكذا، وكلام الشارح في أوله وعند تقرير المذهب يميل إلى الإدراك واحدٌ، ولكنه في حال بيان الدليل يميل إلى أنَّ الإدراك متعدد.
Then know that those who held that Allah, exalted, has an attribute called perception made it an attribute of meaning (sifah ma'nawiyyah), and defined it as: an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, exalted. They argued for it by a rational proof similar to what we mentioned in arguing for the two attributes of hearing and sight. Then they differed regarding its objects: some said this attribute pertains to heard things, smelled things, and tasted things, without contact, touching, or being qualified by their modalities; these held that perception is one attribute. Others said that for tangible things there is a perception, for smelled things another perception, for tasted things a third perception, and so on. The commentator's words at the beginning and when presenting the school tend toward perception being one, but when explaining the proof he tends toward perception being multiple.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of perception (al-idrak) for those who affirm it. It is described as an eternal attribute (sifah azaliyyah) subsisting in Allah's essence, analogous to hearing and sight, and proven by a similar rational proof. The main dispute here concerns its objects (muta'alliq): some scholars hold that perception is a single attribute that encompasses all perceived things (heard, smelled, tasted) without physical contact or qualification; others hold that each category of perceived objects (tangible, olfactible, gustatory) requires a distinct perception, making the attribute multiple. The commentator (al-Sharh) notes that the initial explanation and the presentation of the school lean toward unity (wahdah), but the proof itself leans toward multiplicity (ta'addud), reflecting an internal tension.

Correction: The dispute is about whether the attribute itself is one or many, not about the variety of objects perceived. Allah's perception is not like human perception.

Definition of al-Idrak and Its Impossibility for Allah
Defines human perception and negates it for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the human concept of perception cannot be applied to Allah and the rational argument behind this impossibility.

Core Idea
Human perception requires acquisition; Allah is free from need.

This lesson begins with a crucial distinction: how we define perception (idrak) in human terms and why that definition cannot be applied to Allah. The author presents a concise argument based on consensus and rational implication. Let's unpack it step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the author's words sink in before interpreting.
Notice the compact style of the argument.
2
Understand the definition of idrak
The passage defines idrak as 'the conceptualization of the reality of the thing perceived by the perceiver.' This implies a process of acquiring knowledge.
3
Grasp the reason for impossibility
The author states that this meaning is impossible for Allah because it entails takalluf (effort) and kasb (acquisition), which require a prior state of lacking. Allah is eternal and perfect, so this cannot apply.
The argument is based on rational necessity and consensus.
4
Reflect on the consensus
The author mentions that this impossibility is agreed upon by consensus (bi al-ittifaq). This shows the strength of the argument among scholars.
5
Apply the lesson
Consider common misconceptions: some might think idrak is just 'knowing,' but the definition includes acquisition. Reflect on why this distinction matters for divine attributes.
The key is that human categories involve temporal change, which is impossible for Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أوَّلاً: أن «الإدراك» في حقنا هو: تصور حقيقة الشيء الذي يتعلق به الإدراك عند من يدركه، وهذا المعنى مستحيل على الله تعالى باتفاق ؛ لأنه يقتضي التكلف والكسب، وهي تقتضي سَبْقَ ضده، وذلك مستحيل بالنظر له تعالى.
Know first that 'perception' (al-idrak) in our context is: the conceptualization of the reality of the thing with which perception is concerned on the part of the one who perceives it. And this meaning is impossible for Allah Almighty by consensus, because it implies effort (takalluf) and acquisition (kasb), and they imply the precedence of their opposite, and that is impossible with respect to Him Almighty.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'idrak' as used in human context and then immediately negates that meaning for Allah. The author begins by defining idrak as the conceptualization of the reality of the perceived object by the perceiver. This definition indicates that human perception involves an acquisition of a concept and an effort, which implies that the perceiver was previously without that perception. Such a temporal aspect is impossible for Allah, who is eternal and perfect in all aspects. The author states that this impossibility is agreed upon by consensus (bi al-ittifaq). The reason given is that idrak entails takalluf (effort) and kasb (acquisition), which require a prior state of lacking. Since Allah is necessarily existent and free from any need or deficiency, this is impossible. Thus, the human understanding of idrak cannot be applied to Allah.

Key Terms
الإدراك
التكلف
الكسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Idrak is simply knowing something.
Correction: The passage defines idrak as conceptualization of a thing's reality, which involves effort and acquisition, and this is impossible for Allah.

Checkpoint

B) Because idrak implies effort and acquisition, which require a prior lack.
C) Because idrak is a bodily sense.
D) Because idrak is not mentioned in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Human perception (idrak) involves acquisition and effort, making it impossible for Allah, who is perfect and free from need.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

In what area of your faith are you content with ignorance or claiming to 'perceive' something without true effort? Commit to one concrete action today to seek deeper understanding of a divine attribute.

The Dispute Applied to the Attribute of Idrak
Applies the dispute to idrak and states the two resulting positions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the general dispute about affirming attributes by reason leads to two positions on whether Allah has an attribute called perception (idrak).

Core Idea
The dispute over rational proof determines whether idrak is affirmed or denied.

The author now applies the earlier general dispute to the specific attribute of idrak (perception). This shows how abstract methodological positions have concrete consequences for which attributes we affirm for Allah. The passage presents the two resulting views concisely.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the two positions.
Notice the structural phrase 'فمَن ذهب ... ومن ذهب' indicating the two groups.

Identify the two groups

3

The author then elaborates on the second view: those who affirm idrak consider it an attribute of meaning (sifat al-ma'ani), a real, eternal attribute subsisting in the divine essence. They define it as an eternal attribute and argue for it using the same rational proof used for hearing and sight. This indicates that idrak is seen as analogous to those attributes.

Affirming Idrak: Definition and Proof
Elaborates on the affirming view: definition, classification, and proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of idrak as an eternal attribute of meaning and the rational proof used to establish it.

Core Idea
Those who affirm idrak define it as an eternal attribute of meaning, proven by reason.

In this passage, the author elaborates on the position of those who affirm idrak for Allah. He explains that they classify it as an attribute of meaning (sifat al-ma'ani), define it as an eternal attribute subsisting in the divine essence, and argue for it using a rational proof akin to that used for hearing and sight.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition
Read the Arabic passage and the translation carefully. Focus on the definition and the proof reference.
The phrase 'صفة أزلية قائمة بذاته' is key.
2
Identify the components
Identify three parts: classification (attribute of meaning), definition (eternal, subsisting), and proof (rational, similar to hearing/sight).
3
Connect to earlier content
Recall that hearing and sight are also attributes of meaning. This shows consistency in the school's approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد انبنى على هذا الخلاف اختلافهم في إثبات صفة «الإدراك» لله تعالى ; فمَن ذهب إلى أنه لا يجوز لنا أن نُثبت بمحض العقل صفة ولو كانت دالة على كمال محض قال: ليس لله تعالى صفة تسمى الإدراك، ومن ذهب إلى أنه يجوز لنا أن نُثبت له تعالى كل صفة تدل على الكمال المحض قال: الله تعالى متصف بالإدراك كما أنه متصف بالعلم، والقدرة، والسمع، والبصر، والكلام. ثم اعلم أنَّ الذين ذهبوا إلى أنَّ لله تعالى صفة تسمى الإدراك جعلوها صفة من المعاني، وعرفوها بأنها : صفة أزلية قائمة بذاته تعالى واستدلوا عليها بمثل الدليل العقلي الذي ذكرناه في الاستدلال على صفتي السمع والبصر.
And upon this disagreement was built their disagreement concerning affirming the attribute of 'perception' (al-idrak) for Allah Almighty. For whoever held that it is not permissible for us to affirm by mere reason an attribute even if it indicates pure perfection said: There is no attribute called perception for Allah Almighty. And whoever held that it is permissible for us to affirm for Him, Exalted, every attribute that indicates pure perfection said: Allah Almighty is qualified with perception just as He is qualified with knowledge, power, hearing, sight, and speech. Then know that those who held that Allah Almighty has an attribute called perception made it an attribute of meaning (sifat al-ma'ani), and defined it as an eternal attribute subsisting in His Essence, Exalted, and they argued for it by means of a rational proof similar to the one we mentioned in arguing for the two attributes of hearing and sight.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage applies the general dispute to the specific case of idrak. The author shows that the two positions on rational proof lead directly to two positions on idrak. The first position, which denies rational proof for attributes, denies that idrak is an attribute of Allah. The second position, which allows it, affirms that Allah possesses idrak just like other essential attributes. The author then elaborates on the second view: those who affirm idrak consider it an attribute of meaning (sifat al-ma'ani), a real, eternal attribute subsisting in the divine essence. They define it as an eternal attribute and argue for it using the same rational proof used for hearing and sight. This indicates that idrak is seen as analogous to those attributes.

Key Terms
صفة المعاني
أزلية
قائمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think idrak is merely a synonym for knowledge or an interpretive concept rather than a distinct attribute.
Correction: The passage clearly distinguishes idrak as an attribute of meaning, defined as eternal and subsisting, with its own rational proof.

Checkpoint
How do those who affirm idrak classify it?
A) As an attribute of action.
B) As an attribute of meaning.
C) As a negative attribute.
D) As a relational attribute.

Key Takeaway
Idrak is affirmed as an eternal attribute of meaning, supported by rational proof analogous to that for hearing and sight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God's idrak means He perceives every detail of your life—your thoughts, intentions, and secret actions. Do you truly live as if this is real, or do you act differently when you think no one is watching? Today, commit to one hidden area where you will align your behavior with the reality of divine perception.
?
Idrak is an eternal attribute: God has always perceived you, even before you existed. How does this eternal awareness affect your sense of identity and purpose? Pause and reflect: are you living in a way that honors being known by God from all eternity?
?
If idrak is proven by the same rational argument as hearing and sight, then your rational mind affirms that God sees and hears everything. But does your heart truly feel His presence? Ask yourself honestly: when you sin in secret, do you fear human discovery more than the fact that God is perceiving you? Repent and resolve to live with greater God-consciousness.

Dispute on the Multiplicity of al-Idrak and the Response to an Objection
Discusses the dispute on idrak and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological debate on whether Allah's attribute of perception (idrak) is one or multiple, and how the objection regarding divine simplicity is resolved.

Core Idea
Multiplicity of attributes is allowed when their objects or modes differ.

In this passage, the author navigates a debate among theologians: is Allah's attribute of perception a single attribute that relates to all sensory objects, or are there multiple perceptions for each sense? The objection arises that this seems to violate the unity of divine attributes. The answer lies in the distinction between the object, type, and mode of relation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
The passage presents two views: that perception is one attribute relating to all objects, and that there are distinct perceptions for each sense. Notice the differences.
2
Understand the objection
The objection is that multiple perceptions would imply multiple eternal attributes of the same genus, which is prohibited.
This connects to the principle of unity of attributes.
3
Follow the answer
The author answers that the prohibition applies only when object, type, and mode of relation are identical. Here they differ, so multiplicity is allowed.
The conclusion reminds us of Allah's transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اختلفوا في متعلقها؛ فمنهم من قال: تتعلق هذه الصفة بالمسموعات والمشمومات والمذوقات، مِن غير اتصال بمحالها ولا مُمَاسَّة ولا تكيف بكيفياتها، وهؤلاء ذهبوا إلى أنَّ الإدراك صفةٌ واحدةٌ ومنهم من قال: للمَلْموسات إدراك، وللمشمومات إدراك آخر وللمَذُوقات إدراكاً ثالث .. وهكذا، وكلام الشارح في أوله وعند تقرير المذهب يميل إلى أن الإدراك واحدٌ، ولكنه في حال بيان الدليل يميل إلى أنَّ الإدراك متعدد. فإن قلت: فكيف ذهب هؤلاء إلى تعدد صفة «الإدراك» مع أنَّ الصفات القديمة لا تتعدد؟ وقد بينا في وحدانية الصفات أن الله ليس له صفتان من جنس واحد ؟ فالجواب: أنَّ الممنوع هو تعدد الصفة مع اتحاد المُتعلّق ونوع التعلق وكيفية التعلق، ألا ترى أنه لما اختلف المتعلَّق كما في العلم والسمع لم يضر، ولما اختلفت كيفية التعلُّق كما في السمع والبصر لم يضر؛ فههنا من هذا القبيل. ولا شكّ أنَّ كيفية اللمس غير كيفية الشَّم، وكلاهما غير كيفية الذَّوْقِ، وثمرة كلَّ منهما غير ثمرة الآخر. هذا كله أنه يجب اعتقاد أن الله تعالى منزّه عن سمات الحوادث وأن هذا الكلام يقوله العلماء تقريبا للأذهان، والله المثل الأعلى لا تحيط العقول به ولا ندركه.
Then they differed concerning its object; some of them said: This attribute pertains to sounds, smells, and tastes, without contact with their loci, nor touch, nor being qualified by their qualities, and these went to that perception is one attribute. And some of them said: For tangible things there is a perception, for smells another perception, and for tastes a third perception, and so on. And the words of the commentator at the beginning and when establishing the school tend to that perception is one, but when explaining the proof he tends to that perception is multiple. If you say: How did these people go to multiplicity of the attribute of perception while the eternal attributes do not multiply? And we have explained in the unity of attributes that Allah does not have two attributes of one genus? Then the answer: That which is forbidden is multiplicity of the attribute while there is unity of the object, the type of relation, and the mode of relation. Do you not see that when the object differed as in knowledge and hearing, it did not harm, and when the mode of relation differed as in hearing and sight, it did not harm? So here it is of this kind. And there is no doubt that the mode of touch is different from the mode of smell, and both are different from the mode of taste, and the fruit of each is different from the fruit of the other. All this is that it is necessary to believe that Allah Almighty is transcendent above the characteristics of contingents, and that this speech is said by scholars as an approximation for minds. And Allah has the supreme likeness; minds do not encompass Him nor do we perceive Him.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage addresses the debate on whether idrak is a single attribute or multiple. The author presents two views: one holds that idrak is one attribute that relates to all objects (sounds, smells, tastes) without contact or qualification; the other holds that there are distinct perceptions for each sensory domain (touch, smell, taste). The commentator (al-sharih) initially inclines towards unity but later during the proof leans towards multiplicity. The author then raises an objection: if attributes are eternal and do not multiply, how can one claim multiple idraks? The response is that the prohibition against multiplicity applies only when the object, type of relation, and mode of relation are identical. If they differ, as in the case of knowledge (different object) or hearing vs. sight (different mode), multiplicity is not problematic. Here, the modes of perception (touch, smell, taste) are distinct, so multiple idraks are allowed. The passage concludes with a reminder that despite this rational discussion, Allah transcends all contingent attributes, and such speech is only an approximation.

Key Terms
متعلق
كيفية التعلق
وحدانية الصفات
الإدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that any multiplicity of attributes violates tawhid.
Correction: Only when object, type, and mode of relation are identical. If any differs, multiplicity is not a problem.

Checkpoint
According to the response, when is multiplicity of an attribute not forbidden?
A) When the attribute is eternal.
B) When the object, type, and mode of relation are different.
C) When the attribute is derived from reason.
D) When the attribute is mentioned in scripture.

Key Takeaway
Allah's attribute of perception can be considered one or multiple as long as the object or mode of relation differs, and this does not contradict divine unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah's attributes like hearing, sight, and knowledge, do you tend to imagine them as similar to human senses? How does this lesson's distinction between object and mode challenge your mental pictures?
?
The passage says this discussion is 'an approximation for minds' and that Allah is transcendent. Are you ever tempted to think you fully understand Allah's attributes? What would it cost you to hold that belief in humility?
?
If Allah's perception can be multiple due to different modes, how does that expand your understanding of His nearness? Does it make you more aware that He perceives you in ways beyond your comprehension?
