The Dispute over Multiple Perceptions and Its Resolution
Objection and answer about multiple perceptions in God

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection against multiple perceptions in God is answered by distinguishing the object and modality of perception.

Core Idea
Multiple perceptions in God are possible when their objects or modalities differ.

The discussion begins with a known dispute between two great scholars: al-Baqillani and al-Juwayni over whether God has a distinct attribute of perception for each type of sensory quality. An objection is raised: since God cannot have two attributes of the same genus, how can there be multiple perceptions? The author presents a nuanced answer that safeguards divine simplicity while allowing for multiplicity in divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute and the scholars involved
Read the first few lines to see who disagreed and what the point of contention was.
Al-Baqillani held that the object of perception is multiple, implying several perceptual attributes.
2
Understand the objection against multiplicity
The objection is that God cannot have two attributes of the same genus. Why would that rule out multiple perceptions?
The rule was established earlier that attributes of the same genus are not repeated in God.
3
Examine the answer and its reasoning
The answer distinguishes cases where the object, type of relation, or modality of relation differ. Then it gives examples: knowledge vs. hearing, hearing vs. sight, touch vs. smell vs. taste.
The key point: multiplicity is only prohibited when all aspects are identical.
4
Reflect on the final caveat
The author reminds that such speech is an approximation; God is transcendent beyond our analogies.
This prevents anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخلاف في زيادة صفة الإدراك. والمذوقات من غير اتصال بمحالها، ولا مماسة، ولا تكيف بكيفياتها؟ اختلف القاضي أبو بكر الباقلاني وإمام الحرمين ومن وافقهما في إثباتها وعدمه؛ فذهب القاضي بتعدد متعلقها، وقد بينا في وحدانية الصفات أن الله ليس له صفتان من جنس واحد؟ فالجواب: أن الممنوع هو تعدد الصفة مع اتحاد المتعلق ونوع التعلق وكيفية التعلق، ألا ترى أنه لما اختلف المتعلق كما في العلم والسمع لم يضر، ولما اختلفت كيفية التعلق كما في السمع والبصر لم يضر؛ فههنا من هذا القبيل، ولا شك أن كيفية اللمس غير كيفية الشم، وكلاهما غير كيفية الذوق، وثمرة كل منهما غير ثمرة الآخر، هذا كله أن الله تعالى منزه عن سمات الحوادث وأن هذا الكلام يقوله العلماء تقريبا للأذهان، والله المثل الأعلى لا تحيط العقول به ولا ندركه. قسم الإلهيات. إلى إثباتها؛ لأن الإدراكات المتعلقة بهذه الأشياء زائدة على العلم بها، للتفرقة الضرورية بينهما، وأيضا هي كمالات، وكل حي قابل لها، فإذا لم يتصف بها اتصف بأضدادها، وهي نقص لأن معها فوت كمال، والنقص في حقه تعالى محال، فوجب أن يتصف سبحانه بتلك الإدراكات زائدة على علمه تعالى على ما يليق به من نفي الاتصال بالأجسام ونفي اللذات عنه تعالى والآلام. وسمع أو لا، أي: أو ليس له تعالى صفة زائدة تسمى الإدراك كما ذهب إليه من شيوخه: والده الإمام أبو محمد الجويني، والقاضي حسين، والقاضي أبو الطيب الطبري، الحديث من أبي بكر أحمد بن محمد بن الحارث الأصبهاني، ومنصور بن رامش، وغيرهم كثير.
The dispute concerning the addition of the attribute of perception. And the things tasted without contact with their loci, nor touching, nor being qualified by their modalities? Al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani and Imam al-Haramayn and those who agreed with them differed concerning affirming it and denying it. Al-Qadi held that its object is multiple. And we have already explained in the unity of attributes that God does not have two attributes of the same genus. So the answer: That which is prohibited is multiplicity of the attribute with unity of the object and type of relation and modality of relation. Do you not see that when the object differed, as in knowledge and hearing, it did not harm, and when the modality of relation differed, as in hearing and sight, it did not harm? So here it is of this kind. And there is no doubt that the modality of touch is different from the modality of smell, and both are different from the modality of taste, and the fruit of each is different from the fruit of the other. All this is because God Almighty is transcendent above the attributes of contingents, and that this speech is said by scholars as an approximation for minds. And God has the highest similitude; intellects cannot encompass Him nor do they perceive Him. The section on divine matters. To affirming it; because the perceptions related to these things are additional to knowledge of them, due to the necessary distinction between them, and also because they are perfections, and every living being is receptive to them. So if He were not described by them, He would be described by their opposites, which are deficiencies, because with them there is a lack of perfection. And deficiency with respect to Him, exalted, is impossible. So it is necessary that He, glorified, be described by those perceptions as additional to His knowledge, in a manner befitting Him, by negating contact with bodies and negating pleasures and pains from Him, exalted. And hearing or not, i.e., whether He, exalted, does not have an additional attribute called perception, as held by some of his teachers: his father Imam Abu Muhammad al-Juwayni, and al-Qadi Husayn, and al-Qadi Abu al-Tayyib al-Tabari, the hadith scholar Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn al-Harith al-Isbahani, and Mansur ibn Ramish, and many others.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about the attribute of perception (idrak). It begins by stating the dispute: al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani and Imam al-Haramayn disagreed on whether God has an additional attribute of perception for touch, taste, and smell. Al-Qadi held that the object of this attribute is multiple (i.e., several perceptions). An objection is raised: since God cannot have two attributes of the same genus, how can there be multiple perceptions? The answer is that the prohibition applies only when the object, type of relation, and modality of relation are identical. Here, perceptions differ in object (e.g., touched vs. tasted) and modality (how they relate), similar to how knowledge differs from hearing, or hearing from sight. Therefore, multiple perceptions are possible. The author then asserts that God is transcendent beyond such analogies, and scholars use them only as approximations. Next, the passage provides a positive proof: these perceptions are additional to knowledge because we necessarily distinguish between knowing something and perceiving it. Moreover, they are perfections; if God lacked them, He would have their opposites, which are deficiencies, impossible for God. Thus, He must have these perceptions as real attributes, but without contact or sensory pleasures, befitting His transcendence. Finally, the passage mentions the opposing view of those who deny the attribute, listing scholars who held this opinion, including his father and others.

Key Terms
الإدراك (idrak/perception)
المتعلق (object of attribute)
الكيفية (modality)
التفرقة الضرورية (necessary distinction)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God had multiple perceptions, He would have multiple attributes of the same genus, which is impossible.
Correction: The passage clarifies that multiplicity is only prohibited when object and modality are identical; since perceptions differ in object or modality, they are not of the same genus.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in the passage, when is multiplicity of an attribute prohibited?
A) When the attribute is related to perception.
B) When the object, type of relation, and modality are all identical.
C) When the attribute is a perfection.
D) When the attribute is denied by al-Ash'ari.

Key Takeaway
Multiple perceptions in God are possible because they differ in object or modality, and God's transcendence is preserved by negating any literal similarity to creatures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says that scholars speak 'as an approximation for minds' about God's attributes. Where in your own understanding of God do you hold onto precise mental pictures that might be mere approximations? Are you willing to let go of that certainty for the mystery of God's transcendence? What concrete step can you take this week to cultivate awe over comprehension in your worship?
?
The condition for multiple attributes is that they differ in object or modality. Have you ever assumed a contradiction in God's attributes (e.g., mercy and justice) without examining whether they differ in object? How can you apply this careful distinction to purify your concept of God? Commit to studying one apparent contradiction in divine attributes with this principle in mind.
?
The passage ends by affirming that 'intellects cannot encompass Him.' Do you truly live as if God is beyond your full grasp, or do you secretly believe your theology has Him figured out? What would change in your prayer and humility if you deeply felt that all your knowledge of God is an approximation? Write down one phrase of praise that acknowledges His incomprehensibility and use it in your next salah.

The Proof for Perception as an Additional Attribute
Positive proof for perception as a distinct divine attribute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that God's perceptions are real attributes additional to His knowledge, grounded in necessary distinction and perfection.

Core Idea
Perceptions are perfections; their absence would be a deficiency, impossible for God.

After resolving the objection about multiplicity, the author presents a positive proof that God has an additional attribute of perception. The proof relies on two premises: the necessary distinction between knowing something and perceiving it, and the principle that perceptions are perfections which a perfect being must possess. The passage also lists scholars who held the opposing view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proof's structure
The proof has two parts: first, a distinction between knowledge and perception; second, an argument from perfection and deficiency.
The phrase 'قسم الإلهيات. إلى إثباتها' marks the start of the proof.
2
Understand the necessary distinction
The author claims there is a necessary distinction between knowing something and perceiving it. This shows that perceptions are additional to knowledge.
For example, you can know the taste of honey without actually tasting it.
3
Grasp the perfection argument
Perceptions are perfections; any living being capable of them would be deficient without them. Since God is perfect and cannot have deficiency, He must have these perceptions.
The opposites of perceptions are deficiencies like inability to taste, etc.
4
Note the qualification for God
The passage emphasizes that God's perceptions are without contact or sensory pleasures, befitting His transcendence.
This avoids anthropomorphism.
5
Review the opposing scholars
The passage ends by listing scholars who denied that perception is an additional attribute, including the author's father.
This shows the issue was debated.

It begins by stating the dispute: al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani and Imam al-Haramayn disagreed on whether God has an additional attribute of perception for touch, taste, and smell. Al-Qadi held that the object of this attribute is multiple (i.e., several perceptions). An objection is raised: since God cannot have two attributes of the same genus, how can there be multiple perceptions? The answer is that the prohibition applies only when the object, type of relation, and modality of relation are identical. Here, perceptions differ in object (e.g., touched vs. tasted) and modality (how they relate), similar to how knowledge differs from hearing, or hearing from sight. Therefore, multiple perceptions are possible. The author then asserts that God is transcendent beyond such analogies, and scholars use them only as approximations. Next, the passage provides a positive proof: these perceptions are additional to knowledge because we necessarily distinguish between knowing something and perceiving it. Moreover, they are perfections; if God lacked them, He would have their opposites, which are deficiencies, impossible for God. Thus, He must have these perceptions as real attributes, but without contact or sensory pleasures, befitting His transcendence. Finally, the passage mentions the opposing view of those who deny the attribute, listing scholars who held this opinion, including his father and others.

Key Terms
الإدراك (idrak/perception)
التفرقة الضرورية (necessary distinction)
الكمال (perfection)
النقص (deficiency)
الصفات (attributes)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that God's perception is the same as His knowledge, i.e., no additional attribute.
Correction: The passage argues that perception is distinct from knowledge because we know things without perceiving them, and perceptions are perfections not reducible to knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason given in the passage for affirming that perceptions are additional to knowledge?
A) Because the Quran mentions perception separately.
B) Because there is a necessary distinction between knowing and perceiving.
C) Because every living being has perceptions.
D) Because scholars debated the issue.

Key Takeaway
God's perceptions are real attributes additional to His knowledge, affirmed as perfections in a manner free from anthropomorphism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God truly perceives your every action, thought, and feeling as a real attribute, does that shape how you live in private? When you know He sees you, does it change your secret choices?
?
You have been given the gift of perception—sight, hearing, touch. How do you use these gifts? Are you grateful for them, or do you take them for granted? Remember that these perceptions are perfections; have you used them to draw closer to the One who possesses them perfectly?
?
The author lists scholars who disagreed. Would you be willing to stand for a truth about God even if your teachers or mentors disagreed? Examine your heart: is your faith built on the evidence or on the authority of others?

Proof that Perception is an Extra Attribute Beyond Knowledge
Commentator proves perception is an extra attribute using two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that Allah's perceptions (sight, hearing, etc.) are additional attributes beyond His knowledge, based on necessary distinction and the argument from perfection.

Core Idea
Perceptions are perfections; their absence would imply deficiency, which is impossible for Allah.

Sanusi's commentator now turns to a specific divine attribute: perception (idrak). While Allah's knowledge is all-encompassing, the senses like sight and hearing seem distinct. The commentator argues that perceptions are not merely included in knowledge but are separate attributes. This lesson unpacks the rational proof for this position, drawing on the necessary distinction between knowing and perceiving, and the principle that perfection requires all perfections.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Start by reading the Arabic passage once. Notice the structure: it begins by stating the claim, then gives two reasons.
2
Understand the first proof: necessary distinction
The commentator says there is a tafriqa daruriyya (necessary distinction) between knowledge and perception. How do you distinguish between knowing something and perceiving it? Reflect on this.
3
Understand the second proof: perfection argument
Perceptions are perfections (kamalat). Every living being capable of them that lacks them would have their opposites, which are deficiencies. Since deficiency is impossible for Allah, He must possess them.
4
Apply the principle of befittingness
The passage concludes by noting that these perceptions are affirmed in a manner befitting Allah, denying any physical connection or pleasure/pain. This safeguards divine transcendence.
5
Synthesize
Summarize how the commentator proves that perceptions are extra attributes. Why is this important for understanding Allah's attributes?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم الإلهيات • إلى إثباتها; لأنَّ الإدراكات المتعلقة بهذه الأشياء زائدة على العلم بها، للتفرقة الضرورية بينهما، وأيضا هي كمالات، وكل حي قابل لها، فإذا لم يتصف بها اتصف بأضدادها، وهي نقص لأن معها فوت كمال، والنقص في حقه تعالى محال، فوجب أن يتصف سبحانه بتلك الإدراكات زائدة على علمه تعالى على ما يليق به من نفي الاتصال بالأجسام ونفي اللذات عنه تعالى والآلام.
The division of divine matters: to its affirmation [as attributes], because the perceptions related to these things are additional to knowledge, due to the necessary distinction between them, and also because they are perfections, and every living thing is capable of them, and if it is not qualified with them, it becomes qualified with their opposites, which are deficiencies because they involve the loss of perfection, and deficiency is impossible with respect to Him, may He be exalted, so it is necessary that He, glorified be He, be qualified with those perceptions as additional to His knowledge, may He be exalted, in a manner befitting Him, including denial of connection (ittisal) with bodies and denial of pleasures (ladhdhat) and pains (alam) with respect to Him, may He be exalted.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalil) that the perceptions (idrakat) such as sight, hearing, smell, taste, touch are additional attributes (sifat za'ida) beyond knowledge (`ilm). The author begins by stating that the perceptions related to these things (i.e., the objects of perception) are additional to knowledge. The first reason is the necessary distinction (tafriqa daruriyya) between knowledge and perception in the witnessed world (shahid); since we distinguish between knowing something and perceiving it, the same applies to the unseen (gha'ib). The second reason is that perceptions are perfections (kamalat). Every living being capable of them that lacks them would have their opposites, which are deficiencies because they entail the loss of perfection. Since deficiency is impossible for God, He must be qualified with those perceptions as extra attributes beyond His knowledge, while denying any connection to bodies (ittisal) and any associated pleasures or pains, as these are not befitting Him.

Key Terms
إدراكات (perceptions)
علم (knowledge)
كمالات (perfections)
نقص (deficiency)
اتصال (connection)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Perception is just a type of knowledge; no separate attribute is needed.
Correction: The passage argues that knowledge and perception are necessarily distinct in our experience, and perceptions are perfections, so they are separate attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why must God have perceptions as additional attributes beyond knowledge?
A) Because perceptions are pleasurable.
B) Because they are perfections and their absence would imply deficiency.
C) Because God's knowledge is limited without them.
D) Because the Qur'an explicitly mentions them.

Key Takeaway
Allah's perceptions are distinct attributes from His knowledge, proven by necessary distinction and the perfection argument, while safeguarding transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that Allah's perception of you is a perfection, why do you often act as if He does not see you? Is there a specific area of your life where you need to increase your awareness of His hearing and sight? What will you change today?
?
You affirm that deficiency is impossible for Allah, yet you tolerate deficiencies in your own character. What one deficiency in yourself are you most attached to, and how can you begin to replace it with the perfection that Allah has commanded?
?
Are you content with merely knowing about Allah's attributes, or do you actively seek to perceive His signs in creation and His revelation? How can you move from theoretical knowledge to a living perception of His majesty today?

The Denial of Extra Perception and the Rational Entailment Argument
The passage lists the scholars who deny extra perception and presents the rational entailment argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that some scholars deny an extra attribute of perception for Allah because it would entail physical connection, which is impossible.

Core Idea
Perception would require connection, so it cannot be an attribute of Allah.

This lesson presents the view that perception is not an extra attribute of Allah, held by several scholars including Imam al-Juwayni's father. The key argument is that perception would entail a connection (ittisal) with its objects, which is impossible for Allah. Since the consequent is impossible, the antecedent must also be impossible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scholars
Read the passage and note the scholars who denied an extra attribute of perception. They include Imam Abu Muhammad al-Juwayni, Qadi Husayn, and others.
This list shows that the view was held by notable authorities.
2
Understand the rational entailment
The passage asserts a rational entailment (talazum `aqli) between perception and connection. If perception is an extra attribute, it must involve connection with its objects.
Connection (ittisal) implies physical contact, which is impossible for Allah.
3
Apply the impossibility of the consequent
Since connection is impossible for Allah, and it is a necessary consequence of the extra attribute, the attribute itself must be impossible. This is a logical argument: the impossibility of the consequent entails the impossibility of the antecedent.
This is a standard form of argument in kalam: if A implies B, and B is impossible, then A is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسمع أو لا) أي : أو ليس له تعالى صفة زائدة تسمى الإدراك كما ذهب إليه من شيوخه: والده الإمام أبو محمد الجويني، والقاضي حسين، والقاضي أبو الطيب الطبري، الحديث من أبي بكر أحمد بن محمد بن الحارث الأصبهاني، ومنصور بن رامش، وغيرهم كثير، الخلاف في زيادة صفة الإدراك، جمع; لما أن بينها وبين الاتصال بمتعلقاتها تلازما عقليا; فلا يتصور انفكاكها عنه، والاتصال مستحيل عليه تعالى، واستحالة اللازم توجب استحالة الملزوم، ولأن إحاطة العلم بمتعلقاتها كافية عن إثباتها حيث لم يرد بها سمع ولا دل عليها فعله تعالى، ودعوى أنه تعالى لو لم يتصف بها اتصف بأضدادها فاسدة; لمنافاة العلم لتلك الأضداد، وقد وجب اتصافه تعالى به.
And [some say]: 'No, he does not have an extra attribute called perception (idrak), as held by his teachers: his father Imam Abu Muhammad al-Juwayni, Qadi Husayn, Qadi Abu al-Tayyib al-Tabari, the traditionist Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn al-Harith al-Asbahani, Mansur ibn Rash [Ramish?], and many others.' The disagreement over the extra attribute of perception: gathered? [possibly meaning 'the response is'] because there is a rational entailment (talazum `aqli) between it [i.e., the supposed attribute] and connection (ittisal) with its objects; thus its disconnection from connection cannot be conceived. And connection is impossible for Him, may He be exalted, and the impossibility of the consequent necessitates the impossibility of the antecedent. And because the encompassment of knowledge (`ilm) of its objects suffices for its affirmation, since no revealed text (sam`) has come regarding it, nor do His actions indicate it. And the claim that if He were not qualified with it He would be qualified with its opposites is invalid, because knowledge (al-`ilm) contradicts those opposites, and He is necessarily qualified with knowledge.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage presents the disagreement over whether perception (idrak) is an extra attribute beyond knowledge. First, it lists those who deny it: Imam al-Juwayni's father, Qadi Husayn, Qadi Abu al-Tayyib al-Tabari, and others. Then it gives the refutation, which is the argument against the extra attribute. The refutation offers several arguments. First, there is a rational entailment between the supposed attribute of perception and connection (ittisal) with physical objects; since connection is impossible for God, the attribute leading to it must also be impossible. Second, God's knowledge is sufficient to encompass the objects of perception, so there is no need for an extra attribute, especially as no revealed text (sam`) supports it and God's actions do not indicate it. Third, the claim that the lack of perception would lead to its opposites (like blindness or deafness) is invalid because God's knowledge contradicts those opposites; His knowledge already covers all, so He cannot be described with such deficiencies. Thus, the conclusion is that God is qualified with knowledge alone, and perception is not an extra attribute.

Key Terms
إدراك (perception)
زيادة (extra)
اتصال (connection)
تلازم عقلي (rational entailment)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the rational entailment mentioned in the passage?
A) Between perception and knowledge
B) Between perception and connection
C) Between connection and revelation
D) Between knowledge and revelation

Key Takeaway
Perception as an extra attribute is denied because it would require physical connection, which is impossible for Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson uses rational entailment to deny an attribute that would imply physicality in Allah. Are there any beliefs I hold about Allah that inadvertently involve physical or human-like qualities? How can I actively purify my understanding to align with tawhid?
?
The scholars here were willing to deny a commonly-held notion based on rational proof. Am I ready to give up any comfortable but incorrect ideas about Allah if sound reasoning shows they are impossible? What example in my own thinking needs this correction today?
?
If I truly believe that Allah is free from all physical attributes, then how does this affect my worship and my relationship with Him? Does my heart fully accept that He is beyond any comparison, or do I still imagine Him in limited ways?

Sufficiency of Knowledge and Refutation of the Opposites Claim
The passage gives two additional refutation arguments: sufficiency of knowledge and invalidity of the opposites claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments that knowledge is sufficient for perception and that the claim of opposites is invalid.

Core Idea
Allah's knowledge covers all, negating any need for a separate perception attribute.

In this passage, the author continues the refutation by presenting two additional arguments: first, that Allah's knowledge (ilm) is already sufficient to encompass all objects of perception, so no extra attribute is needed, especially without textual or empirical evidence. Second, the claim that lacking perception would imply opposites like blindness is invalid because knowledge contradicts such opposites.

Guided Path
1
Understand the argument that knowledge suffices
Allah's knowledge encompasses all objects of perception. Therefore, there is no need for a separate attribute of perception; knowledge already does the job.
This is a principle of economy: no need to posit extra attributes when one suffices.
2
Note the lack of evidence
No revealed text (sam') or action of Allah indicates an extra perception attribute. This absence of evidence further supports the denial.
In kalam, attributes are established either by reason or revelation. Here both are absent.
3
Refute the opposites claim
The objection that lacking perception would lead to its opposites (e.g., blindness) is invalid because Allah's knowledge contradicts those opposites. Since Allah has perfect knowledge, He cannot have deficiencies.
Knowledge is incompatible with ignorance or sensory deficiencies.

Source Text

?
